Al-Razi's Integration of Philosophy into Kalam
Atay summarizes al-Razi's two-fold contribution to kalam and philosophy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Razi merged Aristotelian philosophy with kalam and the lasting impact of his method on Islamic theology.

Core Idea
Al-Razi transformed kalam by making it philosophical and philosophy kalamic.

Al-Razi is a pivotal figure in Islamic intellectual history. This lesson summarizes Dr. Atay's assessment of his contributions, highlighting how al-Razi absorbed Aristotelian philosophy and wove it into the fabric of kalam, thereby reshaping both disciplines. We will explore the two key points of this transformation and see evidence of his influence on later scholars.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Pay attention to the two main points Atay presents.
Notice the structure: an introduction, two numbered points, and a concluding example.
2
Identify the two contributions
Find the two summary points. The first is about absorbing philosophy and introducing it into kalam. The second is about giving kalam a new direction.
3
Examine the evidence of influence
Look at how al-Baydawi's Tawali' al-Anwar borrowed from al-Razi's al-Muhassal without attribution, and how al-Isfahani explicitly credits al-Razi.
This shows the direct impact of al-Razi's method.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider how merging philosophy and kalam changed both fields and why al-Razi is considered a pioneer.
This ties back to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يقول (٢) الأستاذ الدكتور حسين آقاي عميد كلية الإلهيات بجامعة أنقره ومن خلال اطلاعنا على ما كتب عن هذا العالم الكبير - فخر الدين الرازي - وعلى ضوء ما توصلنا إليه من نتائج، نستطيع أن نقول: إن «الرازي قد لعب دورا كبيرا في علم الكلام، والفلسفة الإسلامية. ويمكن إيجاز ذلك في نقطتين : الأولى : إنه استوعب فلسفة أرسطو» التقليدية، ثم كان أول من أدخل هذه الفلسفة في علم الكلام. ونتيجة لما قام به الرازي أصبح علم الكلام : فلسفة، ويمكن أن نقول بعبارة أخرى: إنه جعل تلك الفلسفة: كلاما. وهكذا امتزج علم الكلام بالفلسفة .... والثانية : وإذا كان الرازي) قد أدخل الفلسفة في علم الكلام. فإنه على ضوء فلسفة «أرسطو» التقليدية، قد اعطى اتجاها جديدا لعلم الكلام . فأثر بذلك على الفكر الإسلامي. وقد ظهر تأثير الرازي) جليا في غيره من خلال بقاء العديد من العلماء والمفكرين؛ أسرى اتجاهه، ومنهجه بعد ذلك . ويبدو واضحا لمن يقارن المحصل مع متن (طوالع الأنوار» للقاضي عبد الله بن عمر البيضاوي المتوفى سنة ٦٨٥هـ : أن أول من اقتفى أثر المحصل» هو القاضي البيضاوي في متنه المذكور. وهو لم يكتف بالسير على نهج «المحصل» فحسب، بل اقتبس منه العبارات والتعاريف والتعابير والاصطلاحات. ومع ذلك لم يشر - لا من قريب ولا من بعيد ـ للرازي . ولكن الشارح شمس الدين بن محمود بن عبدالرحمن الأصفهاني المتوفى سنة ٧٤٩ هـ قد ذكر لفظ «الإمام» مشيرا إلى الرازي وبذلك مهد الإمام الرازي طريقا ومنهجا جديدا في علم الكلام لمن أتى من بعده، مثل «عضدالدین، عبدالرحمن بن أحمد الإيجي، القاضي، المتوفى سنة ٧٥٢هـ . . .
Professor Dr. Husain Atay, Dean of the Faculty of Divinity at Ankara University, says: (2) And through our examination of what has been written about this great scholar — Fakhr al-Din al-Razi — and in light of the results we have reached, we can say: 'Al-Razi played a major role in kalam and Islamic philosophy. This can be summarized in two points: First: He absorbed the traditional philosophy of Aristotle, then he was the first to introduce this philosophy into kalam. As a result of what al-Razi did, kalam became philosophy; and we can say in other words: He made that philosophy into kalam. Thus kalam and philosophy merged.... Second: Since al-Razi introduced philosophy into kalam, he gave a new direction to kalam in light of the traditional philosophy of Aristotle. Thus he influenced Islamic thought. And al-Razi's influence clearly appeared in others through many scholars and thinkers remaining captives to his direction and method after that. And it becomes clear to whoever compares al-Muhassal with the text of Tawali' al-Anwar of Qadi Abdullah ibn Umar al-Baydawi (d. 685 AH): that the first to follow the path of al-Muhassal was Qadi al-Baydawi in his aforementioned text. And he not only followed the approach of al-Muhassal, but he borrowed from it phrases, definitions, expressions, and terminologies. Yet he did not refer — neither near nor far — to al-Razi. But the commentator Shams al-Din ibn Mahmud ibn Abd al-Rahman al-Isfahani (d. 749 AH) mentioned the word 'al-Imam' referring to al-Razi. And thus Imam al-Razi paved a new path and method in kalam for those who came after him, such as 'Adud al-Din Abd al-Rahman ibn Ahmad al-Iji, the Qadi (d. 752 AH) ...'
Source pages p. 9

Commentary
This passage is an assessment by Professor Husain Atay of Fakhr al-Din al-Razi's contributions to kalam and Islamic philosophy. Atay summarizes al-Razi's role in two points. First, al-Razi fully absorbed Aristotelian philosophy and was the first to integrate it into kalam, causing kalam and philosophy to merge into one another. Second, by introducing philosophy, al-Razi gave kalam a new direction, deeply influencing Islamic thought and leaving many scholars beholden to his method. The passage then provides evidence of al-Razi's influence: al-Baydawi's text Tawali' al-Anwar closely follows al-Razi's al-Muhassal, borrowing its language and definitions without acknowledging al-Razi. However, the commentator al-Isfahani explicitly refers to al-Razi as 'al-Imam'. Thus al-Razi pioneered a new path in kalam that was followed by later theologians like al-Iji.

Key Terms
فخر الدين الرازي
فلسفة أرسطو
المحصل
طوالع الأنوار
البيضاوي
الأصفهاني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Razi simply copied Aristotelian philosophy into kalam without modification.
Correction: The passage shows he absorbed and transformed it, making kalam into philosophy and philosophy into kalam, indicating a creative synthesis.

Checkpoint
According to Dr. Atay, what was the first point summarizing al-Razi's role?
A) He rejected philosophy entirely.
B) He absorbed Aristotelian philosophy and first introduced it into kalam, merging the two.
C) He wrote commentaries on Aristotle's works.
D) He established a new school of kalam independent of philosophy.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi pioneered the fusion of Aristotelian philosophy with kalam, creating a new synthetic discipline that influenced generations of theologians.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Razi was not content to remain within the boundaries of one discipline; he actively pursued integration. In your own journey of learning about your faith, are you courageously engaging with challenging ideas from outside your comfort zone, or are you sheltering in what you already know? What specific step will you take this week to broaden your understanding?
?
The passage highlights how al-Baydawi used al-Razi's work without acknowledgment. Think about your own intellectual life: when you share ideas or insights, are you honest about where they came from? Is there any area where you have taken credit for something you learned from a teacher or scholar without proper recognition?
?
Al-Razi's method became so influential that later scholars were 'captives' to his approach. Reflect on your own beliefs: Are you following a certain method of thinking about Allah and His attributes simply because it is common, or have you critically examined it? Are you a captive to a particular school of thought, or have you personally verified its foundations?

Al-Azhar's Esteem for al-Matalib al-Aliyah
Opening statement on al-Matalib al-Aliyah's status.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Azhar University considers al-Matalib al-Aliyah a foundational 'mother book' in Islamic theology.

Core Idea
Al-Matalib al-Aliyah is honored as a 'mother book' for its depth in defending creed.

This passage opens by establishing the high regard al-Azhar University holds for al-Matalib al-Aliyah, calling it one of the 'mother books.' This sets the stage for appreciating why studying this text is important in the tradition of Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening statement
Read the first line which states al-Azhar's view of al-Matalib al-Aliyah. Notice the phrase 'ummahat al-kutub' (mother books).
This is a strong endorsement from a major institution.
2
Understand 'mother books'
Reflect on what it means for a book to be called a 'mother book' in Islamic scholarship. It indicates a comprehensive, authoritative source that others rely on.
3
Consider the implication
Ask yourself: why would a book earn such status? Hint: the next part of the passage discusses its role in defending the creed.
This connects to the broader importance of kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
- وجامعة الأزهر تعد كتاب المطالب العالية من أمهات الكتب. يقول الاستاذ الشيخ صالح موسى شرف عضو هيئة كبار العلماء وعضو مجمع البحوث الإسلامية ولما كان علم التوحيد - ويسمى علم الكلام - له هذه المنزلة العظيمة في تأصيل هذه العقيدة والدفاع عنها عني المتكلمون قديما وحديثا بالتأليف فيه، وتباروا في ذلك فمنهم المقتصد ومنهم المتعمق ومنهم المتوسط. وكان من أشهر الكتب المتعمقة والمشتملة على الأمور العامة التي يحتاج إليها المستدل على حدوث العالم، ومنه على وجود الصانع : كتاب «المطالب العالية لفخر الدين الرازي. و«العقائد النسفية للنفي. وكتاب «المواقف للإيجي -
And al-Azhar University considers the book al-Matalib al-Aliyah to be one of the mother books (ummahat al-kutub). Professor Shaykh Salih Musa Sharaf, member of the Board of Senior Scholars and member of the Islamic Research Academy, says: Since the science of tawhid — called the science of kalam — holds this great status in establishing and defending this creed, the mutakallimun, past and present, have devoted themselves to authoring in it, and they vied in that; among them are the moderate, the deep, and the intermediate. Among the most famous of the deep books that include the general matters needed by the one who reasons to prove the temporal origination of the world, and thereby the existence of the Creator, are: al-Matalib al-Aliyah by Fakhr al-Din al-Razi, al-Aqa'id al-Nasafiyya by al-Nasafi, and al-Mawaqif by al-Iji.
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage highlights the significance of the book al-Matalib al-Aliyah and the science of kalam (theology). It states that al-Azhar University regards this book as one of the 'mother books' (ummahat al-kutub), i.e., a fundamental reference. The author quotes Shaykh Salih Musa Sharaf, who notes that because the science of tawhid (monotheism), also called kalam, is crucial for establishing and defending the Islamic creed, scholars have competed in writing works on it. He classifies these works into three categories: moderate (muqtasid), deep (muta'ammiq), and intermediate (mutawassit). Among the most famous 'deep' books that cover the general principles needed to argue for the world's temporal origination (huduth) and from there the existence of the Creator (al-Sani') are listed: al-Matalib al-Aliyah by al-Razi, al-Aqa'id al-Nasafiyya by al-Nasafi, and al-Mawaqif by al-Iji.

Key Terms
المطالب العالية
أمهات الكتب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how does al-Azhar describe al-Matalib al-Aliyah?
A) One of the mother books
B) A moderate book
C) An intermediate book
D) A book of history

Key Takeaway
Al-Matalib al-Aliyah is esteemed by al-Azhar as a fundamental reference in kalam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you approach the 'mother books' of your tradition with the same reverence as Al-Azhar does in this passage? If not, what concrete step will you take this week to begin studying one?

The Importance of Kalam and Its Deep Works
Importance of kalam and classification of works.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the role of kalam in defending tawhid and how scholars classify works into moderate, deep, and intermediate categories.

Core Idea
Kalam defends the creed through rational proofs; its deep works provide comprehensive arguments for God's existence.

The passage now expands on the importance of kalam, quoting Shaykh Salih Musa Sharaf. He explains that scholars have written works of varying depth to establish and defend the Islamic creed. Three famous 'deep' books are listed, including al-Matalib al-Aliyah, which serve as essential resources for proving the world's temporal origination and the existence of the Creator.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the purpose of kalam
Read the statement: kalam is crucial for 'ta'sil' (establishing) and 'difaa' (defending) the creed. Understand that it provides rational foundations.
Kalam is synonymous with tawhid in this context.
2
Identify the three categories
The passage mentions three types of kalam works: muqtasid (moderate), muta'ammiq (deep), and mutawassit (intermediate). Notice that al-Matalib al-Aliyah is in the 'deep' category.
3
List the three deep books
The passage names three books: al-Matalib al-Aliyah, al-Aqa'id al-Nasafiyya, and al-Mawaqif. Reflect on why they are considered 'deep'.
These books cover general principles needed for the argument from huduth to the existence of God.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Kalam is purely speculative and not tied to defending the creed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage classifies kalam works into moderate, deep, and intermediate. Which category do you personally tend to rely on when seeking to understand your faith? Does your current approach reflect a genuine hunger for rational proofs, or a comfortable middle ground?
?
The deep works are designed to prove the existence of the Creator from the temporal origination of the world. Have you ever taken the time to study such arguments deeply, or do you depend on inherited belief? What would it cost you to truly wrestle with these proofs until your conviction becomes your own?
?
If you had to choose one of the three deep books mentioned—al-Matalib al-Aliyah, al-Aqa'id al-Nasafiyya, or al-Mawaqif—and commit to studying it thoroughly over the next year, which would it be? What is holding you back from starting today?

Al-Razi Introduces His Book on Divine Science
Al-Razi defines the book's subject, Greek name, and structure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the subject of al-Razi's book, its Greek name, and its organizational structure.

Core Idea
Al-Razi's 'Divine Science' is the Islamic theology called kalam.

In this opening passage, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi sets the stage for his major work. He identifies the book's subject—Divine Science—and mentions its Greek equivalent, revealing the interdisciplinary nature of his approach. He also outlines the book's structure, which will consist of an introduction and several books.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own words introduce you to the work.
2
Identify the three elements
Al-Razi presents three key pieces of information: the subject of the book, its name in Greek, and its structural arrangement. Find each one.
The subject is 'al-ʿilm al-ilāhī'.
3
Reflect on the Greek term
Ask yourself why al-Razi mentions the Greek name 'Pathology'. What does this tell us about his scholarly background or his intended audience?
This connects to the misconception that it refers to medical pathology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بسم الله الرحمن الرحيم (قال مولانا الإمام الداعي إلى الله . أبو عبدالله محمد بن عمر بن الحسين الرازي - رضي الله عنه (۱)) : هذا كتابنا في العلم الإلهي، وهو المسمى في لسان اليونانيين : باثولوجيا . وهو مرتب على مقدمة وكتب (٢) .
In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. Our master, the Imam, the caller to Allah, Abu Abdullah Muhammad ibn Umar ibn al-Husayn al-Razi – may Allah be pleased with him – said: This is our book on Divine Science (al-ʿilm al-ilāhī), which is called in the language of the Greeks: Pathology (bāthūlūjyā). And it is arranged into an introduction (muqaddima) and books (kutub).
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage is an introductory statement by Fakhr al-Din al-Razi. It defines the book's subject as 'Divine Science' (al-ʿilm al-ilāhī), which is the study of matters pertaining to God, a core topic in Islamic theology (kalam). He notes that in Greek this discipline is called 'Pathology' – here likely meaning 'theology' (from Greek 'theologia'). Then he states the book's structure: it consists of a preface (muqaddima) and several books (kutub), indicating a multi-part organization. This sets the reader's expectations for the work's scope and arrangement.

Key Terms
العلم الإلهي
باثولوجيا
مقدمة
كتب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think 'Pathology' here refers to the medical study of diseases.
Correction: Al-Razi uses 'Pathology' as a transliteration of the Greek term for theology, not medical pathology.

Checkpoint
What does al-Razi say his book is about?
A) Logic
B) Divine Science
C) Medicine
D) Greek Philosophy

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's work is a book on Divine Science (kalam), known in Greek as Pathology, arranged as an introduction and several books.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What does it mean for you that a great scholar like al-Razi dedicated his life to writing on 'Divine Science'? How much time do you spend learning about your Creator compared to other pursuits?
?
Al-Razi begins this book by calling on Allah and seeking to call others to Him. In your daily reading and studying, do you begin with the intention of drawing closer to Allah and inviting others? If not, what would need to change?
?
The Greek term 'Pathology' reminds us that the study of God is a discipline with its own terminology and history. Are you treating your study of aqeedah as a serious science, or as something casual? What one step can you take this week to deepen your engagement with Islamic theology?

Preface: The Four Foundational Questions of Kalam
The author enumerates four chapters that outline the foundational questions of the science of kalam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four key methodological and epistemological questions that a classical scholar of kalam addresses at the outset of his work.

Core Idea
Every science begins by asking about its worth, the capacity of reason, the paths to knowledge, and its key terms.

Before diving into arguments about God's attributes or the nature of creation, a kalam scholar first establishes the framework of his science. This preface lists four foundational chapters that map out the key questions: the nobility of kalam, the capacity of human reason, the multiplicity of paths to sacred knowledge, and the need to define key terms. This lesson unpacks each of these four questions.

Guided Path
1
Read the preface passage
Read the Arabic passage once and then the literal translation. Notice the structured enumeration of four chapters.
2
Understand the first chapter: nobility of kalam
The first chapter explains why the science of kalam is the noblest science absolutely. Ask yourself why a scholar would begin with a value statement.
This sets the importance of the subject.
3
Understand the second chapter: human reason and certainty
The second chapter asks whether human intellect can attain certainty in this science or must sometimes rely on the 'first and worthier'—a reference to probabilistic reasoning. This is a deep epistemological inquiry.
The phrase 'awla wa akhlaq' is about preferring the most fitting approach, not about morality.
4
Understand chapters three and four: paths and definitions
The third chapter asks if there is more than one path to sacred knowledge, and the fourth chapter is about 'controlling the knots'—defining key terms and principles. See how these complete the methodological framework.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القدمَة وَفِيهَا أربعة فصول
الفصل الأول ، فِي بَيَان أَنَّ هَذَا العِلْمِ أَشرف العلُومِ على الاطلاق .
الفصل الثاني : في أَنَّهُ هَل لِلعقول البشريَّةِ سَبيل إِلَى تحصيل الجزم واليقين في هذا العلم أو يكتفى في بعض مَبَاحِيهِ وَمَطالبه بالأخذ بالأولى والأخلق؟
الفصل الثالث : فأن تحصيل هذه المعارف المقدسة، هل الطريق إليه [والصدام؟] اكثر من واحد ؟
الفصل الرابع : في ضَبَطَ مَعَاقِد هَذا العلم.
The preface, and it contains four chapters. Chapter one: concerning the explanation that this science is the noblest of sciences absolutely. Chapter two: concerning whether human intellects have a way to attaining certainty and conviction in this science, or is it sufficient in some of its topics and demands to take what is first and worthier? Chapter three: that the attainment of these sacred knowledges, is the path to it and [the way?] more than one? Chapter four: concerning the control of the knots of this science.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage is a preface that enumerates the four chapters of a section of the book. The author first states that the preface contains four chapters. Then each chapter is listed with a brief description of its subject. Chapter one explains why this science (ilm al-kalam) is the noblest of all sciences without restriction. Chapter two asks whether human reason can attain certainty (jazzm wa yaqin) in this science, or whether in some topics one must rely on the 'first and worthier' (al-awla wa al-akhlaq)—a reference to speculative or probabilistic reasoning? Chapter three asks whether the path to acquiring sacred knowledges (al-ma'arif al-muqaddasa) is more than one—i.e., are there multiple valid methods? Chapter four is about 'controlling the knots' (dabt ma'aqid) of this science, meaning defining its key terms and principles. The passage serves as a roadmap for the reader, outlining the major methodological and epistemological questions that the work will address.

Key Terms
القدمَة
فصل
أشرف العلوم
الجزم
اليقين
المعارف المقدسة
ضَبْط مَعَاقِد العلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The phrase 'الأولى والأخلق' (al-awla wa al-akhlaq) refers to moral virtue.
Correction: Actually, 'al-awla wa al-akhlaq' here means 'the first and worthier'—i.e., preferring the most suitable or probable approach in the absence of certainty, not a moral concept.

Checkpoint
What is the subject of the second chapter in this preface?
A) The nobility of this science.
B) Whether human intellect can attain certainty in this science or must rely on the first and worthier.
C) The multiple paths to acquiring sacred knowledges.
D) Defining the key terms of this science.

Key Takeaway
The preface lays out four foundational questions that frame the entire science of kalam: its nobility, the role of reason, the multiplicity of paths, and the need for precise definitions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever consciously reflected on whether your mind can truly attain certainty in matters of faith, or do you avoid the question because it feels unsettling? This preface forces you to confront that very question. What concrete step will you take this week to seek firmer conviction in one central belief?
?
The author asks if there is more than one path to sacred knowledge. Which paths have you neglected—perhaps through study of the Qur'an, reflection on creation, or learning from a teacher? Today, identify one path you have not explored and commit to starting it.
?
Chapter four calls for precise definitions. Think of a key term in your understanding of God (e.g., 'knowledge', 'power', 'will'). Can you define it accurately in your own words? If not, your belief may be built on vague notions. Will you take time today to look up its precise meaning and write it down? This is a step toward controlling the knots of your own faith.

The Nobility of Kalam: Four Proofs of God's Supreme Nobility
Author proves God's supreme nobility via four rational arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the subject matter of kalam (God) is the most noble of existents based on four rational arguments presented by the author.

Core Idea
The nobility of a science stems from the nobility of its subject matter; thus kalam is noble because God is the most noble being.

In this passage, the author begins to establish the unparalleled honor of the science of kalam. He argues that one key measure of a science's nobility is the value of what it studies. Since kalam studies God—His essence and attributes—its subject matter is the most exalted. To demonstrate this, he presents four incisive rational proofs that highlight God's absolute independence and necessity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the author uses a very structured format: first a general principle, then four numbered arguments.
2
Identify the first two arguments
Find the first argument (independence from agent/recipient) and the second (absolute uniqueness). Understand how each points to God's supreme nobility.
The first argument focuses on causal independence, the second on non-compositeness.
3
Identify the next two arguments
Find the third argument (necessity vs contingency) and the fourth (continuous need). Notice how they build on each other to show God's unique status.
The third argument introduces the necessary/contingent distinction, and the fourth extends need to persistence.
4
Reflect on the overall point
Ask yourself: How do these four arguments collectively establish that God is the most noble existent? Then connect this to the nobility of kalam.
The author is not just proving God's nobility; he is using it to demonstrate the honor of the science that studies Him.

الفصلات الأولى 
بيان أن هذا العام أشرف العلوم علوا الي الطاقة 
علوان 
أعلم أن شرف العلم إنما يظهر من وجوه : 
الوجه الأول من الوجوه الموجبة للشرف : 
شرف الأمر المبحوث عنه في ذلك العلم. وذلك في هذا العلم، هو ذات الله تعالى وصفاته وهو أشرف الموجودات على الإطلاق. ويدل عليه وجوه : 
الأول : إنه غني عن الفاعل والقابل وغيره محتاج اليه . 
والثاني : إنه فرد على الاطلاق، فهو غني عن الجزء المقوّم . 
والثالث: إن الواجب لذاته ليس إلا هو، وكل ما سواه فهو ممكن لذاته، محتاج إلى المؤثر فيلزم أن كل ما سواه فهو محتاج إليه، وهو غني عن كل ما سواه، فوجب أن يكون هو أشرف الموجودات. 
والرابع: إنه ثبت أن الممكن كما أنه محتاج إلى المؤثر حال حدوثه ، فهو محتاج إليه أيضا حال بقائه ، وكل ما سواه فهو محتاج إليه في جميع أوقاته ،
The First Sections: Explanation that this science is the noblest of sciences in sublimity to the utmost. Know that the honor of a science only appears from aspects: The first aspect of the aspects that necessitate honor: The honor of the thing investigated in that science. And that, in this science, is the Essence of God Most High and His attributes, and it is the most noble of existents absolutely. And it is indicated by aspects: The first: That He is independent of the agent and the recipient, and other than Him is in need of Him. The second: That He is absolutely unique, so He is independent of the constituent part. The third: That the Necessary by Himself is none other than Him, and everything else is contingent by itself, in need of an effecter, so it follows that everything else is in need of Him, and He is independent of everything else, so it is necessary that He is the most noble of existents. The fourth: That it is established that the contingent, just as it is in need of the effecter at the time of its origination, so too it is in need of Him at the time of its persistence, and everything else is in need of Him at all times.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage introduces the first aspect of the nobility of the science of kalam (ilm al-kalam). The author states that the honor of any science is manifested through several aspects, and the first is the honor of its subject matter. For kalam, the subject matter is the Essence (dhat) of God and His attributes, which is the most noble of all existents. To prove this, the author provides four rational arguments (wujuh). The first argument: God is independent (ghani) of any agent or recipient, meaning He does not need a cause for His existence nor depends on a potential receiver, while everything else is inherently in need of Him. The second: God is absolutely unique (fard 'ala al-itlaq), so He is independent of any constituent part (juz' muqawwim), unlike composite beings. The third: The Necessary Being (al-wajib li-dhatihi) is none other than God; everything else is contingent (mumkin li-dhatihi) and requires an effecter (mu'aththir), so all else is in need of Him while He is independent, establishing His supreme nobility. The fourth: The contingent, from its origination (huduth) onward, continually depends on the effecter for its persistence (baqa'), so everything else is in need of God at all times. These proofs demonstrate that God is the most noble being, thus the science that studies Him is the most noble.

واجب لذاته
ممكن لذاته
محتاج
حدوث
بقاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The nobility of a science comes primarily from its practical benefit or worldly utility.
Correction: The author argues that the primary measure of a science's honor is the nobility of its subject matter. For kalam, that is God—the most noble of existents—making kalam inherently noble regardless of practical outcomes.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how does the author establish that God is the most noble of existents?
A) By stating that God is the Creator of all things.
B) By giving four arguments showing His independence from need and His uniqueness.
C) By citing consensus of the scholars.
D) By referencing the Quran.

Key Takeaway
The first criterion for a science's honor is the nobility of its subject matter; by this measure, kalam is supreme because God is absolutely independent, unique, necessary, and the sustainer of all.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly recognize that your very existence and every moment of your continued survival depends entirely on God, or do you sometimes feel self-sufficient? What would change in your daily life if you genuinely believed this?
?
The author argues that God's essence is the most noble subject of study. Have you approached learning about God's attributes with the reverence and awe that such nobility deserves, or have you treated it as mere intellectual exercise? How can you cultivate a sense of sacredness in your study?
?
Thinking of the fourth proof: you need God not only at the moment of your creation but at every instant. Is there an area of your life where you act as if you are self-sufficient, forgetting your constant need of the Sustainer? What one step can you take today to consciously depend on Him in that area?

The Subject's Nobility Determines the Science's Nobility
Principle statement followed by two proofs of God's nobility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the principle that the nobility of a science stems from the nobility of its subject matter, and see the first two proofs that God is the most noble being.

Core Idea
A science is noble because its subject is noble.

The author begins by laying down a foundational principle: the honor of any science comes from the honor of what it studies. Since this science studies God—the most noble being—it must be the noblest science. He then offers two initial proofs that God is indeed the most noble: His absolute independence and His unique indivisibility.

Guided Path
1
Understand the principle
Read the opening statement: the nobility of a science depends on the nobility of its subject matter. Why does this make sense?
2
Identify the subject of this science
The subject is God's Essence and attributes. The author claims this is the noblest subject. Notice the claim.
3
Examine the first proof
First proof: God is independent (ghani) of any agent or recipient, while everything else depends on Him. What does this imply about nobility?
Independence is a sign of perfection.
4
Examine the second proof
Second proof: God is absolutely unique (fard), so He needs no constituent parts. How does uniqueness relate to nobility?
Composition implies need; independence from parts shows perfection.
5
Reflect on the connection
Consider how both proofs point to God's self-sufficiency. Why would self-sufficiency make something the noblest?
Everything noble about creation is borrowed from God.

الفصلات الأولى
بيان أن هذا العلم أشرف العلوم

أعلم أن شرف العلم إنما يظهر من وجوه :
الوجه الأول من الوجوه الموجبة للشرف : شرف الأمر المبحوث عنه في ذلك العلم. وذلك في هذا العلم، هو ذات الله تعالى وصفاته وهو أشرف الموجودات على الإطلاق. ويدل عليه وجوه : الأول : إنه غني عن الفاعل والقابل وغيره محتاج اليه . والثاني : إنه فرد على الاطلاق، فهو غني عن الجزء المقوّم . الثالث : إن الواجب لذاته ليس إلا هو، وكل ما سواه فهو ممكن لذاته، محتاج إلى المؤثر فيلزم أن كل ما سواه فهو محتاج إليه، وهو غني عن كل ما سواه، فوجب أن يكون هو أشرف الموجودات. والرابع: إنه ثبت أن الممكن كما أنه محتاج إلى المؤثر حال حدوثه ، فهو محتاج إليه أيضا حال بقائه، وكل ما سواه فهو محتاج إليه في جميع أوقاته ، سواء كان ذلك حال الحدوث، أو حال البقاء وكما أنه محتاج إليه في جانب الوجود ففي جانب العدم أيضا كذلك لما ثبت أن الممكن ليس معدوما لذاته، بل علة العدم عدم العلة فثبت بما ذكرنا أن الحق سبحانه وتعالى أشرف من غيره بحسب هذه الاعتبارات . والخامس: هو أن الوجود أشرف من العدم، والواجب لذاته لا يقبل العدم البتة فهو موجود لذاته، وبوجوده يحصل الموجود لكل موجود، بل وجوده كالمنافي للعدم وأما كل ما سواه فإنه ممكن لذاته، والممكن لذاته إذا نظر إليه من حيث هو هو وجد غير موجود وكل ما سواه فإنه إذا اعتبر من حيث هو هو، لم يكن موجوداً. وهو سبحانه إذا اعتبر من حيث هو هو، فهو الموجود. فلهذا المعنى قلنا: إنه حق وما سواه باطل بل الحق أنه لا يليق لفظ الحق إلا له ولا يليق وصف الاعتقاد بأنه حق، إلا باعتقاد وجوده. وأن كل ما سواء فهو الفناء المحض، والهلاك المحض، كما قال في الكتاب ((الإلهي)) كل شيء هالك إلا وجهه) فثبت بهذه الاعتبارات أنه تعالى أشرف الموجودات وأكملها، بل إنه تعالى أشرف وأكمل من أن يقاس هو إلى غيره، فإنه أشرف وأكمل منه . فكلما كان المعلوم أشرف، كان العلم به أشرف ولما كان أشرف المعلومات .
First chapters: Statement that this knowledge is the noblest of sciences. Know that the nobility of knowledge appears from aspects: The first aspect of the aspects necessitating nobility: the nobility of the matter investigated in that science. And that, in this science, is the Essence of God Most High and His attributes, and it is the noblest of existents absolutely. And it is indicated by aspects: First: He is independent of agent and recipient, and other than Him is in need of Him. Second: He is absolutely unique, so He is independent of a constituent part. Third: The Necessary by essence is none other than Him, and everything other than Him is contingent by essence, in need of the efficient cause, so it follows that everything other than Him is in need of Him, while He is independent of everything other than Him, so it is necessary that He is the noblest of existents. Fourth: It is established that the contingent, just as it is in need of the cause at the time of its origination, it is also in need of it at the time of its continuation, and everything other than Him is in need of Him in all its times, whether that time is of origination or continuation; and just as it is in need of Him in the aspect of existence, so too in the aspect of non-existence, because it is established that the contingent is not non-existent by itself, but the cause of non-existence is the non-existence of the cause. So it is established by what we have mentioned that the Truth, Glory be to Him, is more noble than other than Him according to these considerations. Fifth: Existence is more noble than non-existence, and the Necessary by essence does not admit non-existence at all, so He exists by Himself, and by His existence existence is obtained for every existent; rather His existence is like a negation of non-existence. As for everything other than Him, it is contingent by essence, and the contingent by essence, when considered in itself, is found non-existent. And everything other than Him, if considered in itself, is not existent. And He, Glorified, if considered in Himself, is existent. For this meaning we say: He is the Truth and other than Him is false; rather the truth is that the word 'Truth' is not fitting for anyone but Him, and the description of belief as truth is not fitting except through belief in His existence. And that everything other than Him is pure annihilation and pure destruction, as He said in the divine Book: 'Everything is perishing except His Face' (28:88). So it is established by these considerations that He, the Exalted, is the noblest and most perfect of existents, rather He is too noble and perfect to be compared to others, for He is more noble and perfect than them. So whenever the known is more noble, the knowledge of it is more noble, and He is the most noble of known things.
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage is a classification followed by a series of proofs. The author first states that the nobility of a science depends on the nobility of its subject matter. For this science, the subject is God's Essence and attributes, which are the most noble of existents. He then provides five proofs that God is the most noble. 1) God is independent (غني) of any cause, while everything else depends on Him. 2) God is absolutely unique (فرد), so He is independent of any constituent part. 3) The Necessary by essence (الواجب لذاته) is only God; everything else is contingent (ممكن) and in need of a cause, so they all need Him while He needs nothing. 4) The contingent needs the cause both at origination and continuation, and in both existence and non-existence (since its non-existence is due to absence of cause). Thus all other than God are always in need. 5) Existence is nobler than non-existence; God is Existent by Himself and does not admit non-existence, while everything else is contingent and possibly non-existent. Hence God is the Truth (حق) and everything else is perishable. The author concludes that God is the noblest of known things, so knowledge of Him is the noblest science.

Key Terms
شرف
غني
فرد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think a science is noble only because of its practical benefits or worldly applications.
Correction: The author shows that nobility comes primarily from the subject matter itself—what is studied—not just its usefulness.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the first aspect that makes a science noble?
A) The number of scholars studying it
B) The nobility of its subject matter
C) Its practical benefits
D) The difficulty of its proofs

Key Takeaway
The nobility of a science is rooted in the nobility of its subject; God as subject makes this the most noble science.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's nobility lies in His absolute independence, what does your daily dependence on Him reveal about your own station? Will you consciously humble yourself today by acknowledging that need?
?
The author measures nobility by the subject studied. What is the most noble subject you have given your time to this week? If it is not God, what does that say about your priorities, and what will you change starting tomorrow?
?
You have just learned that God is noble because He needs nothing and no one. But you – you need everything and everyone. Does that thought make you shrink, or does it draw you to the One who is self-sufficient? How will you let this truth transform your worship today?

Necessity vs Contingency: The Third Proof
The third proof: God alone is necessary, all else contingent, hence God is the most noble.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third proof: God alone is necessary by essence, and everything else is contingent, so He is the most noble.

Core Idea
Only God is self-sufficient; all else depends on Him.

The author now presents the third and perhaps most powerful proof: God is the Necessary Being (al-wajib li-dhatihi), while everything else is contingent (mumkin li-dhatihi). Contingent things need a cause to exist and remain in existence; the Necessary One needs nothing. This absolute independence establishes His nobility over all creation.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the categories
Understand the two categories: Necessary by essence (wajib li-dhatihi) and contingent by essence (mumkin li-dhatihi). The Necessary cannot not exist; the contingent can either exist or not.
This is a foundational division in Islamic theology.
2
Apply the logic
Since only God is Necessary, everything else is contingent and needs an external cause (mu'aththir). This means every other being depends on God.
3
Derive the nobility
Because God is independent (ghani) and everything else is dependent, God is necessarily the noblest being.
Nobility correlates with independence and perfection.

This passage is a classification followed by a series of proofs. The author first states that the nobility of a science depends on the nobility of its subject matter. For this science, the subject is God's Essence and attributes, which are the most noble of existents. He then provides five proofs that God is the most noble. 1) God is independent (غني) of any cause, while everything else depends on Him. 2) God is absolutely unique (فرد), so He is independent of any constituent part. 3) The Necessary by essence (الواجب لذاته) is only God; everything else is contingent (ممكن) and in need of a cause, so they all need Him while He needs nothing. 4) The contingent needs the cause both at origination and continuation, and in both existence and non-existence (since its non-existence is due to absence of cause). Thus all other than God are always in need. 5) Existence is nobler than non-existence; God is Existent by Himself and does not admit non-existence, while everything else is contingent and possibly non-existent. Hence God is the Truth (حق) and everything else is perishable. The author concludes that God is the noblest of known things, so knowledge of Him is the noblest science.

Misconception: Some think that being 'contingent' means something is unnecessary or worthless.
Correction: Contingent means dependent on a cause; all creation is contingent on God, but that doesn't make it worthless—only less noble than the Necessary One.

Checkpoint

God's necessity and self-sufficiency make Him infinitely nobler than all contingent beings.

?

Continuous Need and the Nobility of Existence
Fourth and fifth proofs: continuous need of contingent beings and the nobility of existence, concluding God is the Truth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth and fifth proofs: all contingent beings need God at every moment, and existence itself is nobler than non-existence, making God the Truth and all else perishable.

Core Idea
Everything other than God is perpetually dependent and essentially non-existent.

The author deepens the argument: not only do contingent things need God to come into existence, they also need Him to continue existing. Moreover, existence is inherently nobler than non-existence, and God is Existence itself. Everything else, when considered in itself, is nothing—pure annihilation. This establishes God as the only true Being (al-Haqq) and the most noble.

Guided Path
1
Understand continuous need
Read the fourth proof: the contingent needs the cause both at origination (huduth) and at continuation (baqa'). Why can't it sustain itself?
This counters the idea that things become independent after creation.
2
Consider need in non-existence
The author also says the contingent needs the cause in the aspect of non-existence—its non-existence is due to the cause's absence. What does this imply?
Everything depends on God even for its potential non-being.
3
Analyze the fifth proof
Existence (wujud) is nobler than non-existence. God is Existent by Himself; everything else is possibly non-existent. What does this say about their essential natures?
4
Grasp the conclusion
God is the Truth (al-Haqq), and all else is false (batil) and perishing. Why does this make Him the most noble?
Truth is inherently noble; falsehood is low.

الفصلات الأولى
بيان أن هذا العلم أشرف العلوم

أعلم أن شرف العلم إنما يظهر من وجوه :
الوجه الأول من الوجوه الموجبة للشرف : شرف الأمر المبحوث عنه في ذلك العلم. وذلك في هذا العلم، هو ذات الله تعالى وصفاته وهو أشرف الموجودات على الإطلاق. ويدل عليه وجوه : الأول : إنه غني عن الفاعل والقابل وغيره محتاج اليه . والثاني : إنه فرد على الاطلاق، فهو غني عن الجزء المقوّم . الثالث : إن الواجب لذاته ليس إلا هو، وكل ما سواه فهو ممكن لذاته، محتاج إلى المؤثر فيلزم أن كل ما سواه فهو محتاج إليه، وهو غني عن كل ما سواه، فوجب أن يكون هو أشرف الموجودات. والرابع: إنه ثبت أن الممكن كما أنه محتاج إلى المؤثر حال حدوثه ، فهو محتاج إليه أيضا حال بقائه، وكل ما سواه فهو محتاج إليه في جميع أوقاته ، سواء كان ذلك حال الحدوث، أو حال البقاء وكما أنه محتاج إليه في جانب الوجود ففي جانب العدم أيضا كذلك لما ثبت أن الممكن ليس معدوما لذاته، بل علة العدم عدم العلة فثبت بما ذكرنا أن الحق سبحانه وتعالى أشرف من غيره بحسب هذه الاعتبارات . والخامس: هو أن الوجود أشرف من العدم، والواجب لذاته لا يقبل العدم البتة فهو موجود لذاته، وبوجوده يحصل الموجود لكل موجود، بل وجوده كالمنافي للعدم وأما كل ما سواه فإنه ممكن لذاته، والممكن لذاته إذا نظر إليه من حيث هو هو وجد غير موجود وكل ما سواه فإنه إذا اعتبر من حيث هو هو، لم يكن موجوداً. وهو سبحانه إذا اعتبر من حيث هو هو، فهو الموجود. فلهذا المعنى قلنا: إنه حق وما سواه باطل بل الحق أنه لا يليق لفظ الحق إلا له ولا يليق وصف الاعتقاد بأنه حق، إلا باعتقاد وجوده. وأن كل ما سواء فهو الفناء المحض، والهلاك المحض، كما قال في الكتاب ((الإلهي)) كل شيء هالك إلا وجهه) فثبت بهذه الاعتبارات أنه تعالى أشرف الموجودات وأكملها، بل إنه تعالى أشرف وأكمل من أن يقاس هو إلى غيره، فإنه أشرف وأكمل منه . فكلما كان المعلوم أشرف، كان العلم به أشرف ولما كان أشرف المعلومات .
First chapters: Statement that this knowledge is the noblest of sciences. Know that the nobility of knowledge appears from aspects: The first aspect of the aspects necessitating nobility: the nobility of the matter investigated in that science. And that, in this science, is the Essence of God Most High and His attributes, and it is the noblest of existents absolutely. And it is indicated by aspects: First: He is independent of agent and recipient, and other than Him is in need of Him. Second: He is absolutely unique, so He is independent of a constituent part. Third: The Necessary by essence is none other than Him, and everything other than Him is contingent by essence, in need of the efficient cause, so it follows that everything other than Him is in need of Him, while He is independent of everything other than Him, so it is necessary that He is the noblest of existents. Fourth: It is established that the contingent, just as it is in need of the cause at the time of its origination, it is also in need of it at the time of its continuation, and everything other than Him is in need of Him in all its times, whether that time is of origination or continuation; and just as it is in need of Him in the aspect of existence, so too in the aspect of non-existence, because it is established that the contingent is not non-existent by itself, but the cause of non-existence is the non-existence of the cause. So it is established by what we have mentioned that the Truth, Glory be to Him, is more noble than other than Him according to these considerations. Fifth: Existence is more noble than non-existence, and the Necessary by essence does not admit non-existence at all, so He exists by Himself, and by His existence existence is obtained for every existent; rather His existence is like a negation of non-existence. As for everything other than Him, it is contingent by essence, and the contingent by essence, when considered in itself, is found non-existent. And everything other than Him, if considered in itself, is not existent. And He, Glorified, if considered in Himself, is existent. For this meaning we say: He is the Truth and other than Him is false; rather the truth is that the word 'Truth' is not fitting for anyone but Him, and the description of belief as truth is not fitting except through belief in His existence. And that everything other than Him is pure annihilation and pure destruction, as He said in the divine Book: 'Everything is perishing except His Face' (28:88). So it is established by these considerations that He, the Exalted, is the noblest and most perfect of existents, rather He is too noble and perfect to be compared to others, for He is more noble and perfect than them. So whenever the known is more noble, the knowledge of it is more noble, and He is the most noble of known things.

The Nobility of Divine Science Through Its Benefit
The author argues that the divine science is the noblest because it leads to true happiness, both physical and spiritual.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second aspect of the nobility of divine science: that it is the most beneficial science because it leads to both physical and spiritual happiness.

Core Idea
The divine science is the noblest because it unlocks true happiness—body and soul.

In this passage, the author presents the second criterion for evaluating the nobility of sciences: their practical benefit and necessity. He argues that the divine science (الإلهي) is the most noble because it directly addresses humanity's ultimate goal—achieving happiness and avoiding misery. Happiness itself is twofold: physical and spiritual. This sets the stage for understanding why this science is indispensable.

Guided Path

The passage is incomplete; it cuts off after 'that...'.

Two Proofs for the Honor of Ilm al-Kalam
Al-Razi presents two further proofs for the honor of ilm al-kalam: the spiritual experience and its necessity for worldly and religious benefits.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two additional proofs for the honor of ilm al-kalam: the experiential proof of spiritual joy and the practical proof of ordering worldly and religious benefits.

Core Idea
The honor of kalam is proven by both spiritual experience and practical necessity.

Al-Razi presents two more compelling reasons why ilm al-kalam is the noblest science. First, he describes the inner experience of the perfect human: immersion in these cognitions brings profound joy and strength. Second, he argues that no worldly order or religious salvation is possible without the knowledge this science provides. These proofs connect personal spiritual fulfillment to the very fabric of society and the afterlife.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own structure come through before you interpret.
Notice that Al-Razi presents two distinct aspects, each with its own argument.
2
Examine the third aspect
Understand the experiential proof: the more one immerses in these cognitions, the greater the spiritual joy, strength, and delight. This shows happiness is tied to this science.
Al-Razi quotes Quran 13:28 to support this.
3
Examine the fourth aspect
Understand the practical proof: benefits are worldly and religious. Worldly benefits require knowledge of the beginning and return (true laws prevent chaos). Religious benefits require knowledge of God, angels, books, messengers, and the Last Day, only attained through this science.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Note how Al-Razi concludes that this science is the origin of all goods and that mastering it elevates a person to the threshold of angelic station.
Consider how these proofs reinforce each other.

والوجه الثالث في بيان شرف هذا العلم : إن الانسان الكامل يجد من نفسه أنه كلما كان اشتغراق (۱) روحه في هذه المعارف أكمل، وكان خوضه فيها أعظم وانجذابه إليها أتم، وانقطاعه عما سواها ،أوفى كان ابتهاجه بذاته أفضل، وقوة روحه أكمل، وفرحه بذاته أوفى . وكلما كان الأمر بالعكس كانت الأحوال الروحانية والآثار النفسانية (بالعكس) (۲) مما ذكرناه وكل ذلك يدل على أن كل السعادات مربوطة بهذه العلة وكل الكمالات والخيرات طالعة من هذا الافق . كما قال في الكتاب الإلهي : ألا بذكر الله تطمئن القلوب) (۳) والوجه الرابع : إن المصالح المعتبرة إما مصالح المعاش، أو مصالح المعاد. أما مصالح المعاش فلا تنتظم إلا بمعرفة المبدأ والمعاد وذلك لأنه لولا استقرار الشرائع الحقة لزال النظام، واختلت المصالح وحصل الهرج ولم يأمن أحد على روحه ومحبوبه وأما مصالح المعاد فلا يتم شيء منها إلا بمعرفة الله تعالى وبمعرفة ملائكته وكتبه ورسله واليوم الآخر، وذلك لا يحصل إلا بهذا العلم . فظهر بهذه المباحث التي قررناها : أن مبدأ الخيرات ومطلع السعادات، ومنبع الكرامات هو هذا العلم. فمن أحاط به على ما ينبغي كان في آخر مراتب الإنسانية، وأول مراتب الملكية.
The third aspect in explaining the honor of this science: The perfect human finds in himself that whenever his immersion [in these cognitions] is more complete, his journeying in them greater, his attraction to them more perfect, and his detachment from other things more fulfilling, his delight in himself is better, the strength of his soul more complete, and his joy in himself more fulfilling. And whenever the matter is the opposite, the spiritual states and psychological effects are opposite to what we mentioned. And all of that indicates that all happiness is tied to this cause and all perfections and goods arise from this horizon. As He said in the Divine Book: 'Verily, in the remembrance of God do hearts find rest' (13:28). And the fourth aspect: The considered benefits are either benefits of livelihood or benefits of the afterlife. As for the benefits of livelihood, they are not ordered except by knowledge of the beginning and the return, because if the true laws were not established, order would be lost, benefits would be corrupted, chaos would occur, and no one would be safe regarding his life and beloved. As for the benefits of the afterlife, none of them are completed except by knowledge of God Almighty, knowledge of His angels, His books, His messengers, and the Last Day, and that is only attained through this science. So it is evident from these discussions that we have established: that the origin of goods, the rising place of happiness, and the source of honors is this science. Whoever encompasses it as it ought to be is at the end of the levels of humanity and the beginning of the levels of angelic station.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage presents two further proofs (the third and fourth aspects) of the honor of the science of kalam. The third proof is experiential: the more a person immerses in these cognitions, the greater their spiritual joy, strength, and delight. This shows that all happiness is tied to this science, and it quotes Quran 13:28. The fourth proof is practical: benefits (masalih) are of two kinds—worldly and religious. Worldly benefits require knowledge of the beginning and return, which necessitates true laws; without such knowledge, chaos results. Religious benefits require knowledge of God, angels, books, messengers, and the Last Day, which is only attained through this science. The conclusion: this science is the source of all goods, and those who master it reach the peak of humanity and the threshold of angelic rank.

Can Intellects Attain Certainty? The First Argument
Al-Razi introduces the question and presents the first proof from self-knowledge difficulty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first argument against the possibility of certainty, which uses the difficulty of self-knowledge as its starting point.

Core Idea
Self-knowledge, the most obvious knowledge, is profoundly elusive, casting doubt on all certainty.

Al-Razi opens a critical inquiry into the limits of human reason. Before presenting his own view, he reports a skeptical argument from philosophers: if even the clearest knowledge—knowledge of oneself—is deeply problematic, how can we claim certainty about anything? This lesson unpacks the first premise of that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the literal translation. Let the author's own words introduce the question and the first proof.
Notice how Al-Razi frames the debate as a choice between certainty and probability.
2
Identify the two opposing views
The passage first presents the question, then reports the view of philosophers who settle for probability. Identify both the question and the reported view before moving to the argument.
3
Focus on the first premise
The first argument relies on a premise: self-knowledge is the most apparent knowledge yet it is extremely difficult. Think about why this is considered a strong objection to certainty.
Consider the proof given: anyone who knows something must know that they know, implying self-awareness is always present.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثانية
إن هل العقول البشرية سبيل إلى تحصيل الجزم واليقين في هذا العالم أم يكتفى في بعض مباحثه ومطالبه بالأولى والأخلق ؟
رأيت في بعض الكتب : أنه نقل عن عظماء الحكمة وأساطين الفلسفة أنهم قالوا الغاية القصوى في هذا الباب الأخذ بالأولى والأخلق) والتمسك بالجانب الأفضل الأكمل وأما الجزم المانع من النقيض فقد لا يمكن تحصيله في بعض المباحث.
وللقائلين (۲) بهذا القول أن يحتجوا بوجوه :
الحجة الأولى : إن أظهر المعلومات لجميع العقلاء : هو علم الإنسان بذاته (۳) المخصوصة ومعرفته بنفه المخصوصة، ثم هذا العلم مع أنه أظهر العلوم وأجل المعارف قد بلغ في الصعوبة والخفاء إلى حيث عجزت العقول عن الوصول إليه، وإذا كان الحال في أظهر المعلومات كذلك، فالحال في أبعد الأشياء عن مناسبة الأمور المعلومة للخلق كيف يكون؟ وهذه الحجة إنما تم بقرير مقدمات .
المقدمة الأولى : إن أظهر المعلومات لكل أحد ذاته المخصوصة. والذي يدل على أن الأمر كذلك أن كل من علم شيئا فلا بد وأن يعلم كونه عالما
The second chapter: Are human intellects able to attain certainty and conviction in this world, or are they content in some of its inquiries and demands with the more appropriate and fitting? I saw in some books that it is transmitted from the great sages and pillars of philosophy that they said: The ultimate goal in this matter is to adopt the more appropriate and fitting and to adhere to the better and more complete side. As for certainty that prevents the opposite, it may not be possible to attain it in some inquiries. And for those who hold this opinion, they may argue by proofs: The first proof: The most apparent of knowledge for all rational beings is a human's knowledge of his own specific self and his recognition of his own specific soul. Then this knowledge, despite being the most apparent of knowledge and the greatest of cognitions, has reached such difficulty and hiddenness that intellects are incapable of reaching it. If the case is thus regarding the most apparent of knowledge, then what is the case regarding the most remote things from the suitability of known matters for creation? This proof is completed by establishing premises. The first premise: The most apparent of knowledge for everyone is his own specific self. And what indicates that the matter is so is that everyone who knows something must necessarily know that he knows.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage introduces a new section (likely a second chapter) that raises a fundamental epistemological question: can human intellects achieve certainty (jazzm wa yaqīn) in this world, or must they settle for probability (al-awla wa al-akhlaq)? The author reports that some philosophers claim that only probability is attainable. Then, for those who hold this view, the first argument is presented. It begins with the premise that the most evident knowledge for any rational person is knowledge of one's own self. Yet even this self-knowledge is extremely difficult and hidden from the intellect. If the clearest knowledge is so obscure, then how can we expect to know things that are far more remote? The argument is then broken into premises; the first premise is stated: the most evident knowledge for everyone is knowledge of one's own self. The proof for this premise is that anyone who knows something must know that they know it, implying self-awareness.

Key Terms
الجزم
اليقين
الأولى والأخلق
الحجة الأولى
المقدمة الأولى
ذات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Razi himself endorses skepticism about certainty.
Correction: Al-Razi is merely reporting an argument from philosophers; he is not necessarily endorsing it. The passage is part of an examination of this view.

Checkpoint
What is the first premise of the first argument?
A) Certainty is impossible
B) The most apparent knowledge for everyone is knowledge of one's own specific self
C) Philosophers are the ultimate authorities
D) The more appropriate and fitting is the goal

Key Takeaway
The first argument against certainty relies on the surprising claim that self-knowledge, though most evident, is most elusive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If self-knowledge is the most apparent yet most elusive, how honestly have you examined your own soul, your motives, and your hidden biases? What would it take for you to truly know your own deficiencies and not merely assume you know yourself?
?
This argument suggests that even the most basic knowledge is uncertain. Does that shake your confidence in your own beliefs? Or do you rely on something beyond intellect? What is that foundation?
?
The passage says intellects are incapable of reaching self-knowledge. Have you ever found that your intellect fails to give you clarity about yourself? How do you respond when your own mind cannot resolve deep personal questions?

The Problem of Certainty and the Philosophers' View
Introduction to the chapter and the skeptical claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the central debate about human certainty and the position attributed to great philosophers.

Core Idea
Certainty may be unattainable in some investigations.

This passage introduces a major epistemological question: can human intellects achieve absolute certainty in all matters? The author cites the view of great philosophers who argue that the best one can do is choose the more appropriate opinion, as certainty that excludes doubt may be impossible in some areas. This sets the stage for the arguments to come.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core question
Read the opening line and identify the two possibilities: either intellects attain certainty or they fall short in some investigations.
The phrase 'الجزم واليقين' refers to decisive certainty.
2
Understand the philosophers' claim
The author reports that great philosophers said the ultimate goal is to take the more appropriate and worthy, not absolute certainty.
This is a skeptical position, but limited to certain topics.
3
Note the structure of the argument
The passage announces that proponents of this view have several proofs, and the first one will be presented in subsequent passages.
We are only reading the introduction here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثانية: أن هل العقول البشرية سليلة إلى تحصيل الجزم واليقين في هذا العالم أم يكتروني في بعض مباحثه ومطالبه بالفقدان والاختلاف؟ رأيت في بعض الكتب: أنه نقل عن عظماء الحكمة وأساطين الفلسفة أنهم قالوا الغاية القصوى في هذا الباب الأخذ بالأولى والأخلق والتمسك بالجانب الأفضل الأكمل. وأما الجزم المانع من النقيض فقد لا يمكن تحصيله في بعض المباحث. وللقائلين بهذا القول أن يحتجوا بوجوه: الحجة الأولى: إن أظهر المعلومات لجميع العقلاء: هو علم الإنسان بذاته المخصوصة ومعرفته بنفسه المخصوصة، ثم هذا العلم مع أنه أظهر العلوم وأجل المعارف قد بلغ في الصعوبة والخفاء إلى حيث عجزت العقول عن الوصول إليه. وإذا كان الحال في أظهر المعلومات كذلك، فالحال في أبعد الأشياء عن مناسبة الأمور المعلومة للخلق كيف يكون؟ وهذه الحجة إنما تم بقرير مقدمات. المقدمة الأولى: إن أظهر المعلومات لكل أحد ذاته المخصوصة. والذي يدل على أن الأمر كذلك أن كل من علم شيئا فلا بد وأن يعلم كونه عالما بذلك الشيء، ولذلك فإنه يقول: أدركت هذا الشيء وعرفته، إلا أن علمه بكونه عالما بذلك الشيء، مسبوق بعلمه بذاته المخصوصة (لأن من لا يعلم ذاته، كيف يمكنه أن يحكم عليها بكونها عالمة بذلك المعلوم)؟ وكذلك فإنهم قالوا كل تصديق فإنه مسبوق بتصور، ومن الظاهر أن الشرط سابق بالرتبة على المشروط. وهذا يدل على أن علم كل أحد بأي معلوم كان مشروط بعلمه بذاته المخصوصة ومسبوق بعلمه بذاته المخصوصة، فيثبت أن علم كل أحد بذاته سابق على علمه بكل ما يغاير ذاته سواء كان ذلك العلم من البديهيات الجليات، أو من الكسبيات. والسابق على جملة الجليات أولى بكونه جليا بديهيا. فثبت بهذا البرهان أن علم كل أحد بذاته المخصوصة، أجلى العلوم وأجلها وأظهرها وأقواها. المقدمة الثانية في تقرير أن علم كل أحد بذاته المخصوصة علم في غاية الصعوبة والخفاء. والذي يدل عليه أن المشار إليه لكل أحد بقوله: أنا إما أن يكون هو هذا الهيكل المشاهد، أو يكون جسما من الأجسام الموجودة داخل هذا الهيكل، أو يكون صفة من صفات هذا الهيكل أو يكون جوهرا مجردا عن هذا البدن وعن علائقه. وهذه الأقسام الأربعة قد حارت عقول العقلاء فيها، ودارت رؤوسهم في تعيينها. ومن تأمل في مباحث كلام النفس يجد أن هذه المسألة قد بلغت في الصعوبة إلى الغاية القصوى. فثبت أن هذا العلم صعب غامض. المقدمة الثالثة: إنا قد بينا أن أظهر المعلومات هو علم كل أحد بذاته المخصوصة ونفسه المعينة وبينا أنه مع كونها أظهر المعلومات، فقد بلغ العلم بها إلى الغاية القصوى في الصعوبة والخفاء والغموض. وإذا ثبت
The Second Chapter: Whether human intellects attain certainty and conviction in this world or fall short in some of its investigations and demands due to deficiency and disagreement? I saw in some books: that it is transmitted from the great wise men and pillars of philosophy that they said: the ultimate goal in this subject is to take the more appropriate and more worthy and to adhere to the better and more perfect side. As for certainty that prevents the opposite, it may not be attainable in some investigations. And those who hold this opinion may argue with several points: The First Evidence: The most apparent of known things to all rational people is a human's knowledge of his particular self and his recognition of his particular soul. Then this knowledge, despite being the most apparent of sciences and the greatest of cognitions, has reached such difficulty and hiddenness that intellects are incapable of reaching it. And if the situation is like this regarding the most apparent of known things, then how is the situation regarding the farthest things from suitability to the things known to creation? This evidence is only completed by establishing premises. The First Premise: The most apparent of known things to everyone is his particular self. What indicates that the matter is so is that whoever knows something, he must know that he knows that thing, and therefore he says: I have perceived this thing and recognized it, except that his knowledge that he knows that thing is preceded by his knowledge of his particular self (because he who does not know his self, how can he judge it as being knowledgeable of that known thing?). And likewise they said: every assent is preceded by a conception, and it is apparent that the condition is prior in rank to the conditioned. This indicates that everyone's knowledge of any known thing is conditioned by his knowledge of his particular self and preceded by his knowledge of his particular self. So it is established that everyone's knowledge of himself precedes his knowledge of everything other than himself, whether that knowledge is from the self-evident clear matters or from acquired matters. And what precedes the totality of self-evident matters is more deserving of being clear and self-evident. So by this proof it is established that everyone's knowledge of his particular self is the clearest of sciences, the greatest, the most apparent, and the strongest. The Second Premise: Concerning establishing that everyone's knowledge of his particular self is knowledge of extreme difficulty and hiddenness. What indicates this is that the referent for everyone by his saying 'I' is either this observable structure, or a body from the bodies existing inside this structure, or a quality from the qualities of this structure, or a substance abstracted from this body and its attachments. These four divisions have perplexed the intellects of the wise, and their heads have spun in determining them. And whoever contemplates the investigations of the psychology will find that this issue has reached extreme difficulty. So it is established that this knowledge is difficult and obscure. The Third Premise: We have shown that the most apparent of known things is everyone's knowledge of his particular self and his specific soul, and we have shown that while it is the most apparent of known things, knowledge of it has reached the utmost limit in difficulty, hiddenness, and obscurity. And when it is established...
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of a chapter and presents the first argument (الحجة الأولى) for the claim that human intellects cannot attain certainty in some investigations. The author first states the claim being debated: whether intellects fall short in some inquiries. He then cites great philosophers who say the ultimate aim is to take the more appropriate and worthy, and that certainty may not be attainable. Then he begins the first evidence, which is structured as a proof with three premises. 

**First premise:** The most apparent knowledge to everyone is knowledge of one's own particular self. The proof is that any knowledge of something requires knowing that one knows it, which itself requires prior knowledge of one's self. This is because the statement 'I know this' presupposes awareness of oneself. Also, every assent (تصديق) is preceded by a conception (تصور), and the condition (knowledge of self) is prior in rank. Therefore, self-knowledge precedes all other knowledge, including the most obvious self-evident truths, so it is the clearest and strongest. 

**Second premise:** Despite being the most apparent, self-knowledge is extremely difficult and hidden. The evidence is that the referent of the word 'I' has four possible meanings: the physical body, a body inside it, a quality, or a non-material substance. Philosophers have been perplexed about which one is correct, showing the extreme difficulty of this knowledge. 

**Third premise:** As a result of the first two premises, we have established that the most apparent knowledge (self-knowledge) is also extremely difficult. The author then states a conditional conclusion: if the most apparent knowledge is so difficult, then how much more difficult must be matters that are far from our ordinary experience? Though the text cuts off, the conclusion is implied: if the clearest knowledge is obscure, then certainty in other matters is even harder, supporting the claim. The argument is not completed in this passage, but the three premises are fully given.

Key Terms
الجزم واليقين
الأخذ بالأولى والأخلق

Key Takeaway
Certainty that excludes doubt may not be achievable in every field of inquiry, according to some philosophers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The philosophers claim that the best we can achieve in some investigations is 'الأخذ بالأولى والأخلق' – preferring the more plausible opinion. In which areas of your own religious knowledge have you settled for ‘good enough’ rather than seeking certainty? What would it cost you to pursue a deeper conviction?
?
When you read that even great wise men admitted that absolute certainty may be impossible in some investigations, does that shake your confidence in your own intellect or drive you to rely more on divine revelation? Be honest: which fear is more real to you – the fear of being wrong or the fear of not having all the answers?
?
The passage highlights 'الفقدان والاختلاف' (deficiency and disagreement) as obstacles to certainty. Reflect: Do you use the existence of scholarly disagreement as an excuse to avoid pursuing certainty in your own beliefs? What is one area where you have allowed disagreement to settle your heart instead of motivating you to seek the truth?

The First Evidence: Self-Knowledge as the Most Apparent
First premise of the first evidence: self-knowledge is the most apparent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first premise of the argument: that knowledge of one's own self is the most apparent and precedes all other knowledge.

Core Idea
Self-knowledge is the clearest and most fundamental knowledge.

The first evidence begins by asserting that the most obvious knowledge any person has is knowledge of their own particular self. The author then provides a logical proof: to know anything else, one must first be aware of oneself. This self-knowledge, in turn, is the foundation for all other knowledge, even the most self-evident truths.

Guided Path
1
Read the first premise statement
Locate the sentence 'المقدمة الأولى: إن أظهر المعلومات لكل أحد ذاته المخصوصة' and note what it claims.
2
Follow the proof
The proof says that any knowledge of something requires knowing that one knows it, which presupposes self-knowledge. Also, every assent is preceded by a conception, and the condition precedes the conditioned.
The argument relies on psychological and logical principles.
3
Understand the conclusion
The proof concludes that self-knowledge is prior to and clearer than all other knowledge, including self-evident truths.
This sets up a paradox: the clearest knowledge is also the most difficult.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
If self-knowledge is the clearest and strongest knowledge, why do you think you often avoid looking inward? What are you afraid of finding, and how does that fear keep you from the foundation of all certainty?
?
You claim to know many things—your beliefs, your habits, your relationships—but do you actually know the 'you' that knows all these? Before you finish this lesson, take a moment to ask yourself: 'Who is the knower behind my knowledge?' and be honest: is that answer clear or vague?

The Second Premise: The Difficulty of Self-Knowledge
Second premise: self-knowledge is extremely difficult.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why self-knowledge, despite being the most apparent, is also extremely difficult and obscure.

Core Idea
The self is a mystery: 'I' can refer to four different things, and philosophers disagree which one is correct.

The second premise aims to show that the same self-knowledge that is clearest is also deeply perplexing. The evidence is that when people refer to themselves as 'I', they could mean their physical body, a body inside it, a quality, or a non-material substance. Philosophers have been unable to determine which is correct, demonstrating the extreme difficulty of this seemingly simple knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the second premise statement
The premise asserts that self-knowledge is extremely difficult and hidden.
2
Consider the four possibilities
The referent of 'I' could be: the observable body, a body inside it, a quality, or a non-material substance.
These are the main philosophical theories of self.
3
Understand the confusion
The author notes that wise people have been perplexed about which one is correct, showing the difficulty of even defining what the self is.
This is a classic problem in philosophy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثانية: أن هل العقول البشرية سليلة إلى تحصيل الجزم واليقين في هذا العالم أم يكتروني في بعض مباحثه ومطالبه بالفقدان والاختلاف؟ رأيت في بعض الكتب: أنه نقل عن عظماء الحكمة وأساطين الفلسفة أنهم قالوا الغاية القصوى في هذا الباب الأخذ بالأولى والأخلق والتمسك بالجانب الأفضل الأكمل. وأما الجزم المانع من النقيض فقد لا يمكن تحصيله في بعض المباحث. وللقائلين بهذا القول أن يحتجوا بوجوه: الحجة الأولى: إن أظهر المعلومات لجميع العقلاء: هو علم الإنسان بذاته المخصوصة ومعرفته بنفسه المخصوصة، ثم هذا العلم مع أنه أظهر العلوم وأجل المعارف قد بلغ في الصعوبة والخفاء إلى حيث عجزت العقول عن الوصول إليه. وإذا كان الحال في أظهر المعلومات كذلك، فالحال في أبعد الأشياء عن مناسبة الأمور المعلومة للخلق كيف يكون؟ وهذه الحجة إنما تم بقرير مقدمات. المقدمة الأولى: إن أظهر المعلومات لكل أحد ذاته المخصوصة. والذي يدل بديهيا. فثبت بهذا البرهان أن علم كل أحد بذاته المخصوصة، أجلى العلوم وأجلها وأظهرها وأقواها. المقدمة الثانية في تقرير أن علم كل أحد بذاته المخصوصة علم في غاية الصعوبة والخفاء. والذي يدل عليه أن المشار إليه لكل أحد بقوله: أنا إما أن يكون هو هذا الهيكل المشاهد، أو يكون جسما من الأجسام الموجودة داخل هذا الهيكل، أو يكون صفة من صفات هذا الهيكل أو يكون جوهرا مجردا عن هذا البدن وعن علائقه. وهذه الأقسام
