The Analogy of Mountains and Minerals
Analogy between mineral deposits and spiritual capacity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the distribution of precious minerals in mountains serves as an analogy for the varying spiritual capacities of human souls.

Core Idea
Spiritual attainment depends on innate capacity, not just effort.

In this passage, the author draws on an empirical observation of nature to illustrate a spiritual truth. Just as mountains contain different minerals requiring different amounts of labor, souls have varying capacities for divine knowledge. This analogy sets the stage for understanding why spiritual paths differ.

Guided Path
1
Observe the mineral distribution
Read how empirical observation shows that precious minerals are rare and variable.
Notice the contrast between base metals and gold/silver.
2
Apply the analogy to souls
Notice how the author maps mountain types to soul types: barren, productive, and rare cave-filled mountains.
3
Recognize the infinite variation
Understand that the ranks of souls, like mountains, differ in ways that cannot be precisely determined.
This prepares for the warnings in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذلك لأن الاستقراء يشهد بأن معادن الحديد والنحاس والرصاص والابوت أكثر بكثير من معادن الذهب والفضة، ثم نقول أن معادن الذهب والفضة أيضا مختلفة فمن الجبال ما يحتاج فيها إلى العمل الكثير الشاق حتى يحصل منه ذهب قليل، ومنها ما لا يكون كذلك، بل العمل القليل السهل قد يوصل إلى وجدان المال الكثير، وبين هذين الطرفين أوساط متباينة الدرجات في القلة والكثرة، ثم لا يزال يزداد الخير والكمال حتى أنه ربما انتهى الأمر إلى جبل يجد الانسان فيه غارا مملوءا من الذهب والفضة. إذا عرفت هذه المراتب ظهر عندك أن مثل هذا الجبل المشتمل على مثل هذا الغار يكون نادرا جدا ولا يتفق الوصول إليه ولا الفوز به إلا في الأدوار المتباعدة جدا. وإذا عرفت هذا فنقول: لتكن الأرواح البشرية جارية مجرى الجبال والتلال، ولتكن أنوار معرفة الله ومحبته جارية مجرى الذهب الإبريز الخالص، وكما أن أكثر جبال الدنيا وتلالها خالية عن المعادن، فكذلك أرواح أكثر الخلق خالية عن الميل إلى عالم الروحانيات، ثم إن هذا القسم لو بالغ في الرياضة الجسدانية فإنه يقل انتفاعه بها، كما أن الجبل الخالي عن المعدن لو أتعب الإنسان نفسه في علاجه فإنه لا يجد فيه شيئاً البتة. وأما القسم الثاني: وهو الأرواح التي حصلت فيها هذه المعادن فكما أن الجبال المشتملة على معادن الذهب والفضة مختلفة، فبعضها يحتاج فيه إلى العمل الكثير ليحصل الفوز بالنفع القليل، فكذلك هذا القسم من الأرواح منها ما يحتاج إلى الرياضة الشديدة الكثيرة، ليحصل له القدر القليل من المكاشفات، ومنها ما لا يكون كذلك بل العمل القليل يوصله إلى الفوز بالنعم العظيمة. وكما أن مراتب الجبال المشتملة على المعادن مختلفة في القلة والكثرة، اختلافا لا يمكن ضبطه فكذلك مراتب درجات الأرواح مختلفة بالقوة والضعف والقلة والكثرة، اختلافا لا يمكن ضبطه وكما أنه لا يبعد أن يوجد في النوادر جبل مشتمل على غار مملوء من الذهب، فكذلك لا يمتنع أن يوجد في الاعصار المتباعدة إنسان يكون غار روحه مملوءا من أنوار جلال الله. وإذا وقفت على هذا المثال، عرفت أنه ليس كل من خاض في الرياضة، وإن كانت على أصعب الوجوه، وجب أن يصل إلى شيء. وأيضا فليس كل من وصل إلى شيء، فقد وصل إلى الغاية، بل الغاية في هذا الطريق ممتنعة، فكما أنه لا نهاية لجلال الله، ولعلو كبريائه، فكذلك لا نهاية لمراتب السعادات في هذا الباب. فليكن هذا المثال نصب عينيك، وقائها عند خيالك لئلا تغتر، فتظن أن كل من سلك وصل وكل من طلب وجد، ونقول: لا ننكر أن لتلك الرياضات آثارا من بعض الوجوه، فإن للمواظب على العمل أثرا من بعض الوجوه. إلا أنه من الظاهر أنه ليس التكحل في العينين كالكحل. ونعم ما قاله حكيم الشعراء: يراد من القلب نسيانكم وتأبى الطباع على الثاقل. وقال أرسطاطاليس: من أراد أن يشرع في طلب هذه المعارف الإلهية فليستحدث لنفسه فطرة أخرى والمراد منه أن يبالغ الإنسان في تجويد عقله من علائق الحس والوهم والخيال. هذا ما في هذا المقام.
And that is because empirical observation (istiqrāʾ) testifies that the mines of iron, copper, lead, and [al-abūt?] are much more abundant than the mines of gold and silver. Then we say that the mines of gold and silver are also varied. Among the mountains are those that require much laborious work to obtain a little gold, and among them are those that are not like that; rather, easy little work may lead to finding much wealth. Between these two extremes are intermediate degrees varying in scarcity and abundance. Then goodness and perfection keep increasing until it may reach a point where a man finds a cave filled with gold and silver. When you have understood these ranks, it becomes clear to you that such a mountain containing such a cave is extremely rare, and arriving at it or obtaining it only happens in very distant eras. And when you have understood this, we say: Let human souls be like mountains and hills, and let the lights of knowledge of God and His love be like pure refined gold. And just as most mountains and hills of the world are devoid of minerals, so too most people's souls are devoid of inclination toward the spiritual world. Moreover, if this type exerts itself strenuously in bodily discipline, it benefits little from it, just as a mountain devoid of mineral, if a man wearies himself in working it, he will find nothing at all in it. As for the second type: the souls in which these minerals are present, just as mountains containing gold and silver differ—some need much work to gain little benefit—so too among this type of souls, some need intense and abundant discipline to obtain a small amount of unveilings (mukāshafāt), and some are not like that; rather, little work leads them to gain great bounties. And just as the ranks of mountains containing minerals differ in scarcity and abundance in a way that cannot be precisely determined, so too the ranks and degrees of souls differ in strength and weakness and scarcity and abundance in a way that cannot be precisely determined. And just as it is not unlikely that in rare instances a mountain containing a cave filled with gold exists, so too it is not impossible that in distant ages a person exists whose spirit's cave is filled with the lights of the majesty of God. And when you have grasped this example, you know that not everyone who plunges into discipline, even in the most difficult ways, necessarily attains something. And also, not everyone who attains something has attained the ultimate goal. Rather, the ultimate goal in this path is unattainable. For just as there is no end to God's majesty and the height of His greatness, so too there is no end to the ranks of felicities in this matter. So let this example be before your eyes and present in your imagination, lest you be deceived and think that everyone who travels arrives and everyone who seeks finds. And we say: We do not deny that these disciplines have some effects in some respects, for persistence in action has some effect in some respects. However, it is obvious that applying kohl to the eyes is not like kohl itself. How excellent is what the wise poet said: 'Forgetfulness of you is desired from the heart, but natures refuse the heavy one.' And Aristotle said: 'Whoever intends to undertake pursuing these divine sciences, let him acquire for himself another nature.' The intended meaning is that a person should strive to purify his intellect from the attachments of sense, illusion (wahm), and imagination. This is what is in this place.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage presents an extended analogy (tamthīl) between the distribution of precious minerals in mountains and the spiritual capacities of human souls. It is a methodological argument for the variability of spiritual attainment and the rarity of high levels of divine knowledge. The author begins by noting an empirical observation: the mines of base metals like iron, copper, and lead are far more common than those of gold and silver. Among gold and silver mines, there is further variation: some require great effort for meager yield, while others yield much with little work. The rarest of all is a mountain containing a cave filled with gold and silver, attained only in very distant ages. The author then applies this directly to souls: let the souls be like mountains, and the lights of knowledge and love of God be like pure gold. Most souls are devoid of any inclination to the spiritual realm, just as most mountains lack minerals. Even if such a soul engages in strenuous bodily discipline, it reaps little benefit—like working a barren mountain. The second type of soul has some innate spiritual 'mineral'—a capacity for divine knowledge. Among these, there is variation: some require intense discipline for only a small unveiling, while others achieve great bounties with little effort. Just as the degrees of mineral wealth in mountains are infinitely varied, so too the strengths of souls vary infinitely. Rarely, a soul may be filled with the lights of God’s majesty, like a cave filled with gold in a mountain. The author then draws two warnings: first, not everyone who undertakes discipline necessarily attains anything; second, even those who attain something have not reached the ultimate goal, because God’s majesty is infinite and so the degrees of felicity are endless. The example is meant to prevent the reader from being deceived into thinking that all who seek will find. The author concedes that disciplines have some effect, but emphasizes that the effect is not equivalent to the goal itself—using the analogy of applying kohl (antimony) to the eyes, which is not the same as the kohl itself. The passage concludes with a quote from a wise poet and from Aristotle, stating that one must acquire a new nature and purify the intellect from attachments of sense, illusion, and imagination.

Key Terms
استقراء (istiqrāʾ) empirical observation
رياضة (riyāḍa) spiritual discipline
مكاشفات (mukāshafāt) unveilings

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Anyone who undertakes spiritual discipline will necessarily attain divine knowledge.
Correction: The analogy shows that innate capacity is crucial; even with great effort, a barren soul will not attain much, and even among capable souls, the degree of attainment varies.

Checkpoint
According to the analogy in the passage, what does a mountain containing a cave filled with gold represent?
A) A person who attains the highest spiritual knowledge and divine illumination.
B) A person who exerts much effort in discipline but gains little.
C) A person who has no inclination toward spirituality.
D) A person who only needs a little discipline to succeed.

Key Takeaway
The analogy teaches that spiritual attainment is not solely a matter of effort; it depends on the soul's innate capacity, which varies infinitely.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What does this passage reveal about your own soul? If you have struggled spiritually despite effort, does it mean you lack capacity, or that your capacity requires more discipline? And what difference does that realization make in how you approach your prayers tonight?
?
The analogy says most souls are like barren mountains. If you suspect you are among them, does that lead you to despair or to desperate need for Allah's grace? What specific plea can you make to Allah right now to soften your heart?
?
If you sense that you are among those who benefit easily, are you grateful or proud? How does this passage warn you against taking spiritual gifts for granted, and what responsibility does ease place on your shoulders?

Method of Learning

Warnings and Conclusion on Spiritual Pursuit
Warnings and conclusions from the mountain analogy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two key warnings about spiritual discipline: that not everyone who strives attains anything, and that even those who attain do not reach the ultimate goal.

Core Idea
Spiritual attainment is rare and never final; only God is infinite.

Having established the analogy of mountains and minerals, the author now draws important conclusions. He warns against assuming that discipline guarantees results and reminds us that the highest goal is unattainable because God's majesty is infinite. The lesson ends with practical advice from a poet and Aristotle.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the rarity of high attainment
Understand that a soul filled with divine lights is as rare as a mountain cave filled with gold.
2
Heed the first warning
Not everyone who undertakes discipline necessarily attains anything, even if the discipline is extreme.
The example of a barren mountain shows this.
3
Heed the second warning
Even those who attain something have not reached the ultimate because God's perfections are endless.
The ultimate goal is unattainable.
4
Apply the practical advice
Consider the quotes: the poet's reminder of human nature and Aristotle's call to purify the intellect from sensory attachments.
This shows what true preparation requires.

فيه شيئاً البتة. وأما القسم الثاني: وهو الأرواح التي حصلت فيها هذه المعادن فكما أن الجبال المشتملة على معادن الذهب والفضة مختلفة، فبعضها يحتاج فيه إلى العمل الكثير ليحصل الفوز بالنفع القليل، فكذلك هذا المعادن مختلفة في القلة والكثرة، اختلافا لا يمكن ضبطه فكذلك مراتب درجات الأرواح مختلفة بالقوة والضعف والقلة والكثرة، اختلافا لا يمكن ضبطه وكما أنه لا يبعد أن يوجد في النوادر جبل مشتمل على غار مملوء من الذهب، فكذلك لا يمتنع أن يوجد في الاعصار المتباعدة إنسان يكون غار روحه مملوءا من أنوار جلال الله. وإذا وقفت على هذا المثال، عرفت أنه ليس كل من خاض في الرياضة، وإن كانت على أصعب الوجوه، وجب أن يصل إلى شيء. وأيضا فليس كل من وصل إلى شيء، فقد وصل إلى الغاية، بل الغاية في هذا الطريق ممتنعة، فكما أنه لا نهاية لجلال الله، ولعلو كبريائه، فكذلك لا نهاية لمراتب السعادات في هذا الباب. فليكن هذا المثال نصب في العينين كالكحل. ونعم ما قاله حكيم الشعراء: يراد من القلب نسيانكم وتأبى الطباع على الثاقل. وقال أرسطاطاليس: من أراد أن يشرع في طلب هذه المعارف الإلهية فليستحدث لنفسه فطرة أخرى والمراد منه أن يبالغ الإنسان في تجويد عقله من علائق الحس والوهم والخيال. هذا ما في هذا المقام.
And that is because empirical observation (istiqrāʾ) testifies that the mines of iron, copper, lead, and [al-abūt?] are much more abundant than the mines of gold and silver. Then we say that the mines of gold and silver are also varied. Among the mountains are those that require much laborious work to obtain a little gold, and among them are those that are not like that; rather, easy little work may lead to finding much wealth. Between these two extremes are intermediate degrees varying in scarcity and abundance. Then goodness and perfection keep increasing until it may reach a point where a man finds a cave filled with gold and silver. When you have understood these ranks, it becomes clear to you that such a mountain containing such a cave is extremely rare, and arriving at it or obtaining it only happens in very distant eras. And when you have understood this, we say: Let human souls be like mountains and hills, and let the lights of knowledge of God and His love be like pure refined gold. And just as most mountains and hills of the world are devoid of minerals, so too most people's souls are devoid of inclination toward the spiritual world. Moreover, if this type exerts itself strenuously in bodily discipline, it benefits little from it, just as a mountain devoid of mineral, if a man wearies himself in working it, he will find nothing at all in it. As for the second type: the souls in which these minerals are present, just as mountains containing gold and silver differ—some need much work to gain little benefit—so too among this type of souls, some need intense and abundant discipline to obtain a small amount of unveilings (mukāshafāt), and some are not like that; rather, little work leads them to gain great bounties. And just as the ranks of mountains containing minerals differ in scarcity and abundance in a way that cannot be precisely determined, so too the ranks and degrees of souls differ in strength and weakness and scarcity and abundance in a way that cannot be precisely determined. And just as it is not unlikely that in rare instances a mountain containing a cave filled with gold exists, so too it is not impossible that in distant ages a person exists whose spirit's cave is filled with the lights of the majesty of God. And when you have grasped this example, you know that not everyone who plunges into discipline, even in the most difficult ways, necessarily attains something. And also, not everyone who attains something has attained the ultimate goal. Rather, the ultimate goal in this path is unattainable. For just as there is no end to God's majesty and the height of His greatness, so too there is no end to the ranks of felicities in this matter. So let this example be before your eyes and present in your imagination, lest you be deceived and think that everyone who travels arrives and everyone who seeks finds. And we say: We do not deny that these disciplines have some effects in some respects, for persistence in action has some effect in some respects. However, it is obvious that applying kohl to the eyes is not like kohl itself. How excellent is what the wise poet said: 'Forgetfulness of you is desired from the heart, but natures refuse the heavy one.' And Aristotle said: 'Whoever intends to undertake pursuing these divine sciences, let him acquire for himself another nature.' The intended meaning is that a person should strive to purify his intellect from the attachments of sense, illusion (wahm), and imagination. This is what is in this place.

Commentary
This passage presents an extended analogy (tamthīl) between the distribution of precious minerals in mountains and the spiritual capacities of human souls. It is a methodological argument for the variability of spiritual attainment and the rarity of high levels of divine knowledge. The author begins by noting an empirical observation: the mines of base metals like iron, copper, and lead are far more common than those of gold and silver. Among gold and silver mines, there is further variation: some require great effort for meager yield, while others yield much with little work. The rarest of all is a mountain containing a cave filled with gold and silver, attained only in very distant ages. The author then applies this directly to souls: let the souls be like mountains, and the lights of knowledge and love of God be like pure gold. Most souls are devoid of any inclination to the spiritual realm, just as most mountains lack minerals. Even if such a soul engages in strenuous bodily discipline, it reaps little benefit—like working a barren mountain. The second type of soul has some innate spiritual 'mineral'—a capacity for divine knowledge. Among these, there is variation: some require intense discipline for only a small unveiling, while others achieve great bounties with little effort. Just as the degrees of mineral wealth in mountains are infinitely varied, so too the strengths of souls vary infinitely. Rarely, a soul may be filled with the lights of God’s majesty, like a cave filled with gold in a mountain. The author then draws two warnings: first, not everyone who undertakes discipline necessarily attains anything; second, even those who attain something have not reached the ultimate goal, because God’s majesty is infinite and so the degrees of felicity are endless. The example is meant to prevent the reader from being deceived into thinking that all who seek will find. The author concedes that disciplines have some effect, but emphasizes that the effect is not equivalent to the goal itself—using the analogy of applying kohl (antimony) to the eyes, which is not the same as the kohl itself. The passage concludes with a quote from a wise poet and from Aristotle, stating that one must acquire a new nature and purify the intellect from attachments of sense, illusion, and imagination.

Key Terms
فطرة (fiṭra) nature/disposition
علائق الحس والوهم والخيال (ʿalāʾiq al-ḥiss wa-l-wahm wa-l-khayāl) attachments of sense, illusion, and imagination

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If I practice spiritual discipline intensely, I will reach the highest level of divine knowledge.
Correction: The passage teaches that even with extreme discipline, one may attain nothing, and even if one attains something, it is not the ultimate. True success requires innate capacity and purification from sensory attachments.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ultimate goal in the spiritual path?
A) Attaining divine knowledge completely.
B) It is unattainable because God's majesty is infinite.
C) Achieving a cave full of gold.
D) Following Aristotle's advice.

Key Takeaway
Spiritual discipline is necessary but not sufficient; true progress requires innate capacity and purification, and the ultimate goal is never fully reached.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
After reading that 'not everyone who disciplines attains something,' examine your own spiritual practices: Are you secretly assuming that your efforts guarantee progress? If you were told today that all your disciplines might yield nothing in this life, would you still continue? Commit now to a single act of worship this week with no expectation of reward, just for God's sake.
?
The passage says the ultimate goal is unattainable because God's majesty is infinite. Have you been chasing a 'final state' of spiritual perfection, thinking you could arrive? How does it change your daily walk with God to know the journey never ends? Write a prayer surrendering your desire for completion and asking for contentment in the endless pursuit.
?
Aristotle advises to 'acquire another nature' by purifying the intellect from attachments. What specific attachment – a sensory pleasure, a false assumption, or a wandering imagination – is currently weighing down your heart? What concrete step will you take this week to cut that attachment, even if it feels like losing part of yourself?

Method of Learning
intermediate
6 min
The Mountain Analogy: Spiritual Attainment Is Rare and Infinite
The author uses an analogy of mountains and souls to warn against spiritual pride.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why spiritual discipline alone does not guarantee gnosis, and how the author uses the analogy of mountains to caution against pride.

Core Idea
Spiritual attainment is rare, infinite, and requires complete intellectual purification.

This passage from Al-Matalib al-Aliyah uses a vivid mountain analogy to warn against the false confidence that comes from spiritual practice. The author argues that just as mountains vary and only rarely contain gold, so souls vary and only rarely attain divine illumination. Moreover, even partial attainment is not the ultimate goal, which is infinite. This lesson unpacks the analogy and its implications for the spiritual seeker.

Guided Path
1
Read the analogy carefully
Read the mountain-gold analogy from the beginning. Notice how the author draws a parallel between the varying levels of mountains and the varying levels of souls. Observe the rarity emphasized.
The author uses 'لا يمكن ضبطه' (cannot be measured) to stress the limitless variation.
2
Identify the warning
Find the sentence that begins 'وإذا وقفت على هذا المثال' — here the author explicitly warns that not everyone who practices attains something, and even those who attain may not reach the ultimate goal.
The ultimate goal is unattainable because God's majesty is infinite.
3
Understand the quotes
Read the poetic quote and Aristotle's statement. The first says that natures resist forgetting (attachment to things of lower world), and the second advises to acquire a new nature by purifying the intellect.
These reinforce that mere discipline isn't enough; transformation is needed.
4
Reflect on the lesson
Consider why intellectual purification (from sense, estimation, imagination) is necessary alongside discipline. The author wants to prevent spiritual pride and false confidence.
The kohl analogy (التكحل في العينين كالكحل) indicates that mere appearance of effort is not the same as substance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكما أن مراتب الجبال (المشتملة على المعادن) (۱) مختلفة في القلة والكثرة، اختلافا لا يمكن ضبطه فكذلك مراتب درجات الأرواح مختلفة بالقوة والضعف والقلة والكثرة، اختلافا لا يمكن ضبطه وكما أنه لا يبعد أن يوجد في النوادر جبل مشتمل على غار مملوء من الذهب (۲) ، فكذلك لا يمتنع أن يوجد في الاعصار المتباعدة إنسان يكون غار روحه مملوءا من أنوار جلال الله .
وإذا وقفت على هذا المثال، عرفت أنه ليس كل من خاض في الرياضة، وإن كانت على أصعب الوجوه، وجب أن يصل إلى شيء.
وأيضا فليس كل من وصل إلى شيء، فقد وصل إلى الغاية، بل الغاية في هذا الطريق ممتنعة، فكما أنه لا نهاية لجلال الله، ولعلو كبريائه، فكذلك لا نهاية لمراتب السعادات في هذا الباب.
فليكن هذا المثال نصب عينيك، وقائها عند خيالك لئلا تغتر، فتظن أن كل من سلك وصل وكل من طلب وجد، ونقول: لا ننكر أن لتلك الرياضات آثارا من بعض الوجوه، فإن للمواظب على العمل أثرا من بعض الوجوه. إلا أنه من الظاهر أنه ليس التكحل في العينين كالكحل .
ونعم ما قاله حكيم الشعراء :
يراد من القلب نسيانكم وتأبى الطباع على الثاقل
وقال أرسطاطاليس: من أراد أن يشرع (۳) في طلب هذه المعارف الإلهية فليستحدث لنفسه فطرة أخرى والمراد منه أن يبالغ الإنسان في تجويد عقله من علائق الحس (والوهم) (٤) والخيال. هذا ما في هذا المقام.
And just as the levels of mountains (containing minerals) differ in paucity and abundance, a difference that cannot be measured, so too the levels of the degrees of souls differ in strength and weakness and paucity and abundance, a difference that cannot be measured. And just as it is not improbable that, in rare cases, there exists a mountain containing a cavern filled with gold, so too it is not impossible that, in distant ages, there exists a person whose heart's cavern is filled with the lights of the majesty of God. And if you understand this example, you will know that not everyone who plunges into spiritual discipline (riyada), even if it is in the most difficult ways, necessarily attains something. And also, not everyone who attains something has attained the ultimate goal; rather, the ultimate goal in this path is unattainable. For just as there is no end to God's majesty and the sublimity of His greatness, so too there is no end to the levels of felicities in this matter. So let this example be before your eyes and a shield for your imagination, lest you be deceived and think that everyone who travels arrives and everyone who seeks finds. And we say: we do not deny that those disciplines have effects in certain respects, for perseverance in action has an effect in some respects. But it is apparent that applying kohl (kohl) to the eyes is not like kohl itself. Excellent is what the poet-sage said: 'The heart is required to forget you, but natures refuse to the heavy.' And Aristotle said: 'Whoever wants to embark on seeking these divine sciences should acquire for himself another nature.' What is meant is that a person should make his intellect pure from the attachments of sense, (estimation), and imagination. This is what pertains to this station.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage presents an extended analogy between the physical world (mountains containing minerals) and the spiritual world (souls containing degrees of divine illumination). The author argues that just as mountains vary enormously and only rarely contain a cavern of gold, so souls vary in strength and purity, and only rarely does a soul become filled with the lights of God's majesty. The purpose is to warn against the false assumption that anyone who engages in rigorous spiritual discipline necessarily attains genuine spiritual insight. Moreover, even if one attains something, it may not be the ultimate goal, which is infinite because God's majesty is infinite. The author then quotes a poetic maxim to illustrate that the heart's natural disposition may resist change, and Aristotle to emphasize that entering these divine sciences requires a complete transformation of one's intellectual nature—purifying the mind from the distractions of sense, estimation, and imagination. The lesson is that spiritual discipline must be combined with intellectual purification, and that pride in one's spiritual efforts is misguided.

Key Terms
رياضة
غار
أنوار جلال الله
فطرة
تجويد العقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that strenuous spiritual discipline automatically guarantees gnosis.
Correction: The author clarifies that not everyone who practices attains something, and even those who attain do not reach the ultimate goal because it is infinite.

Checkpoint
What is the main purpose of the mountain analogy in this passage?
To prove that souls are equal in their capacity for divine illumination.
To warn that not every spiritual practitioner attains the ultimate goal due to the rarity and infinite nature of divine illumination.
To argue that God's majesty is limited.
To show that gold is the highest spiritual attainment.

Key Takeaway
Spiritual attainment is not guaranteed by effort alone; it requires rare disposition and complete intellectual purification, and even then the ultimate goal is infinite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself thinking that your consistent prayers or fasting automatically make you spiritually superior? The author says not everyone who strives attains. What would it take for you to genuinely examine whether your efforts are producing real inner transformation—or just external habit? Will you set aside time this week to honestly assess the gap between your actions and your heart's condition?
?
If God's majesty has no end, then every spiritual insight you have is still infinitely far from the whole truth. Does that humble you—or discourage you? The author says the goal is unattainable, yet we must keep seeking. How will you respond to the discomfort of never fully arriving? Can you commit to being a lifelong learner, even if you never feel you have reached the summit?
?
Aristotle's advice to acquire 'another nature' by purifying your intellect from sense, estimation, and imagination is radical. What is one specific attachment to your senses or imagination—like a desire, a fear, or a mental image—that you have allowed to cloud your understanding of God? What concrete step could you take this week to begin purifying your intellect from that attachment?

The Second Station: Emptying the Heart for God
The author describes the second station as emptying the heart of everything but God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that spiritual purification is not merely negative but leads to the manifestation of God's light in the soul.

Core Idea
Purification unveils the soul's innate love for God.

The second station of spiritual wayfaring is about emptying the heart of everything but God. However, the author emphasizes that this emptying is not just removal but leads to positive illumination and joy. Let's read his description.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the English translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the nature of the soul
Notice that the author describes the soul as naturally in love with God's majesty. This is its original disposition.
The soul's original nature is key to understanding the path.
3
Recognize the obstacles
The soul's attachment to the body and sensory pleasures prevents it from being drawn toward God. Identify these obstacles.
The author calls these 'accidents' (العوارض).
4
Understand the result of purification
When these obstacles are removed, the light of God's majesty appears in the soul. See how removal leads to positive manifestation.
The result is not just emptiness but fullness with divine light.
5
Reflect on the motivation
The author exhorts the seeker to strive for even a speck of this knowledge because it brings great pleasure and draws one closer to God.
The path is not just intellectual but experiential.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المقام الثاني : فهو أن حاصل هذا الطريق : إخلاء القلب عن ذكر غير الله . فإن جوهر النفس، كأنها بالجبلة الأصلية والغريزة القطرية عاشقة (1) على حضرة جلال الله، إلا أنها (لما) (۲) تعلقت بهذا البدن واشتغلت بهذه اللذات الجدانية والطيبات الخسيسة (صار) (۳) استغراقها في هذا الجانب، مانعا لها من الإنجذاب الى الوطن (٤) الأصلي، والمركز الذاتي، فإذا بالغ الإنسان في إزالة هذه العوارض، بقيت جوهرة النفس مع لوازمها الأصلية، وارتفع الغبار الحاجب، والغطاء المانع. فحينئذ يظهر فيه نور (۹) جلال الله . فليجتهد الانسان في هذا الباب بمقدار ما يحصل لـه الالتذاذ بالوقوف على ذرة من أنوار ذلك العلم، فإنه إذا حصلت تلك السعادة قويت اللذة وعظم الابتهاج، ويصير ذلك من أعظم الجواذب له إلى الانصراف إليه والإقبال عليه .
As for the second station: the essence of this path is emptying the heart of remembrance of other than God. For the substance of the soul, as if by original nature and innate disposition, is in love with the presence of God's majesty, except that when it became attached to this body and preoccupied with these sensory pleasures and base delights, its immersion in this side prevented it from being drawn towards the original homeland and the essential center. So when a person fully removes these accidents, the substance of the soul remains with its original necessities, and the dust that veils and the covering that hinders are lifted. Then the light of the majesty of God appears in it. Let a person strive in this matter to the extent that he attains the pleasure of standing upon a speck of the lights of that knowledge; for when that felicity is attained, pleasure is strengthened and joy is magnified, and that becomes one of the greatest attractions for him to turn towards it and to approach it.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage defines the second station of spiritual wayfaring: the essence of the path is to empty the heart from the remembrance of anything other than God. The author explains that the soul (nafs) is naturally in love with God's majesty by original constitution, but due to attachment to the body and engagement with sensory pleasures and base delights, it becomes immersed in this world and prevented from being drawn towards its original home (the divine presence). Once these accidental impediments are removed, the soul's essential attributes remain and the veils of dust are lifted. Then the light of God's majesty manifests within the soul. The author emphasizes that one should strive to attain even a particle of the pleasure of standing upon that divine knowledge, because that felicity will increase desire and draw one further toward God. The tone is exhortatory, encouraging effort in purification.

Key Terms
إخلاء القلب
جوهر النفس
الوطن الأصلي
نور جلال الله
العوارض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Spiritual purification is only about removing negative qualities.
Correction: The passage explains that emptying leads to positive illumination: the light of God's majesty appears in the soul, bringing joy and pleasure.

Checkpoint
What is the main obstacle that prevents the soul from being drawn to God according to the second station?
A) The soul's natural love for God.
B) Attachment to the body and sensory pleasures.
C) Lack of knowledge about God.
D) The dust of the earth.

Key Takeaway
Emptying the heart of everything but God unveils the soul's innate love and allows the light of God's majesty to appear, bringing profound joy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
The author says even 'a speck of the lights of that knowledge' strengthens joy. Have you ever tasted even a moment of nearness to God? If yes, why have you not pursued it more earnestly? If no, are you willing to start emptying your heart to find out?

The Danger of Spirituality Without Reason
Sanusi warns against spiritual discipline without rational theology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why rational theology is necessary to prevent spiritual experiences from becoming obstacles.

Core Idea
Spiritual experiences without reason can deceive you into thinking you've reached the end.

Sanusi introduces the third station and warns that spiritual practice without rational theology is dangerous. A practitioner may encounter powerful mystical states and mistakenly believe they are the ultimate goal, thus halting their progress. This lesson explores that pitfall.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Sanusi describes the scenario of a spiritual practitioner lacking rational inquiry.
Pay attention to the words 'mukashafat' (unveilings) and 'ghayat al-darajat' (ultimate degrees).
2
Identify the core warning
The author explains that without the method of nazar and istidlal, one may become certain that intermediate unveilings are the final goal. Identify the consequence: 'becomes an obstacle from reaching the sought goal.'
3
Reflect on the relevance
Consider why this warning is important. How can spiritual experiences be deceptive without a rational framework to evaluate them?
This connects to the broader theme of integrating reason and spirituality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المقام الثالث من المقامات المعتبرة في هذا الباب :
أن صاحب الرياضة إن كان خاليا عن طريق النظر والاستدلال، فربما لاحت له في درجات (٦) الرياضات مكاشفات قوية (واحوال) (۷) عاليـة قاهرة، يتيقن بها أنها أحوال نهايات المكاشفات وغايات الدرجات، ويصير ذلك عائقا له عن الوصول إلى المطلوب، أما إذا كان قد مارس طريقة النظر والاستدلال، وميز مقام ما يمتنع عن مقام ما لا يمتنع، كان آمنا من هذه المغالطة، ولو اتفق لإنسان كان كاملا في طريقة الاستدلال الفكري، ثم رزق الكمال في طريقة التصفية والرياضة، وكانت نفسه في مبدأ الفطرة، عظيمة
As for the third station among the stations considered in this matter: if the practitioner of spiritual discipline is devoid of the method of rational inquiry (nazar) and inference (istidlal), perhaps strong unveilings and high, overwhelming states may appear to him in the degrees of his disciplines, and he becomes certain that they are the final states of unveiling and the ultimate degrees, and that becomes an obstacle for him from reaching the sought goal. But if he has practiced the method of rational inquiry and inference, and distinguished the domain of what is impossible from the domain of what is not impossible, he is safe from this fallacy. Even if a person were perfect in the method of intellectual inference, and then was granted perfection in the method of purification (tasfiya) and discipline (riyada), and his soul was, from the beginning of its nature, great...
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage presents the third station: the necessity of combining rational theology (nazar and istidlal) with spiritual discipline. The author warns that if a spiritual practitioner lacks the method of rational reasoning, he may encounter powerful mystical unveilings and states during his disciplines, and mistakenly believe that these are the ultimate ends and highest degrees of spiritual attainment. This become a barrier to reaching the true goal (the sought-after knowledge of God). However, if he has first mastered rational reasoning and can distinguish metaphysical impossibility (mumtani') from possibility (la yamtani'), he is safe from such self-deception. The author then implies that the ideal is integration: one who is perfect in intellectual reasoning and then also granted perfection in purification and discipline, with a naturally great soul, is the most complete. The argument underscores that spiritual experiences alone are insufficient without sound theological reasoning; they can lead to error if the person cannot judge their true status.

Key Terms
مقام ثالث
رياضة
مكاشفات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Spiritual experiences are self-authenticating and sufficient for reaching the goal.
Correction: Without rational theology, one may mistake intermediate states for the final goal, becoming stuck.

Checkpoint
What is the danger of practising spiritual discipline without rational theology according to this passage?
A) One will have no spiritual experiences.
B) One may become arrogant.
C) One may mistake intermediate unveilings for the ultimate goal and become blocked.
D) One will lose all spiritual progress.

Key Takeaway
Spiritual discipline without rational theology can lead to deceptive certainty and halted progress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever been so overwhelmed by a spiritual experience that you stopped questioning whether it was truly from Allah or merely a deceptive intermediate stage? What will you do to guard against that deception starting today?
?
Think of a time you felt absolutely certain about a spiritual insight. How do you know that certainty was not a barrier to deeper knowledge? What step will you take this week to submit that feeling to rational scrutiny?
?
If you were to counsel a close friend who claims to have reached the 'final station' through spiritual experiences alone, what would you say? Are you willing to apply that same counsel to yourself?

The Safeguard of Reason and Ideal Integration
Sanusi explains how reason prevents deception and describes the ideal integration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how rational reasoning protects against spiritual deception and the value of combining both methods.

Core Idea
Reason discerns truth from illusion; the ideal is reason and spirituality integrated.

The Second Station: Purifying the Heart
The author explains the second stage of the spiritual path: purifying the heart.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning and purpose of the second station of the spiritual path: emptying the heart of everything except Allah.

Core Idea
The soul naturally loves God; purifying the heart removes the veil.

In this passage, the author describes the second stage of the spiritual journey: purifying the heart. He explains that the soul is inherently drawn to God, but attachment to the body and worldly pleasures obstructs this pull. By striving to remove these obstacles, the believer can experience the light of divine majesty. This lesson unpacks that process.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Pay attention to the author’s description of the soul’s nature and the goal of emptying the heart.
2
Identify the core teaching
What is the outcome of the second station? The passage states it is to empty the heart of remembrance of other than God. Notice how the author frames this as a return to the original home.
3
Reflect on the process
The passage describes how removing accidental attachments allows the soul’s original nature to shine. Consider what ‘accidents’ (عوارض) might be in your own life and how effort (الرياضة) plays a role.
This connects to the idea of spiritual struggle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المقام الثاني : فهو أن حاصل هذا الطريق : إخلاء القلب عن ذكر غير الله . فإن جوهر النفس، كأنها بالجبلة الأصلية والغريزة القطرية عاشقة على حضرة جلال الله، إلا أنها لما تعلقت بهذا البدن واشتغلت بهذه اللذات الجدانية والطيبات الخسيسة صار استغراقها في هذا الجانب، مانعا لها من الإنجذاب الى الوطن الأصلي، والمركز الذاتي، فإذا بالغ الإنسان في إزالة هذه العوارض، بقيت جوهرة النفس مع لوازمها الأصلية، وارتفع الغبار الحاجب، والغطاء المانع. فحينئذ يظهر فيه نور جلال الله . فليجتهد الانسان في هذا الباب بمقدار ما يحصل له الالتذاذ بالوقوف على ذرة من أنوار ذلك العلم، فإنه إذا حصلت تلك السعادة قويت اللذة وعظم الابتهاج، ويصير ذلك من أعظم الجواذب له إلى الانصراف إليه والإقبال عليه .
And as for the second station: its outcome is to empty the heart of the remembrance of other than God. For the essence of the soul, as if by original nature and innate disposition, is a lover of the presence of God's majesty, except that when it became attached to this body and occupied with these sensory pleasures and base delights, its absorption in this side became a preventer for it from being drawn to the original home and the essential center. So when man exerts himself in removing these accidents, the essence of the soul remains with its original concomitants, and the obstructing dust and the preventing veil are lifted. Then the light of God's majesty appears in it. So let man strive in this matter to the extent that he obtains the pleasure of stopping upon an atom of the lights of that knowledge. For when that happiness is obtained, the pleasure strengthens and joy increases, and that becomes among the greatest attractors for him to turn away from other things and turn towards it.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage defines the second stage of the spiritual path. The author explains that the goal is to empty the heart of everything except Allah. The soul is naturally inclined to love God, but due to attachment to the body and sensory pleasures, it becomes absorbed in this world, preventing it from returning to its original abode. By removing these accidental attachments through effort, the soul's essence remains with its original attributes, and the veils are lifted, allowing the light of divine majesty to shine. The author then emphasizes that one should strive in this practice to the extent of experiencing even a particle of that knowledge, as it becomes a powerful incentive to turn towards God.

Key Terms
إخلاء القلب
جوهر النفس
الرياضة
الوطن الأصلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This passage is about completely abandoning worldly responsibilities.
Correction: The passage advocates internal detachment, not abandonment of responsibilities. It speaks of emptying the heart of remembrance of other than God, not necessarily withdrawing from the world.

Checkpoint
What is the outcome of the second station according to the passage?
A) To engage in sensory pleasures
B) To empty the heart of remembrance of other than God
C) To attain the highest unveiling
D) To practice intellectual reasoning

Key Takeaway
The second station of the spiritual path is to empty the heart of everything except God, allowing the soul's innate love for Him to shine.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The passage says the soul is already a lover of God by nature, but attachments block that love. Reflect on a time when you felt a genuine longing for God but then got distracted. Which sensory pleasure or worldly delight pulled you away? Will you let it continue to veil you, or will you actively remove it starting now?
?
You are told that even an atom of the light of that knowledge brings immense pleasure and becomes a great attractor toward God. Are you sincerely striving to taste even a bit of that light, or are you content with the base pleasures of this life? What is one sacrifice you can make this week to prioritize the purification of your heart?

The Danger of Discipline Without Reasoning
Warning against discipline without reasoning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why spiritual discipline (riyada) without intellectual reasoning (nazar and istidlal) can lead to self-deception and obstruction from the ultimate goal.

Core Idea
Discipline without reasoning can trap you in false certainty.

This lesson addresses a critical methodological warning from the author. He explains that while spiritual discipline is valuable, it must be guided by sound reasoning. Without it, one may mistake intermediate spiritual experiences for final achievement, thereby blocking further progress. The ideal path combines both rigorous reasoning and disciplined purification.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let the author's warning and contrast come through.
Notice the three scenarios: without reasoning, with reasoning, and the ideal combination.
2
Identify the two types of practitioners
The passage describes a practitioner devoid of reasoning and one who has practiced reasoning. Compare their outcomes.
3
Recognize the fallacy described
The practitioner without reasoning may experience strong unveilings and mistakenly believe they are the final goal. Understand why this is a fallacy.
The fallacy is mistaking intermediate states for ultimate attainment.
4
Understand the ideal combination
The passage ends by describing a person perfect in both reasoning and spiritual discipline. See how this leads to the ultimate goal.
This shows that the author does not reject discipline but insists on pairing it with reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المقام الثالث من المقامات المعتبرة في هذا الباب : أن صاحب الرياضة إن كان خاليا عن طريق النظر والاستدلال، فربما لاحت له في درجات الرياضات مكاشفات قوية وأحوال عالية قاهرة، يتيقن بها أنها أحوال نهايات المكاشفات وغايات الدرجات، ويصير ذلك عائقا له عن الوصول إلى المطلوب، أما إذا كان قد مارس طريقة النظر والاستدلال، وميز مقام ما يمتنع عن مقام ما لا يمتنع، كان آمنا من هذه المغالطة، ولو اتفق لإنسان كان كاملا في طريقة الاستدلال الفكري، ثم رزق الكمال في طريقة التصفية والرياضة، وكانت نفسه في مبدأ الفطرة، عظيمة المناسبة لهذه الأحوال كان ذلك الإنسان واصلا في هذه المدارج والمعارج إلى أقصى الغايات
And as for the third station among the stations considered in this matter: that the practitioner of spiritual discipline (riyada), if he is devoid of the path of theoretical reasoning (nazar) and inferential proof (istidlal), perhaps there may appear to him in the degrees of spiritual disciplines strong unveilings (mukashafat) and powerful high states, by which he becomes certain that they are states of the ends of unveilings and the final ranks, and that becomes an obstacle for him from reaching the sought goal. But if he has practiced the method of theoretical reasoning and inferential proof, and distinguished the station of what is impossible from the station of what is not impossible, he becomes safe from this fallacy. And if it happens for a person that he is perfect in the method of intellectual inference, then is granted perfection in the method of purification and spiritual discipline, and his soul is, from the beginning of his nature, greatly suited for these states, then that person will reach in these stages and ascents the ultimate goals.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage warns against practicing spiritual discipline without intellectual reasoning. The author describes the third station: a person who engages in riyada without nazar and istidlal may experience powerful unveilings and mistakenly believe they have reached the final goal, thus becoming obstructed. In contrast, one who has trained in reasoning can distinguish between what is possible and impossible, thus avoiding this fallacy. The author then posits an ideal person who is perfect in both intellectual reasoning and spiritual purification, with a natural disposition, such a person will reach the ultimate ends.

The Ineffability of Spiritual States
The author quotes Aristotle and comments on the ineffability of spiritual states.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why spiritual states cannot be fully described in words and require direct experiential knowledge.

Core Idea
Spiritual knowledge is tasted, not told.

This lesson presents a profound quote from Aristotle, which the author uses to illustrate the difference between ordinary intellectual pursuit and the quenching satisfaction of divine knowledge. The passage then asserts that such spiritual experiences elude verbal description, known only to those who have tasted or witnessed them.

Guided Path
1
Read the quote from Aristotle
Read the Arabic and English of Aristotle's statement. Notice the metaphor of drinking and quenching.
Contrast his earlier unsatisfied drinking with the complete satisfaction after drinking from the 'sea'.
2
Understand the author's comment
The author explains that these spiritual states cannot be explained by discourse or reached by speech. He emphasizes that only those who have tasted or witnessed can truly know.
This affirms the limits of rational discourse in capturing direct spiritual experience.
3

Consider the terms 'ذوق' (tasting) and 'مشاهدة' (witnessing). How do they relate to the idea of experiential knowledge?
These terms are central to Sufi epistemology.
4
Answer the checkpoint question
Test your understanding by answering the MCQ based solely on this passage.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونقل عن أرسطاطاليس، أنه قال : وكنت أشرب فلا أروى. فلما شربت من هذا البحر ، رويت ريا لا ظمأ بعده . وهذه الأحوال لا يشرحها المقال، ولا يصل إليها الكلام، ومن لم يذق لم يعرف ، ومن لم يشاهد لم بصدق . والله أعلم بالمغيبات

And these states are not explained by discourse, nor does speech reach them. He who has not tasted does not know, and he who has not witnessed does not believe. And God knows best the unseen things.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage provides a quotation from Aristotle and a comment. Aristotle says he used to drink (seek knowledge) without satisfaction, until he drank from this sea (divine knowledge) and was fully satisfied. The author then notes that such spiritual states cannot be fully described in words; only those who have tasted or witnessed can understand. God knows the unseen.

Introduction to the Three Knots of This Science
Author introduces three states and the three knots for the past.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the study of divine matters begins with three 'immense knots' corresponding to knowledge of God's Essence, Attributes, and Actions.

Core Idea
Knowing the past requires untying three immense knots: God, His attributes, and His actions.

The author begins by framing human inquiry into three temporal states: past, present, and future. He then focuses on the past, explaining that understanding it is impossible without first grasping three fundamental truths about God. These three truths are described as 'immense knots' that this science aims to untie. Let us explore the passage and see how the author sets up the entire theological project.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice how the author introduces the three states and then zooms in on the past.
The term 'عقدة هائلة' is key—think of it as a difficult problem to solve.
2
Identify the three states of man
The passage says man has three states: past, present, and future. Why do you think the author lists all three before focusing on the past?
This sets the scope of inquiry.
3
Extract the three premises for knowing the past
The author says knowing the past requires three premises: knowing the First Principle, knowing His attributes, and knowing how states emanated from Him. These correspond to the three knots.
These three premises are the core of the science.
4
Reflect on the metaphor of 'knots'
Why does the author call each premise an 'immense knot'? This suggests difficulty and the need for careful untangling.
This metaphor appears throughout the text.
5
Connect to the broader science
The rest of the work will address each knot in detail. This passage gives you the map of the entire discussion.
Keep this structure in mind.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ضبط معاقد هذا العلم أعلم ان الإنسان له أحوال ثلاثة : الماضي والحاضر والمستقبل. أما الماضي : فهو يريد أن يعرف أن هذه الأحوال كيف كانت في الماضي؟ وذلك لا يحصل إلا بأن يعرف المبدأ الأول ويعرف صفاته، ويعرف أنه كيف صدر عنه هذه الأحوال؟ فهذه مقدمات ثلاث وعلى طريق كل واحد منها عقدة هائلة.
The Controlling of the Knots of This Science. Know that man has three states: the past, the present, and the future. As for the past: he wants to know how these states were in the past. That cannot be attained except by knowing the First Principle, and knowing His attributes, and knowing how these states emanated from Him. So these are three premises, and on the path of each one of them is an immense knot.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage introduces the structure of the author's discussion on the difficulties of theology. It is a classification passage. The author states that human inquiry concerns three temporal states: past, present, and future. For the past, one must understand the First Principle (God), His attributes, and how all past states originated from Him. These three requirements—knowledge of the Essence, Attributes, and Actions—are described as three 'immense knots' (عقدة هائلة) that must be untied. The author will then elaborate each knot.

Correction: The author argues that the past can only be understood through metaphysical knowledge of God, His attributes, and His creative action.

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Does your daily gratitude or complaint reflect that belief? What would you have to change in your heart to truly live as though God's actions are the source of every past moment?

The Threefold Division of Theology: Past, Present, Future
Author introduces the threefold theological inquiry and explains the purpose of earlier hints.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three areas of theological inquiry—past, present, and future—and how the author uses hints as a teaching method before full treatment.

Core Idea
Theology is divided into knowledge of the past, present, and future.

This passage serves as a methodological roadmap for the entire book. The author clarifies that the previous problems were only brief hints (tanbīh), not exhaustive treatments, and then he outlines the three domains of inquiry that every theologian must address: knowledge of the past (God and His actions), present (what beliefs and deeds lead to felicity), and future (the afterlife). This structure grounds the traditional scope of kalām.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage slowly, then its English translation. Notice how the author first states his purpose for the problems mentioned earlier, then transitions into the threefold inquiry.
The author uses the phrase 'So this is the indication of knowledge of the past' to refer back to preceding material.
2
Identify the three inquiries
Locate the three distinct areas: past (ma‘rifat al-māḍī), present (aḥwāl ḥāḍira), and future (mustaqbal). For each, note what specific knowledge is sought.
For the present, it is about beliefs and deeds leading to happiness or misery. For the future, it includes the nature of the return (spiritual, physical, or both) and states of felicity and misery.
3
Reflect on the teaching method
Observe that the author says the purpose of the problems is 'drawing attention' (tanbīh), not full exposition. Consider why a teacher might first give hints before detailed explanations.
This method encourages students to anticipate and engage with material before receiving complete answers.
4
Analyze the subdivisions of future inquiry
The author breaks down the future inquiry into two parts: the certainty of a return (ma‘ād) and the possible forms (purely spiritual, purely physical, or both). Also, one must know the states of happiness and misery in that return.
This shows that eschatology is not monolithic; it involves multiple possibilities that need examination.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن هذه الإشكالات التي نذكرها ههنا الغرض منها: التنبيه على هذه الإشكالات وأما تقرير كل واحد منها فسيجيء على سبيل التمام والكمال في موضعه من هذا الكتاب إن شاء الله تعالى، فهذا هو الإشارة إلى معرفة الماضي. وأما البحث عن الأحوال الحاضرة : فهو أن الإنسان محتاج إلى أن يعرف : أي الاعتقادات وأي الأعمال يسوقه إلى الفوز بالسعادة الكبرى والدرجة العظمى، وأي الاعتقادات والأعمال بالضد من ذلك؟ وأما البحث عن المستقبل : فهو أن يعرف أن له معادا. ثم ذلك المعاد يحتمل أن يكون روحانيا فقط أو أن يكون جسمانيا فقط أو أن يحصل القسمان معا . وأن يعرف أحوال سعادته وشقاوته في ذلك المعاد .
And know that these problems which we mention here have as their purpose: drawing attention to these problems. As for the full exposition of each one, it will come in its proper place in this book, God willing. So this is the indication of knowledge of the past. As for inquiry into present states: it is that man needs to know which beliefs and which deeds lead him to attaining the greatest happiness and the highest rank, and which beliefs and deeds are opposite to that. As for inquiry into the future: it is that he knows that he has a return. Then that return may be purely spiritual, or purely physical, or both together. And he needs to know the states of his happiness and misery in that return.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage serves as a transitional note from the problems just raised to the overall structure of theology. The author clarifies that the problems mentioned are only hints (tanbih), not full treatments. Full exposition will come later in the book. Then he categorizes theological knowledge into three parts: knowledge of the past (ma‘rifat al-madi) - referring to the previous discussions about God's attributes and actions; knowledge of the present - which is soteriological: knowing what beliefs and actions lead to felicity and what lead to misery; and knowledge of the future - eschatology: knowledge of the afterlife, whether it is purely spiritual, purely physical, or both, and the states of happiness and misery therein. This outlines the scope of kalam.

Key Terms

تنبيه
معرفة الماضي
أحوال حاضرة
معاد
سعادة
شقاوة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This passage is a digression or unnecessary aside from the main argument.
Correction: The author is deliberately outlining the scope of the entire discipline: theology covers the past (God), present (human action and belief), and future (afterlife). The 'hints' are a pedagogical device, not a digression.

Checkpoint

C) Knowledge of the afterlife
D) Knowledge of the world's creation

Key Takeaway
The author organizes theology into three domains: past (God), present (soteriology), and future (eschatology), with the initial problems serving only as hints for deeper study.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Three Domains of Theological Inquiry
The author divides theological inquiry into past, present, and future domains.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the science of divinity is organized into three temporal domains: past, present, and future, and what each domain investigates.

Core Idea
Theology guides our present actions and future hope, not just past beliefs.

After establishing that theology must consider the past (creation and divine attributes), the author turns to the other two necessary dimensions: the present moment of human choice and the future of the afterlife. This passage shows how the divine science (العلم الإلهي) is comprehensive, covering not only what happened but what we must do and what awaits us.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage aloud twice: once for rhythm and once for meaning. Then read the English translation to confirm comprehension.
Notice the threefold division: past (previous section), present, and future.
2
Identify the present domain
The author says man needs to know which beliefs and deeds lead to the greatest happiness and which lead to the opposite. Reflect on why this is part of divine science.
This shows theology is practical, not just theoretical.
3
Examine the future domain
The future domain includes the certainty of a return (معاد), its possible nature (spiritual, physical, or both), and the states of happiness/misery. Note how eschatology is integral.
The word 'معاد' is central to Islamic eschatology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما البحث عن الأحوال الحاضرة : فهو أن الإنسان محتاج إلى أن يعرف : أي الاعتقادات وأي الأعمال يسوقه إلى الفوز بالسعادة الكبرى والدرجة العظمى، وأي الاعتقادات والأعمال بالضد من ذلك؟ وأما البحث عن المستقبل : فهو أن يعرف أن له معادا. ثم ذلك المعاد يحتمل أن يكون روحانيا فقط أو أن يكون جسمانيا فقط) (٤) أو أن يحصل القسمان معا . وأن يعرف أحوال سعادته وشقاوته في ذلك المعاد . فهذا ضبط أبواب العلم الإلهي
As for investigation of present matters: it is that man needs to know which beliefs and which deeds lead him to attaining the greatest happiness and the highest rank, and which beliefs and deeds are the opposite of that. As for investigation of the future: it is that he know that he has a return (معاد). Then that return is likely to be spiritual only, or physical only, or that both categories occur together. And that he know the states of his happiness and misery in that return. So this is the regulation of the chapters of the divine science (العلم الإلهي).
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage defines three domains of inquiry within the divine science (العلم الإلهي) after the previous section on the past. The author first discusses present matters: man needs to know which beliefs and actions lead to ultimate happiness and which lead to the opposite. This is essentially the ethical and soteriological dimension of theology. Then he discusses future matters: man must know that there is a return (معاد) or afterlife. This return may be purely spiritual, purely physical, or both. Man must also know the states of happiness and misery in that afterlife. The passage concludes by stating that this (including the previous discussion of the past) constitutes the proper regulation of the chapters of the divine science.

Key Takeaway

Core Idea
The introduction outlines ten sections; the last five cover angels, space/time, prophethood, ethics, and resurrection.

This passage lists the remaining sections of the introduction, providing a roadmap for the entire work. It also contains a textual note indicating that the author died before writing the final two sections, giving insight into the work's incomplete state.

Guided Path
1

2

This marks the formal close of the introduction.
4

والقسم السادس: الكلام في مراتب الأرواح المقدسة وبيان درجات الملائكة الروحانيين والكروبيين على اختلاف درجاتها .

والقسم الثامن : الكلام في النبوة وشرح حقيقتها . واختلاف مذاهب الناس فيها . وتقرير ما في كل واحد من تلك المذاهب من الوجوه المقوية والوجوه الطاعنة
(۱)
والقسم التاسع: (الكلام) في كيفية اكتساب الصفات الفاضلة التي معها تصير النفس من جملة السعداء الأبرار، لا من زمرة الأشقياء الفجار.
والقسم العاشر : الكلام في المعاد الروحاني والجسماني. وشرح صـفـات
كل واحد من هذين القسمين .
وههنا آخر الكلام في العلم الإلهي .
ونسأل الله المعونة والتوفيق في الوصول إلى هذه المطالب العالية، والمقاصد
المقدسة بحسب القوة البشرية، والطاقة الإنسانية.
وهذا تمام الكلام في المقدمة .
(۱) من (ز) واعلم : أن القسم التاسع هو في علم الأخلاق . والقسم العاشر هو في المعاد الروحاني والجسماني . والمؤلف انتقل إلى رحمة الله تعالى من قبل أن يكتب في الأخلاق وفي المعاد
And it includes: an exposition of whether the corporeal world is one or not.
And the sixth section: discourse on the ranks of the holy spirits and an exposition of the degrees of the spiritual and cherubic angels according to their different degrees.
And the seventh section: discourse on the reality of space and time and a detailed discussion of them.
And the eighth section: discourse on prophethood and an explanation of its reality, and the difference of people's doctrines concerning it, and establishing what in each of those doctrines are strengthening aspects and critical aspects.
(1)
And the ninth section: discourse on how to acquire virtuous attributes through which the soul becomes among the company of the righteous happy ones, not among the band of the wicked wretched ones.
And the tenth section: discourse on the spiritual and physical resurrection, and a description of the attributes of each of these two divisions.
And here is the end of the discourse on the divine science.
And we ask God for help and success in attaining these lofty pursuits and sacred objectives according to human capacity and human energy.
And this is the end of the discourse on the introduction.
(1) From (z): And know that the ninth section is on ethics, and the tenth section is on the spiritual and physical resurrection. And the author passed on to the mercy of God Almighty before writing on ethics and on resurrection.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration and conclusion. The author lists the final sections (6–10) of the introduction to his work, then concludes the introduction. The sixth section deals with the ranks of holy spirits and the degrees of angels. The seventh discusses the reality of space and time. The eighth covers prophethood, including different doctrines and their strengths and weaknesses. The ninth, on acquiring virtuous attributes (ethics), and the tenth, on spiritual and physical resurrection, are noted in a marginal remark (from manuscript 'z') to have been left unwritten because the author died before completing them. The author then declares this the end of the discourse on divine science (al-ilm al-ilahi) and of the introduction, and prays for divine assistance.

Introduction to the Proofs for God and the Necessary Existent
Opening sentence announcing the topic of the first part

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main objectives of the first part of the book: proving that there is a God for this perceptible world and proving that this God is the Necessary Existent by virtue of His essence.

Core Idea
The first part aims to prove God's existence and His necessary, self-subsisting nature.

This lesson introduces the opening statement of the first part. It sets the agenda for the entire section by declaring two key goals: to establish that the perceptible world has a god, and to prove that this god is the Necessary Existent — whose existence is inherent and not dependent on anything else.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation

The sentence is short but packed with two key points.
2
Break down the two objectives

3
Reflect on 'li-dhatihi'

This phrase is foundational for the concept of wajib al-wujud.

The Book's Three Sections
The author states the three-part division of the book.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three-part structure of the book and the different types of proofs and topics each section covers.

Core Idea
The book is a map: certain proofs, persuasive proofs, then deep metaphysics.

Before diving into the arguments, the author first gives us a roadmap. This short passage tells us exactly what to expect: three sections that move from decisive proof, through persuasive argument, to the most fundamental metaphysical concepts. It's a glimpse into the author's method.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation

The passage includes a textual footnote about an original variant reading 'two sections'.
2
Identify the three sections

The third section covers a range of metaphysical topics.
3
Understand the classification

The third section seems to be a separate, more advanced discussion.
4
Consider the footnote
The original manuscript had 'two sections' – note that this might cause confusion about the book's structure.

(2) This section is mentioned after the third chapter of the second section.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the book's structure. It begins with a call to attention ('Know that') and an ascription to 'Shahid', likely the author's title. The author states that the book is divided into three sections. The first section deals with decisive certain proofs (ألدلائل القطعية اليقينية), i.e., demonstrative arguments that yield certainty. The second section deals with strong persuasive proofs (ألدلائل الإقناعية القوية), which are dialectical or rhetorical arguments that are convincing but not necessarily certain. The third section covers metaphysical topics such as obligation (الوجوب), existence (الوجود), wills (الإرادات), determination (التعين), quiddity (الماهية), and similar inquiries. The footnotes (1) and (2) are textual notes: (1) indicates that in the original manuscript it read 'two sections' instead of three, and (2) notes that the third section appears after the third chapter of the second section, providing a cross-reference.

The Three Divisions of the Book
The author enumerates the three divisions of his book with footnotes on textual variants.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the overall structure of the author's book as three distinct divisions and the types of arguments each contains.

Core Idea
The book is built on three tiers of argument: certain, persuasive, and metaphysical.

Before diving into the detailed arguments, the author first gives us a map of the entire work. This passage is the methodological key: it tells us that the book proceeds from certain proofs to persuasive ones, and then to deeper metaphysical questions. Understanding this structure helps you follow the author's pedagogical logic.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the three divisions
Read the Arabic and translation once. Then list the three divisions as stated by the author. Notice that the first two are about types of proofs, while the third covers metaphysical topics.
The footnotes indicate a variant reading; you may ignore them for now.
2
Compare the first two divisions
The first division uses 'قطعية' (certain) and 'يقينية' (definitive). The second uses 'إقناعية' (persuasive) and 'قوية' (strong). Ask yourself: what is the practical difference between a certain proof and a persuasive one?
Certain proofs yield absolute certainty; persuasive proofs yield strong probability.
3
Examine the third division
The third division covers topics like necessity, existence, volitions, individuation, and quiddity. These are core metaphysical concepts. Why do you think the author places these after the two types of proofs?
This suggests that metaphysical discussion depends on first establishing certain and persuasive arguments.
4
Consider the footnote about textual variants
The footnote says the original had 'two divisions' and that the third division is actually placed after a chapter of the second. This shows that the book's structure was refined over time. How does this affect your understanding of the book's layout?
It means the third division may not be a separate main part but an appendix to the second.

The footnotes provide textual variants: the original manuscript had 'two divisions' instead of three, and the third division is actually located after the third chapter of the second division, not as a separate section.
