The Six Ways to Prove a Necessary Being
Sanusi enumerates the six rational proofs for a necessary being.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the six rational proofs for the existence of a necessary being are derived from the sources of need (imkan and huduth) applied to essences and attributes.

Core Idea
Six proofs arise from three sources of need in two subjects.

This passage presents the systematic classification of rational proofs for God's existence in the Ash'ari tradition. The author explains that all proofs rely on the principle of causality: observed things are in need of a cause. He then analyzes the nature of that need into three grounds and applies them to two aspects of things, yielding six distinct arguments.

Guided Path
1
Understand the problem
Read the opening lines to grasp what the author is trying to prove: a being beyond sensory perception. Note that the only way is through reason judging that perceived things are in need.
This sets the stage for the entire classification.
2
Identify the sources of need
The author states that need arises from possibility (imkan), origination (huduth), or both. Understand each term: imkan means the thing could have not existed; huduth means it came into existence after non-existence.
3
Note the subjects of need
The need can be considered in essences (dhat) or attributes (sifat). Essence refers to the thing itself; attributes are its qualities.
This doubles the number of possible proofs.
4
Multiply to get six ways
Combine the three sources with the two subjects to derive six distinct proofs. The author lists them explicitly.
Memorize the six: (1) imkan of essence, (2) imkan of attribute, (3) huduth of essence, (4) huduth of attribute, (5) both in essence, (6) both in attribute.
5
Conclude the lesson
The author states that these six are the complete set of rational proofs for a necessary being. Reflect on how this classification covers all possibilities.
This shows the methodical nature of kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بسم الله الرحمن الرحيم
المقدمة
بيان ما قد ضبط هذا الباب
اعلم أنا إذا أردنا إثبات موجود لا تحكم بوجوده حواسنا، ولا تحكم بوجوده أيضاً فطرة (1) نفوسنا وعقولنا، فهذا مما لا سبيل إليه إلا بطريق واحد، وهو أن يحكم عقلنا الصريح بأن هذه الموجودات التي نحكم بوجودها بحسب حواسنا وعقولنا، محتاجة إما في وجودها، أو في وجود صفة من صفاتها إلى وجود موجود غائب عن حواسنا وأوهامنا، وبهذا الطريق يتمكن العقل من إثبات ذلك الموجود الغائب. إذا عرفت هذا فنقول: منشأ الحاجة إما الإمكان، وإما الحدوث، وإما مجموعهما، فهذه أحوال ثلاثة وهي إما أن تعتبر في الذوات أو في الصفات، فللمجموع طرق ستة:
أولها: إمكان الذوات.
وثانيها: إمكان الصفات.
وثالثها: حدوث الذوات.
ورابعها: حدوث الصفات.
وخامسها: مجموع الإمكان والحدوث في الذوات.
وسادسها: مجموع الإمكان والحدوث في الصفات.
فهذه هي الطرق التي يمكن الاستدلال بها على إثبات موجود واجب الوجود لذاته، وهذا تمام الكلام في هذه المقدمة.
In the name of God, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful.
Introduction
Clarification of what this chapter encompasses.
Know that when we want to prove a being whose existence our senses do not judge, nor does the innate disposition (fiṭra) of our souls and intellects judge its existence, then there is no way to it except one way: that our sound reason judges that these existing things whose existence we judge by our senses and intellects are in need—either in their existence or in the existence of an attribute of their attributes—of the existence of a being absent from our senses and imaginations. And by this way the intellect is able to prove that absent being. If you have understood this, we say: The source of need is either possibility (imkān), or origination (ḥudūth), or both of them. These are three states, and they are either considered in essences (dhāt) or in attributes (ṣifa). So for the whole there are six ways:
First: possibility of essences.
Second: possibility of attributes.
Third: origination of essences.
Fourth: origination of attributes.
Fifth: the combination of possibility and origination in essences.
Sixth: the combination of possibility and origination in attributes.
These are the ways by which one can infer to prove a being necessary of existence (wājib al-wujūd) in itself. And this completes the discourse in this introduction.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage is a methodological introduction that classifies the rational proofs for the existence of a being not perceived by the senses or intuition. The author begins by stating the problem: when we want to prove a being that is not directly perceived, the only means is reason's judgment that the objects we do perceive are in need—either in their existence or in their attributes—of a cause that is beyond sensory and imaginative perception. This establishes the principle of causality. Then the author analyzes the nature of need: it arises from three possible grounds: possibility (imkān), meaning the thing could have not existed; origination (ḥudūth), meaning it came into existence after not existing; or both. These grounds can be considered in two subjects: the essences (dhāt) of things or their attributes (ṣifa). Multiplying the three grounds by the two subjects yields six distinct arguments: (1) possibility of the essence, (2) possibility of the attribute, (3) origination of the essence, (4) origination of the attribute, (5) combination of possibility and origination in the essence, and (6) combination of possibility and origination in the attribute. The author concludes that these six paths are the complete set of rational proofs for establishing a necessary existent (wājib al-wujūd). The passage is systematic and exhaustive, showing the author's methodical approach to kalam cosmology.

Key Terms
موجود
إمكان
حدوث
ذات
صفة
واجب الوجود
حاجة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the need for a cause applies only to the existence of things, not to their attributes.
Correction: The author explicitly states that need can be in the existence of an attribute itself, not just the essence. This doubles the number of proofs.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how many distinct ways are there to prove the existence of a necessary being?
A) Three ways
B) Four ways
C) Six ways
D) Nine ways

Key Takeaway
There are six rational proofs for a necessary being, derived from three sources of need (imkan, huduth, both) applied to two subjects (essences, attributes).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
After reading this lesson, can you honestly say that you rely more on rational arguments for God's existence or on blind faith? What one change can you make today to strengthen your intellectual conviction in the necessary being?
?
Your own existence and every attribute you possess are needy upon Allah. When was the last time you truly felt this dependence in your heart? Write down one concrete way you can live out this dependency tomorrow.
?
Imagine you are asked to present the six ways to a skeptic. Which of the six resonates most with you personally, and why? Are you willing to study it deeply until you can explain it clearly?

Ibn Sina's Inconsistency on the Premise
Critiques Ibn Sina's contradictory statements on the premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the inconsistency in Ibn Sina's approach to proving the premise that a contingent requires a cause.

Core Idea
Ibn Sina wavers between claiming a proof and appealing to self-evidence.

This lesson examines a sharp critique of Ibn Sina's methodology. The author points out that whenever Ibn Sina discusses the crucial premise of the contingency argument, he first claims to have a demonstrative proof, but when pressed, he falls back on self-evidence. This contradiction reveals a fundamental weakness in his philosophical approach.

Guided Path
1
Read the critique carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the author's charge of contradiction. Notice the shift from 'istidlālī' (inferential) to 'badīhī' (self-evident).
2
Identify the two claims
The author says Ibn Sina first claims the premise is established by proof, then when cornered, claims it is self-evident. Identify these two distinct phases in the text.
This inconsistency is the core of the critique.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this inconsistency is a problem for Ibn Sina's overall argument. If the premise is truly self-evident, why attempt a proof? If it requires proof, why fall back on self-evidence?
This prepares you for the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثالث
أعلم أن الشيخ الرئيس أبا علي ابن سينا مضطرب القول في هذا الموضوع ، وذلك لأنه أينما يذكر هذه المقدمة في كتبه الطويلة والمختصرة فإنه يدعي إقامة البرهان على صحتها ، ثم إنه يذكر تقسيمات طويلة خارجة عن المقصود ، ثم إذا انتهى إلى هذا القسم الذي هو المطلوب والمقصود فإنه يدعي فيه البديهة . وكل من طالع كتبه بالتأمل التام عرف أن الأمر كما ذكرناه . فهو في أول الأمر يدعي كونها استدلالية ، وفي آخر الأمر عند ضيق الكلام عليه يدعي كونها بديهية ، ورأيت أبا الحسين محمد بن علي البصري - وهو كان من أذكياء المعتزلة - احتج على صحة هذه المقدمة في الكتاب الذي سماه بالتصفح فقال : (الممكن هو الذي استوى طرفاه فلو حصل الرجحان من غير مرجع ، لزم أن يحصل الرجحان حال حصول الأستواء وذلك جمع بين النقيضين وهو محال ، ولقائل أن يقول : هذا التناقض غير لازم ، وذلك لأن الممكن هو الذي تكون ماهيته غير مقتضية لرجحان أحد الطرفين على الآخر ونقيض القضية أن يقال : إن تلك الماهية مقتضية لرجحان أحمد الطرفين على الآخر فأما أن يقال : إن حقيقته لا تقتضي الرجحان ، ثم إنه حصل الرجحان لا لذاته ولا لغيره ، فعلى هذا التقدير لا يلزم التناقض )
Chapter Three
Know that the chief master Abu Ali Ibn Sina has contradictory statements on this topic. This is because wherever he mentions this premise in his lengthy and concise books, he claims to have established a proof for its validity, then he gives lengthy divisions beyond the purpose, then when he reaches this part which is the desired and intended, he claims self-evidence for it. Anyone who studies his books with complete reflection will know that the matter is as we have described. So at first he claims it is inferential (istidlāliyya), and at the end when he is pressed, he claims it is self-evident. And I saw Abu al-Husayn Muhammad ibn Ali al-Basri – and he was one of the intelligent Mu'tazila – arguing for the validity of this premise in the book he called al-Tasaffuḥ. He said: 'The possible (al-mumkin) is that whose two sides are equal; so if preponderance (rujḥān) occurs without a referent, then preponderance would occur while equality exists, and that is a conjunction of two contradictories, which is impossible.' And one may say: 'This contradiction is not necessary, because the possible is that whose essence does not necessitate the preponderance of one side over the other. The contradictory of the proposition would be to say that that essence necessitates the preponderance of one side over the other. But as for saying that its reality does not necessitate preponderance, then preponderance occurs not from itself nor from another – on this supposition, the contradiction does not follow.'
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage begins a new chapter ( الفصل الثالث ) with a critical observation about Ibn Sina's inconsistency. The author notes that wherever Ibn Sina discusses the premise that a contingent needs a cause, he first claims to have a proof (burhān) for it, then digresses, but when he gets to the crucial point he falls back on claiming it is self-evident. Thus he contradicts himself between claiming it is inferential (istidlālī) and self-evident (badīhī). Then the author presents an argument from Abu al-Husayn al-Basri, a leading Mu'tazilite. Al-Basri argues that the possible (al-mumkin) is defined as having two equal sides; if preponderance (rujḥān) were to occur without any cause (without a 'referent' – marji‘), that would mean preponderance arises while equality still holds, which is a contradiction. Finally, an objection is raised against al-Basri: the supposed contradiction does not necessarily follow, because the possible's essence does not require preponderance; one could say that preponderance occurs from no cause at all without implying that the essence necessitates the opposite. On that interpretation, the contradiction is avoided. This objection is presented without an answer in this passage, leaving the argument open.

Key Terms
مضطرب
ابن سينا
البرهان
البديهة
استدلالية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the author criticize about Ibn Sina's treatment of the premise?
A) He never mentions it in his books.
B) He first claims proof then appeals to self-evidence when pressed.
C) He relies only on Mu'tazilite arguments.
D) He denies the premise entirely.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina's inconsistency between claiming proof and claiming self-evidence for the same premise reveals a methodological flaw.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself in a debate about a belief you hold deeply, do you ever shift from trying to prove it logically to simply asserting it as obvious? Can you recall a specific instance where you did that? How will you guard against that inconsistency now?
?
Ibn Sina, a towering intellect, fell into a contradiction between proof and self-evidence. What does that say about your own intellectual honesty? Are there areas in your faith or worldview where you claim certainty but have never examined the grounds? What will you do this week to test one such claim?
?
The author says 'anyone who studies his books with complete reflection will know' the inconsistency. When was the last time you studied a text with complete reflection, not just to confirm what you already think, but to expose flaws? Will you commit to reading one challenging text this month with that level of scrutiny?

Al-Basri's Argument and the Objection
Presents al-Basri's argument for the premise and an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Basri's argument that a possible cannot become preponderant without a cause, and the objection that this does not necessarily entail a contradiction.

Core Idea
Al-Basri argues preponderance without a cause is contradictory; the objection challenges that necessity.

In this lesson, we explore a Mu'tazilite argument for the necessity of a cause. Al-Basri defines the possible as having equal sides, then argues that preponderance without a cause would violate that equality. An objection is then raised questioning whether the contradiction necessarily follows.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of the possible
Al-Basri defines the possible (al-mumkin) as that whose two sides are equal ('istawā ṭarafāhu). This means there is no inherent preference for existence or non-existence.
This definition is crucial for the argument.
2
Follow al-Basri's argument
He argues that if preponderance (rujḥān) were to arise without any cause (marji‘), then preponderance would occur while equality still holds, which is a contradiction (collecting two opposites).
The contradiction is that the state of equality and the state of preponderance cannot coexist.
3
Parse the objection
The objector says the contradiction is not necessary. The possible's essence does not necessitate preponderance of either side, but it is not contradictory for preponderance to occur without a cause—unless the essence were to necessitate the opposite.
The objector distinguishes between 'not necessitating' and 'necessitating the opposite'.

Why Refuting Infinite Regress is Necessary
Author explains why refutation of infinite regress was needed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author needed to refute infinite regress in the previous chapter, based on the assumption that a possible being can be the cause of another possible being.

Core Idea
If possibles can cause other possibles, infinite regress must be refuted.

In the previous chapter, the author argued against infinite regress. But why was that argument necessary? Here he reveals the key assumption: that one possible being can cause another possible being. Without that assumption, there would be no regress to worry about. This lesson unpacks that logical connection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the phrase 'because we allowed that the cause of existence of a possible could be another possible.' This is the crucial point.
Focus on the causal link between possibles.
2
Identify the key assumption
The author states that in the previous chapter, they assumed that a possible being can be the cause of another possible being. This assumption is what made the refutation of infinite regress necessary.
3
Connect the assumption to the need for refutation
If one possible can cause another, you can have a chain of possibilities going back infinitely. To avoid an infinite regress, you must show that such a chain cannot occur. That is why the previous chapter's refutation was needed.
If possibles could not cause each other, the problem of infinite regress would not arise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الحادي عشر في إبطال التسلسل سوى ما تقرر ذكره اعلم أنا في الفصل المتقدم إنما افتقرنا إلى إبطال التسلسل ، لأنا جوزنا أن تكون علة وجود الممكن ممكنا آخر ، فلما جوزنا هذا في الجملة افتقرنا إلى إبطال التسلسل ، ومن الناس من يقول : إن ما كان ممكن الوجود لذاته ، فإنه لا يصلح للعلية والتأثير ، واحتجوا على صحة هذه المقدمة بوجوه :
The eleventh chapter on refuting infinite regress beyond what has been established. Know that in the previous chapter we indeed needed to refute infinite regress because we allowed that the cause of existence of a possible could be another possible. When we allowed this in general, we needed to refute infinite regress. And among people there are those who say: 'That which is possible in existence in itself is not suitable for causality and influence.' And they have argued for the soundness of this premise with proofs.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage opens the eleventh chapter of the work. The author explains why the previous chapter's refutation of infinite regress was necessary: because they had assumed that one possible could be the cause of another. However, some thinkers disagree with that assumption. They hold that a possible being (mumkin al-wujud li-dhatihi) cannot function as a cause or influence the existence of another. This alternative claim would make the previous refutation unnecessary, because if possibles cannot be causes, then there is no regress of possible causes to begin with. The author then notes that these thinkers support their claim with proofs. This passage sets up the subsequent discussion of those proofs.

Key Terms

تسلسل
إبطال التسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the author refutes infinite regress simply to show that chains must end, without realizing it is based on the assumption that possibles can cause each other.
Correction: The author explicitly states that the need to refute infinite regress arises only because we allowed one possible to cause another. If that assumption were not made, there would be no regress to refute.

Checkpoint
Why did the author need to refute infinite regress in the previous chapter?
A) Because infinite regress is always false by definition
B) Because they assumed a possible being can cause another possible being
C) Because the alternative view denies causality
D) Because they wanted to prove God's existence

Key Takeaway
The refutation of infinite regress is necessary only if we accept that one possible being can cause another.

The Alternative View: Possibles Cannot Be Causes
Author presents the alternative view that possible beings cannot be causes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the alternative claim that possible beings are not suitable for causality and influence, and why this would change the discussion about infinite regress.

Core Idea
Some argue that possible beings are inherently incapable of causing anything.

After explaining why the refutation of infinite regress was needed, the author introduces an opposing view. Some thinkers deny that possible beings can act as causes. If this is true, then the whole problem of infinite regress from one possible to another disappears. This lesson presents that alternative and sets the stage for the author's response.

Setting the Stage: Regress and the Possibility of Causality
Introduces the need to refute regress and states the opposing claim.

Learning Objective

Core Idea

The Unity of the Necessary Existent and the Role of Observation
Author introduces the chapter on the unity of the Necessary Existent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the methodological distinction between observable truths and rational proofs in the context of establishing the unity of the Necessary Existent.

Core Idea
Observation settles the manyness of bodies; reason proves the oneness of the Necessary Existent.

Before diving into the detailed proofs, the author clarifies the scope of the chapter. He distinguishes between what is known through observation—the multiplicity of bodies—and what requires rational demonstration—the unity of the Necessary Existent. This methodological note prevents confusion and sets the stage for the arguments to come.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that it is a chapter heading followed by a methodological remark.
The passage is concise; every phrase carries weight.
2
Identify the two claims
The author makes two main points: (1) The Necessary Existent is one in itself. (2) The multiplicity of bodies is observable and needs no proof.
These are foundational distinctions for the argument.
3
Understand the methodological implication
The author is telling us that some truths are self-evident through sense perception, while others require logical demonstration. This is a key principle in kalam methodology.
4
Recognize the broader context
The author mentions that detailed investigations on the proof are elsewhere. This lesson is just an introduction; deeper proofs will come later.

The Value of the Premise of Similarity of Bodies
The author explains the value of the premise of bodies' similarity for proving God, prophethood, and resurrection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the premise of similarity of bodies serves as a foundation for proving God's existence, prophethood, and the resurrection in Islamic theology.

Core Idea
Similarity of bodies is the key premise that unlocks proofs for God, prophethood, and the afterlife.

This passage presents the essential premise of similarity of bodies, which the theologians consider a noble and highly beneficial foundation in Islamic theology. By establishing this premise, they build arguments for three central doctrines: the existence of a free-agent God, the possibility of miracles and prophethood, and the reality of the resurrection. The author emphasizes that this premise makes possible the breaking of natural habits and the events described in the Qur'an.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core premise
Read the first sentence to see that the core of the argument is establishing the similarity of bodies. Recognize this as the foundational concept.
The author states he has already thoroughly discussed it.
2
Note the three applications
The author lists three areas where theologians use this premise: proving God, proving prophethood, and proving the resurrection. Observe how each is introduced.
3
Understand the logical links
For each application, see how similarity of bodies leads to the conclusion: for prophethood, it makes breaking habits possible; for resurrection, it makes cosmic changes possible.
Reflect on why these possibilities depend on the uniformity of bodies.
4
Read the concluding remark
The author concludes that this completes the exposition of this method. Recognize the closing summary.

The Two Views on the Need for a Cause in Origination
Introduces consensus that origination suffices, then classifies two views on need for a cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two positions among theologians regarding whether the knowledge that an originated thing needs a cause is necessary (intuitive) or inferential, and why mere origination is considered sufficient to prove the existence of an agent.

Core Idea
Mere origination suffices; the debate is whether the need for a cause is intuitive or requires proof.

In this passage, the author sets the stage for the 'method of origination' that does not require adding possibility. He notes that most theologians agree that simply observing an originated event is enough to infer an agent. But they split into two camps: those who see this knowledge as intuitive and those who see it as inferential. Al-Ka‘bi’s view is highlighted with an intuitive argument, including the example of a sound.

Guided Path
1
Read the consensus statement
Begin by reading the author's opening claim: the majority of theologians agree that mere origination suffices for inference to the agent. This is the foundation of the chapter.
Note the phrase 'جمهور المتكلمين' (majority of theologians).
2
Identify the two groups
The passage divides those who hold this consensus into two factions. Identify each group and its epistemological claim: one says knowledge is necessary (ḍarūrī), the other says it is inferential (istidlālī).
The first group includes al-Ka‘bi and a great many Muslims; the second includes Abu ‘Ali and Abu Hashim.
3
Examine al-Ka‘bi's argument
Read al-Ka‘bi's reasoning: rational people immediately seek a cause when they perceive an originated event, without hesitation. This, he argues, shows the knowledge is intuitive and ingrained.
Focus on the phrase 'بديهة العقل' (intuition of the intellect).
4
Understand the intuition's requirement
Notice the intuition of the intellect judges that an originated event must have an originated cause—not a pre-existing one from months or years before. This strengthens the immediacy of the need.
The example of the sound illustrates this point clearly.
5
Reflect on the debate's significance
Consider why this debate matters: if the need for a cause is intuitive, then the proof from origination is immediate; if inferential, it requires additional premises. Yet all agree on the sufficiency of origination.
This connects to broader kalām discussions on causality and the existence of God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل التاسع عشر تقرير طريقة الحدوث لا يحتاج فيها إلى ضم الإمكان أعلم : أن جمهور المتكلمين اتفقوا على أن مجرد الحدوث يكفي في صحة الاستدلالات به على وجود الفاعل . إلا أن القائلين بهذا القول فريقان : أحدهما : الذين قالوا : العلم بافتقار المحدث إلى المحدث علم ضروري. وهذا قول أبي القاسم الكعبي من المعتزلة وقول طائفة عظيمة من أهل الإسلام والثاني : قول من يقول : العلم بافتقار المحدث إلى الفاعل والصانع : علم استدلالي . لا يتم إلا بعد الدليل . وهو قول أبي علي وأبي هاشم وأصحابها من المعتزلة . أما الكعبي فكان يقول : إنا نرى العقلاء متي أحسوا بحدوث حادث طلبوا له سبباً وعلة من غير توقف ولا تأمل . فعلمنا : أن العلم بافتقار المحدث إلى المؤثر والمرجح : علم بديهي مركوز في عقول العقلاء ولقائل أن يقول : بديهة العقل تحكم بأن الحادث لابد له من سبب حادث . ولا يكتفي باستناد حدوث الحادث إلى سبب كان موجوداً قبل ذلك بشهور وسنين . ألا ترى أن من سمع حدوث صوت فإنه يطلب له علة
The Nineteenth Chapter: Exposition That the Method of Origination Does Not Require Joining Possibility. Know that the majority of theologians agreed that mere origination suffices for the validity of inferences by it to the existence of the agent. However, those who hold this saying are two groups: One of them: those who said that the knowledge of the originated thing's need for an originator is necessary knowledge. This is the saying of Abu al-Qasim al-Ka‘bi from the Mu‘tazila and the saying of a great group from the people of Islam. And the second: the saying of those who say: the knowledge of the originated thing's need for the agent and maker is inferential knowledge, which is only completed after a proof. This is the saying of Abu ‘Ali and Abu Hashim and their followers from the Mu‘tazila. As for al-Ka‘bi, he used to say: 'We see that rational people, whenever they perceive the occurrence of an originated event, they seek a cause and reason for it without hesitation or reflection. So we know that the knowledge of the originated thing's need for an effecting cause and preponderator is intuitive knowledge, ingrained in the minds of rational people.' And one may say: The intuition of the intellect judges that the originated event necessarily requires an originated cause. And it is not satisfied with attributing the occurrence of the originated event to a cause that existed months and years before. Do you not see that whoever hears the occurrence of a sound, he seeks a cause for it?
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of the nineteenth chapter, titled 'Exposition That the Method of Origination Does Not Require Joining Possibility'. The author first states a consensus: the majority of theologians (jumhūr al-mutakallimīn) agree that mere origination (mujarrad al-ḥudūth) is sufficient to infer the existence of an agent. Then he divides those who hold this view into two groups. The first group holds that the knowledge that an originated thing needs an originator is necessary (ḍarūrī) and intuitive; this is the position of al-Ka‘bī (a Mu‘tazilī) and many from Ahl al-Islam. The second group holds that this knowledge is inferential (istidlālī), requiring a proof; this is the position of Abu ‘Ali and Abu Hashim (also Mu‘tazila). The author then elaborates on al-Ka‘bī's argument: rational people instinctively seek a cause for any new event without reflection, so the need for a cause is intuitive. The intellect's intuition (badīhat al-ʿaql) rules that an originated event must have an originated cause (sabab ḥādith), not a pre-existing cause from months or years earlier. He gives the example of a sound: when you hear a sound, you immediately seek its cause. The passage thus presents a key debate within kalam about the epistemological status of the need for a cause, and argues for the sufficiency of origination alone.

علم ضروري
علم استدلالي
الكعبي
بديهة العقل
سبب حادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that the proof from origination always requires adding the notion of possibility (imkān) to be valid.
Correction: The chapter explicitly argues that mere origination (mujarrad al-ḥudūth) suffices and does not require joining possibility, as the need for a cause is either intuitive or can be established directly.

Checkpoint

C) It is neither necessary nor inferential
D) It is only known through divine revelation

Key Takeaway
The knowledge that an originated event needs a cause may be intuitive for some and inferential for others, but all agree that origination alone points to an agent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When something unexpected happens in your life, do you immediately seek a worldly cause or do you attribute it to Allah? Reflect on your heart's first reaction. Which of the two groups does your instinct belong to? Will you train your soul to see Allah's hand behind every event, even the ones you've attributed to coincidence?
?
You know that the need for a cause is either intuitive or inferential. But do you live as if you truly need Allah for every breath and every moment? Or do you act as if the universe runs on its own? Ask yourself: at this very moment, are you aware that your existence is originated and thus dependent on Allah? How can you make this awareness a constant reality?
?
The rational people described by al-Ka‘bī seek a cause for every sound they hear. How often do you hear the verses of the Qur'an, or the call to prayer, and fail to seek the Cause behind them? What would change if you took each sound as a direct reminder of the Creator who is bringing it into existence right now?

The Two Positions on Knowledge of Dependence
Classification of two groups and elaboration of one group's argument

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main positions on how we know that originated things depend on an agent, and the intuitive argument of al-Ka'bi.

Core Idea
Some theologians see the dependence as intuitive, others as inferential; both agree huduth suffices.

The majority of theologians agree that the mere fact that something is originated (huduth) is enough to prove an agent. But they disagree on how we know that dependence—whether it is immediately obvious (intuitive) or requires a rational proof. This passage introduces these two camps and presents the intuitive argument with a vivid example.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening classification
The author states the agreement among most theologians that huduth suffices, then divides the claimants into two groups. Note the consensus and the division.
2
Study the first group (al-Ka'bi)
Al-Ka'bi holds that knowledge of dependence is necessary (ḍarūrī) and intuitive (badīhī). His reasoning: rational people spontaneously seek a cause for any new event without reflection.
This group includes a large party of Muslims.
3
Study the second group (Abu Ali, Abu Hashim)
This group holds that knowledge of dependence is inferential (istidlālī) and requires a proof. They are Mu'tazili theologians.
4
Analyze al-Ka'bi's example
Al-Ka'bi uses the example of hearing a sound: one instinctively seeks a cause. If the explanation is a pre-existing condition (sky above, earth below), the intellect rejects it because that condition existed before the sound. This shows that the cause must be contemporary (ḥādith) and that this judgment is intuitive.
This example illustrates why the knowledge is considered intuitive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أعلم : أن جمهور المتكلمين اتفقوا على أن مجرد الحدوث يكفي في صحة الاستدلالات به على وجود الفاعل . إلا أن القائلين بهذا القول فريقان : أحدهما : الذين قالوا : العلم بافتقار المحدث [ إلى المحدث ] علم ضروري. وهذا قول أبي القاسم الكعبي : [ من المعتزلة ] وقول طائفة عظيمة من أهل الإسلام والثاني : قول من يقول : العلم بافتقار المحدث إلى الفاعل والصانع : علم استدلالي . لا يتم إلا بعد الدليل . وهو قول أبي علي ، وأبي هاشم ، وأصحابها من المعتزلة . أما « الكعبي ، فكان يقول : « إنا نرى العقلاء متي أحسوا بحدوث حادث ، طلبوا له سبباً وعلة ، من غير توقف ولا تأمل . فعلمنا : أن العلم بافتقار المحدث إلى المؤثر والمرجح : علم بديهي ، مركوز في عقول العقلاء ، ولقائل أن يقول : بديهة العقل تحكم بأن الحادث لابد له من سبب حادث . ولا يكتفي باستناد حدوث الحادث، إلى سبب كان موجوداً قبل ذلك بشهور وسنين . ألا ترى أن من سمع حدوث صوت ، فإنه يطلب له علة وسبباً . فلو قيل : السبب في حدوثه : كون السماء فوقنا ، والأرض تحتنا ، حكم صريح العقل بفساد هذا التعليل . وكل عاقل يقول : إن كون السماء فوقنا ، والأرض تحتنا : كان حاصلا قبل هذا الوقت . فكيف يمكن جعله سبباً لحدوث الصوت في هذه الساعة . بل لابد لهذا الصوت [ الحادث ] من سبب حادث؟
Know that the majority of theologians agreed that mere occurrence (ḥudūth) suffices for the validity of inferences from it to the existence of the agent. However, those who hold this opinion are two groups: one of them is those who said: knowledge of the dependence of an originated thing (al-muḥdath) on an originator (al-muḥdith) is necessary (ḍarūrī). This is the statement of Abū al-Qāsim al-Ka‘bī from the Mu‘tazila and the statement of a great group from the people of Islam. The second is the statement of those who say: knowledge of the dependence of the originated thing on the agent and maker is inferential (istidlālī), not completed except after proof. This is the statement of Abū ‘Alī and Abū Hāshim and their followers from the Mu‘tazila. As for al-Ka‘bī, he used to say: 'We see that rational people, whenever they perceive an occurrence of an event, they seek for it a cause and a reason, without stopping or reflection. So we know that the knowledge of the dependence of the originated thing on the effective cause and preponderator is intuitive (badīhī), ingrained in the minds of rational people.' And it may be said: The intuition of the intellect judges that the event necessarily has a recent cause, and it is not satisfied with attributing the occurrence of the event to a cause that existed before it months or years. Do you not see that whoever hears an occurrence of a sound, he seeks for it a cause and a reason? If it were said: the cause of its occurrence is that the sky is above us and the earth below us, the explicit judgment of the intellect would deem this explanation invalid. And every rational person says: The fact that the sky is above us and the earth below us existed before this time. So how can it be made a cause for the occurrence of the sound at this hour? Rather, this sound necessarily has a recent cause?
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage is a classification of two groups who agree that mere occurrence (ḥudūth) suffices as evidence for an agent. The author first states the consensus of the majority of theologians (mutakallimūn) on this point. Then he divides them into two groups. The first group, represented by Abū al-Qāsim al-Ka‘bī and a large party of Muslims, holds that knowledge of the dependence of an originated thing on an originator is necessary (ḍarūrī) and intuitive (badīhī). Al-Ka‘bī argues that whenever rational people perceive a new event, they spontaneously seek a cause for it without reflection. This shows that such knowledge is ingrained in the mind. The second group, represented by Abū ‘Alī, Abū Hāshim, and their Mu‘tazilī followers, holds that this knowledge is inferential (istidlālī) and requires a proof. The author then elaborates al-Ka‘bī's position with an example: if someone hears a sound, he immediately looks for a cause; if someone claimed that the cause was the sky being above or the earth being below—things that existed before the sound—the intellect would reject that explanation because a cause must be contemporary with the effect. Thus, the need for a recent cause is intuitive.

بديهي (badīhī)

محدث (muḥdath)
مؤثر (mu’aththir)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the second group denies the intuitive recognition of causality.
Correction: They only require a proof to make it explicit or to justify it against skeptics, not that they deny the intuitive impulse.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the view of al-Ka'bi regarding knowledge of the dependence of an originated thing?
A) It is inferential.
B) It is necessary and intuitive.
C) It is impossible.
D) It is from revelation.

Key Takeaway
The two groups differ on whether knowledge of dependence is intuitive or inferential, but both agree that huduth proves an agent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perceive a new event—a blessing, a trial, or a change in your life—does your heart instinctively turn to Allah as the cause, or do you need to remind yourself? What does your immediate reaction reveal about the state of your fitrah?
?
Al-Ka'bi says rational people seek a cause without reflection. Think of a moment today when something unexpected happened. Did you automatically attribute it to secondary means, or did you recognize Allah's direct role? If the latter, was it intuitive or did you have to reason it out? How can you train your heart to make that attribution intuitive?
?
The second group says knowledge of dependence requires proof. Is your certainty in Allah's existence merely intellectual, or has it penetrated your heart to the point where you feel your absolute dependence on Him every moment? What would it take for you to move from inferential belief to an unshakable, intuitive conviction that colors all your thoughts and actions?

The Four Pillars of the Mu'tazilite Qiyas
Mu'tazilites present the four pillars of their qiyas for contingency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite claim that the argument from contingency requires a detailed analogical proof and the four pillars (asl, far', illa, hukm) that structure it.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazilites insist that the inference from contingency to a creator must be a formal qiyas with four components.

The Mu'tazilites, led by figures like Abu Ali and Abu Hashim, argued that the simple statement 'the world is contingent, therefore it needs a creator' is insufficient. They demanded a detailed analogy (qiyas mufassal) with clearly defined components. This lesson introduces that structure, which sets the stage for the rest of their proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the English translation. Notice how the Mu'tazilites present their claim as a formal analogy.
This passage is a report of the Mu'tazilite doctrine, not an original argument.
2
Identify the four pillars (arkan)
The passage explicitly states that every qiyas has four pillars: asl, far', illa, hukm. Locate these in the text and note what each one represents in this specific argument.
The asl is the human servant originating his own actions, the far' is the world, the hukm is the need for an agent, and the illa is contingency.
3
Understand the role of the qiyas
Why do they insist on a qiyas? Reflect on why they think a direct inference is not enough. The commentary will help explain the reasoning behind this methodological requirement.
4
Connect to the next lesson
The passage ends by stating the need to establish the asl (original case). This lesson sets up the next lesson, which will cover how they prove the asl.
The asl is the most critical part to establish for the analogy to work.

الفصل العشرون
تقرير قول من يقول: الاستدلال بالحدوث على الفاعل لا يتم إلا بدليل مفصل
قد حكينا: أن هذا القول هو مذهب أبي علي وأبي هاشم وهو القاضي عبد الجبار بن أحمد وأصحابهم من المعتزلة. قالوا: نظم هذا الدليل أن يقال: العالم محدث فوجب أن يفتقر إلى الفاعل قياسا على أفعالنا المحتاجة إلينا في حدوثها. قالوا: وكل قياس فلا بد فيه من أركان أربعة: الأصل والفرع والعلة والحكم. فالأصل هو كون العبد محدثا لأفعال نفسه، والفرع هو العالم، والحكم هو الاحتياج إلى الفاعل، والعلة هي الحدوث.
فنفتقر إلى إثبات هذه المقامات الأربعة: أما الأصل فهو قولنا: إن أفعالنا محتاجة في الحدوث إلينا. وهذا المقام لا بد فيه من تقرير أمرين: أحدهما: إثبات الأعراض [أعني] إثبات أن حركاتنا وسكناتنا أمور موجودة. الثاني: بيان أن أفعالنا واقعة بنا بمعنى محدثها نحن لا غير. أما المقام الأول فقد ذكروا فيه الدلائل الدالة على إثبات الأعراض. وأما المقام الثاني فقد احتجوا عليه بأن هذه الأفعال يجب وقوعها عند دواعينا الخالصة ويجب انتفاؤها عند قيام الصوارف. وإذا كان الأمر كذلك وجب القطع بكونها واقعة بنا لا بغيرنا.
والدليل على وجوب وقوعها عند حصول الدواعي القوية: أن الصائم في الصيف [الصائف] إذا اشتدت شهوته لشرب الماء والشارع يأمره بالشرب والطبيب يأمره بالشرب وعلم هذا الإنسان أنه لا مضرة في هذا الشرب لا في دينه ولا في دنياه فإنه يحصل له عند هذا الشرب أعظم اللذات. فإن عند حضور هذه الاعتقادات في قلبه يستحيل أن لا يشرب الماء. فثبت أن الفعل عند توفر الدواعي الخالصة عن الصوارف واجب الوقوع. والدليل على أن الفعل ممتنع الوقوع عند حصول الصوارف الخالصة: أن من علم ما في دخول النار من الضرر العظيم وعلم أنه لا منفعة له في دخولها البتة لا في الحال ولا في الاستقبال ولا يتوسل بذلك الدخول إلى دفع ضرر أعظم منه فإن عند حضور هذه الاعتقادات في قلبه يمتنع منه دخول النار.
فيثبت بهذا البيان أن عند خلوص الدواعي يجب حصول الأفعال وعند خلوص الصوارف يمتنع حصولها. وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول: وجب القطع بأن هذه الأفعال واقعة بنا لا بغيرنا. لأنه لو لم يكن وقوعها بنا لكان وقوعها إما أن يكون لا لمؤثر أو لمؤثر غيرنا. وعلى التقديرين فكان لا يمتنع أن تقع وإن كرهناها ولا أن لا تقع وإن أردناها. كما أن فعل زيد لما لم يكن واقعا بعمرو لا جرم ربما وقع وإن كرهه عمرو وربما لا يقع وإن أراده عمرو. وذلك يقدح فيما بيناه من أن تلك الأفعال واجبة الوقوع عند توفر الدواعي وممتنعة الوقوع عند توفر الصوارف. فيثبت بهذا البيان الذي ذكرناه أن أفعالنا واقعة بنا. وهذا هو إثبات أصل القياس المذكور.
Chapter Twenty
Statement of the one who says: The inference from contingency to the agent is not complete except with a detailed proof.
We have reported that this statement is the doctrine of Abu Ali and Abu Hashim, who is al-Qadi Abd al-Jabbar ibn Ahmad, and their companions from the Mu'tazila. They said: The arrangement of this proof is that it is said: The world is contingent, so it necessarily depends on an agent, by analogy to our actions which need us in their origination. They said: Every analogy necessarily has four pillars: the original case (al-asl), the derivative case (al-far'), the cause (al-illa), and the ruling (al-hukm). So the original case is that the servant is the originator of his own actions, the derivative case is the world, the ruling is the need for an agent, and the cause is contingency.
So we need to establish these four stations: As for the original case, it is our statement: Our actions need us in their origination. This station requires the establishment of two matters: one: establishing the accidents (al-a'rad), i.e., establishing that our movements and rests are existing things. The second: clarifying that our actions occur by us, meaning we are their originator, not another. As for the first station, they mentioned in it the proofs that demonstrate the existence of accidents. As for the second station, they argued for it by saying that these actions necessarily occur when our motives are pure and necessarily cease when repulsive factors exist. And if the matter is thus, then it is necessary to be certain that they occur by us and not by another.
The proof of the necessity of their occurrence when strong motives are obtained: That the fasting person in summer [the thirsty] when his desire for drinking water intensifies, and the legislator commands him to drink, and the doctor commands him to drink, and this person knows that there is no harm in this drinking, neither in his religion nor in his worldly affairs, then he obtains through this drinking the greatest pleasures. For when these beliefs are present in his heart, it is impossible that he does not drink water. So it is established that the action, when pure motives are available free from repellants, is necessary of occurrence. And the proof that the action is impossible of occurrence when pure repellants are obtained: That one who knows what is in entering the fire of great harm, and knows that there is no benefit for him in entering it at all, neither in the present nor in the future, and he does not seek through that entry to repel a harm greater than it, then when these beliefs are present in his heart, entering the fire is impossible for him.
So it is established by this clarification that when motives are pure, actions necessarily occur, and when repellants are pure, their occurrence is impossible. And once this is established, we say: It is necessary to be certain that these actions occur by us and not by another. For if their occurrence were not by us, then their occurrence would either be without an effect, or by an effect other than us. On both hypotheses, it would not be impossible that they occur even if we dislike them, nor that they do not occur even if we want them. Just as the action of Zayd, since it does not occur by Amr, inevitably perhaps occurs even if Amr dislikes it, and perhaps does not occur even if Amr wants it. And that would contradict what we explained: that those actions are necessary in occurrence when motives are available and impossible in occurrence when repellants are available. So it is established by this clarification we mentioned that our actions occur by us. And this is the establishment of the original case of the aforementioned analogy.
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage is a detailed presentation of the Mu'tazilite position on the argument from contingency (al-istidlal bi al-huduth). The author reports that Abu Ali, Abu Hashim (Qadi Abd al-Jabbar), and their followers argue that the simple inference 'the world is contingent, therefore it needs an agent' is insufficient without a detailed analogical proof (qiyas mufassal). They structure their proof as a qiyas with four pillars: the original case (asl), the derivative case (far'), the cause (illa), and the ruling (hukm). For them, the asl is that the human servant (al-abd) is the originator of his own actions, the far' is the world, the hukm is the need for an agent, and the illa is contingency (al-huduth). To establish the asl, they must prove two things: first, that human actions (movements and rests) are existing entities (accidents, a'rad), and second, that these actions are produced by us and not by another. The author notes that the Mu'tazilites have separate proofs for the existence of accidents. Then he presents their argument for the second point: human actions necessarily occur when our motives (dawa'i) are pure and free from repellants (sawari'), and necessarily cease when pure repellants are present. To prove this, they give two examples: a fasting person who is commanded to drink by both the legislator and a doctor, knowing no harm, will inevitably drink because the pleasure is great and motives are pure. Conversely, a person who knows the immense harm of entering fire and sees no benefit will inevitably refrain. Thus, actions are contingent upon motives and repellants. From this, they conclude that actions are from us, because if actions came from another, they could occur against our will or fail to occur when we want, contradicting the observed necessity. This establishes the asl (original case) of their analogy.

Key Terms

فرع
علة
حكم
قياس

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazilites, what are the four pillars (arkan) of the qiyas in their argument from contingency?
A) Asl, far', illa, hukm
B) Asl, far', dalil, hukm
C) Asl, far', illa, natija
D) Asl, far', sabab, hukm

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite proof for a creator from contingency is structured as a formal analogy with four pillars: asl, far', illa, and hukm.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read that the Mu'tazilites structure their belief in a Creator using a formal qiyas based on human action. Can you genuinely point to a time when your own certainty in Allah was built on careful reasoning like this, or is your faith more inherited? What would it cost you to examine it as rigorously as they did?
?
The Mu'tazilites insist that the original case (al-asl) is that you originate your own actions. Do you live as if you are truly the author of your deeds, accountable before Allah, or do you find yourself making excuses that hide behind divine decree? Which actions today will you own completely as yours?
?
This proof depends on the premise that contingency (huduth) is the cause linking human acts and the world. When you look at the universe, does its constant change and temporality grip your heart with awe of the eternal Creator, or have you become numb to the obvious? What habit could you start this week to make the contingency of the world a real, daily reminder?

Method of Learning
intermediate
6 min
Establishing the Asl: Proving Human Actions are from Us
Mu'tazilites prove the asl by showing human actions are from us via motive/repellant argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Mu'tazilites prove that human actions are produced by the agent using the argument from motives and repellants.

Core Idea
Actions necessarily follow pure motives and are prevented by strong repellants, proving they are from us.

Having presented the four pillars of their qiyas, the Mu'tazilites now focus on establishing the asl (original case): that human actions are originated by the human agent. They do this by proving two things: first, that actions are existing entities (accidents), and second, that they occur by us. The second point is proven through a detailed argument about motives (dawa'i) and repellants (sawari'). This lesson walks through that argument, including the famous examples of the fasting person and the person facing fire.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation in full. This is a long but self-contained argument. Try to grasp the overall structure: two stations, with a detailed proof for the second.
The first station (establishing accidents) is only mentioned; the focus is on the second station.
2
Identify the two stations (maqamat)
The passage says the asl requires two matters: proving accidents exist, and proving actions are from us. Locate these in the text.
The first station is not elaborated; the author says they have proofs elsewhere.
3
Analyze the proof from motives and repellants
The second station uses the principle that actions necessarily occur when motives are pure and free from repellants, and necessarily do not occur when repellants are pure. The examples of the fasting person and the fire illustrate this.
Note the specific conditions: pure motives, no repellants, knowledge of harm/benefit.
4
Understand the reductio ad absurdum
After establishing the necessity of actions based on motives/repellants, the Mu'tazilites argue that if actions were not from us, they could occur against our will, contradicting the observed necessity. This forces the conclusion that actions are from us.
This is a classic kalām argument from observed regularity.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage ends by stating that the asl of the analogy is established. Connect this back to the overall qiyas: if human actions are from us, then the world, being contingent like them, must also need an agent.

الفصل العشرون
تقرير قول من يقول: الاستدلال بالحدوث على الفاعل لا يتم إلا بدليل مفصل
قد حكينا: أن هذا القول هو مذهب أبي علي وأبي هاشم وهو القاضي عبد الجبار بن أحمد وأصحابهم من المعتزلة. قالوا: نظم هذا الدليل أن يقال: العالم محدث فوجب أن يفتقر إلى الفاعل قياسا على أفعالنا المحتاجة إلينا في حدوثها. قالوا: وكل قياس فلا بد فيه من أركان أربعة: الأصل والفرع والعلة والحكم. فالأصل هو كون العبد محدثا لأفعال نفسه، والفرع هو العالم، والحكم هو الاحتياج إلى الفاعل، والعلة هي الحدوث.
فنفتقر إلى إثبات هذه المقامات الأربعة: أما الأصل فهو قولنا: إن أفعالنا محتاجة في الحدوث إلينا. وهذا المقام لا بد فيه من تقرير أمرين: أحدهما: إثبات الأعراض [أعني] إثبات أن حركاتنا وسكناتنا أمور موجودة. الثاني: بيان أن أفعالنا واقعة بنا بمعنى محدثها نحن لا غير. أما المقام الأول فقد ذكروا فيه الدلائل الدالة على إثبات الأعراض. وأما المقام الثاني فقد احتجوا عليه بأن هذه الأفعال يجب وقوعها عند دواعينا الخالصة ويجب انتفاؤها عند قيام الصوارف. وإذا كان الأمر كذلك وجب القطع بكونها واقعة بنا لا بغيرنا.
والدليل على وجوب وقوعها عند حصول الدواعي القوية: أن الصائم في الصيف [الصائف] إذا اشتدت شهوته لشرب الماء والشارع يأمره بالشرب والطبيب يأمره بالشرب وعلم هذا الإنسان أنه لا مضرة في هذا الشرب لا في دينه ولا في دنياه فإنه يحصل له عند هذا الشرب أعظم اللذات. فإن عند حضور هذه الاعتقادات في قلبه يستحيل أن لا يشرب الماء. فثبت أن الفعل عند توفر الدواعي الخالصة عن الصوارف واجب الوقوع. والدليل على أن الفعل ممتنع الوقوع عند حصول الصوارف الخالصة: أن من علم ما في دخول النار من الضرر العظيم وعلم أنه لا منفعة له في دخولها البتة لا في الحال ولا في الاستقبال ولا يتوسل بذلك الدخول إلى دفع ضرر أعظم منه فإن عند حضور هذه الاعتقادات في قلبه يمتنع منه دخول النار.
فيثبت بهذا البيان أن عند خلوص الدواعي يجب حصول الأفعال وعند خلوص الصوارف يمتنع حصولها. وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول: وجب القطع بأن هذه الأفعال واقعة بنا لا بغيرنا. لأنه لو لم يكن وقوعها بنا لكان وقوعها إما أن يكون لا لمؤثر أو لمؤثر غيرنا. وعلى التقديرين فكان لا يمتنع أن تقع وإن كرهناها ولا أن لا تقع وإن أردناها. كما أن فعل زيد لما لم يكن واقعا بعمرو لا جرم ربما وقع وإن كرهه عمرو وربما لا يقع وإن أراده عمرو. وذلك يقدح فيما بيناه من أن تلك الأفعال واجبة الوقوع عند توفر الدواعي وممتنعة الوقوع عند توفر الصوارف. فيثبت بهذا البيان الذي ذكرناه أن أفعالنا واقعة بنا. وهذا هو إثبات أصل القياس المذكور.
Chapter Twenty
Statement of the one who says: The inference from contingency to the agent is not complete except with a detailed proof.
We have reported that this statement is the doctrine of Abu Ali and Abu Hashim, who is al-Qadi Abd al-Jabbar ibn Ahmad, and their companions from the Mu'tazila. They said: The arrangement of this proof is that it is said: The world is contingent, so it necessarily depends on an agent, by analogy to our actions which need us in their origination. They said: Every analogy necessarily has four pillars: the original case (al-asl), the derivative case (al-far'), the cause (al-illa), and the ruling (al-hukm). So the original case is that the servant is the originator of his own actions, the derivative case is the world, the ruling is the need for an agent, and the cause is contingency.
So we need to establish these four stations: As for the original case, it is our statement: Our actions need us in their origination. This station requires the establishment of two matters: one: establishing the accidents (al-a'rad), i.e., establishing that our movements and rests are existing things. The second: clarifying that our actions occur by us, meaning we are their originator, not another. As for the first station, they mentioned in it the proofs that demonstrate the existence of accidents. As for the second station, they argued for it by saying that these actions necessarily occur when our motives are pure and necessarily cease when repulsive factors exist. And if the matter is thus, then it is necessary to be certain that they occur by us and not by another.
The proof of the necessity of their occurrence when strong motives are obtained: That the fasting person in summer [the thirsty] when his desire for drinking water intensifies, and the legislator commands him to drink, and the doctor commands him to drink, and this person knows that there is no harm in this drinking, neither in his religion nor in his worldly affairs, then he obtains through this drinking the greatest pleasures. For when these beliefs are present in his heart, it is impossible that he does not drink water. So it is established that the action, when pure motives are available free from repellants, is necessary of occurrence. And the proof that the action is impossible of occurrence when pure repellants are obtained: That one who knows what is in entering the fire of great harm, and knows that there is no benefit for him in entering it at all, neither in the present nor in the future, and he does not seek through that entry to repel a harm greater than it, then when these beliefs are present in his heart, entering the fire is impossible for him.
So it is established by this clarification that when motives are pure, actions necessarily occur, and when repellants are pure, their occurrence is impossible. And once this is established, we say: It is necessary to be certain that these actions occur by us and not by another. For if their occurrence were not by us, then their occurrence would either be without an effect, or by an effect other than us. On both hypotheses, it would not be impossible that they occur even if we dislike them, nor that they do not occur even if we want them. Just as the action of Zayd, since it does not occur by Amr, inevitably perhaps occurs even if Amr dislikes it, and perhaps does not occur even if Amr wants it. And that would contradict what we explained: that those actions are necessary in occurrence when motives are available and impossible in occurrence when repellants are available. So it is established by this clarification we mentioned that our actions occur by us. And this is the establishment of the original case of the aforementioned analogy.

Commentary
This passage is a detailed presentation of the Mu'tazilite position on the argument from contingency (al-istidlal bi al-huduth). The author reports that Abu Ali, Abu Hashim (Qadi Abd al-Jabbar), and their followers argue that the simple inference 'the world is contingent, therefore it needs an agent' is insufficient without a detailed analogical proof (qiyas mufassal). They structure their proof as a qiyas with four pillars: the original case (asl), the derivative case (far'), the cause (illa), and the ruling (hukm). For them, the asl is that the human servant (al-abd) is the originator of his own actions, the far' is the world, the hukm is the need for an agent, and the illa is contingency (al-huduth). To establish the asl, they must prove two things: first, that human actions (movements and rests) are existing entities (accidents, a'rad), and second, that these actions are produced by us and not by another. The author notes that the Mu'tazilites have separate proofs for the existence of accidents. Then he presents their argument for the second point: human actions necessarily occur when our motives (dawa'i) are pure and free from repellants (sawari'), and necessarily cease when pure repellants are present. From this, they conclude that actions are from us, because if actions came from another, they could occur against our will or fail to occur when we want, contradicting the observed necessity. This establishes the asl (original case) of their analogy.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Human actions could be produced by an external cause, such as God, without human agency.
Correction: The Mu'tazilites argue that if actions were from an external cause, they could occur against our will, contradicting the observed necessity that actions follow our motives and repellants.

Checkpoint
Which example does the Mu'tazilite argument use to prove that actions are necessary when motives are pure?
A) A person entering fire
B) A fasting person drinking water
C) A person sleeping
D) A person walking

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilites prove human agency by showing that actions necessarily follow pure motives and are prevented by strong repellants, which would be impossible if actions were not from us.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Can you recall a moment when your desire for something was so strong that you acted on it despite knowing it was wrong? According to this lesson, what does that say about your role in that action? How will you strengthen your repellants (sawari') before your next temptation?
?
When you fail to do a good deed you intended to do, what repellant stopped you? Is it a valid excuse or a weakness in your motive? How will you purify your motives (dawa'i) to overcome such repellants starting today?
?
If the Mu'tazilites are correct that your actions necessarily follow your motives and repellants, then what does your daily pattern of actions reveal about the state of your heart? Are you willing to change your motives and repellants to align with what pleases Allah, even if it means abandoning lifelong habits?

Method of Learning
beginner
4 min
Preferring the Quranic Method over Rational Arguments
Concludes that the Quran's method of presenting proofs is superior.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author considers the Quranic method of presenting proofs superior to the subtle arguments of philosophers and theologians, and how this method avoids doubt.

Core Idea
The Quranic method avoids doubt by focusing on tangible creation rather than subtle reasoning.

The author concludes his discussion by praising the Quranic method. He acknowledges the strength of philosophical and theological proofs but notes their subtlety opens doors to doubts. The Quranic approach, by contrast, prohibits excessive speculation and directs the intellect to observe the universe. This lesson explores that comparison and the author's personal endorsement.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the author's tone: he is concluding and recommending.
2
Identify the two methods compared
The passage compares the method of philosophers/theologians with the Quranic method. Find what each entails and what makes them different.
Notice the key terms: الحكماء والمتكلمون vs الطريق الوارد في القرآن
3
Understand why the Quranic method is preferred
The author says rational proofs are strong but their subtlety opens doubts. The Quranic method forbids excessive depth and idle talk, and promotes observing creation. Why does that reduce doubts?
Reflect on the phrase 'المنع من التعمق والاحتراز عن فتح باب القيل والقال'.
4
Reflect on the author's personal testimony
The author claims personal experience: 'ومن ترك التعصب وجرب مثل تجربتي علم أن الحق ما ذكرته'. How does this personal note strengthen the argument?
Consider how experience and leaving bias relate to accepting the Quranic method.

ونختم هذه الفصول بخاتمة عظيمة النفع، وهي أن الدلائل التي ذكرها الحكماء والمتكلمون وإن كانت كاملة قوية ، إلا أن هذه الطريقة المذكورة في القرآن عندي : أنها أقرب إلى الحق والصواب ، وذلك لأن تلك الدلائل دقيقة ، وبسبب ما فيها من الدقة انفتحت أبواب الشبهات ، وكثرت السؤالات . وأما الطريق الوارد في القرآن فحاصله راجع إلى طريق واحد وهو المنع من التعمق ، والاحتراز عن فتح باب القيل والقال ، وحمل الفهم والعقل على الاستكثار من دلائل العالم الأعلى والأسفل. ومن ترك التعصب ، وجرب مثل تجربتي ، علم أن الحق ما ذكرته ، ولما ثبت أن هذا الطريق الذي ذكره الله في القرآن أنفع ، وفي القلوب أرجح ، لا جرم أفردنا له بابا مستقلا ، وهو القسم الثاني من هذا الكتاب. ونسأل الله تعالى الهداية والرحمة والإرشاد إلى الحق ، بفضله ، فإنه خير موفق ومعين
And we conclude these chapters with a conclusion of great benefit, namely that the proofs mentioned by the philosophers (hukama') and theologians (mutakallimun), even though they are complete and strong, yet this method mentioned in the Qur'an is, in my view, closer to truth and correctness — and that is because those proofs are subtle, and due to their subtlety, the doors of doubts have opened and questions have multiplied. As for the method present in the Qur'an, its essence goes back to one method, which is the prohibition of delving deep (ta'ammuq), and avoiding opening the door of idle talk (qil wa qal), and directing the understanding and intellect to multiply the proofs from the upper and lower worlds. And whoever leaves partisanship and tries like my experience will know that the truth is what I have mentioned. And since it has been established that this method which Allah mentioned in the Qur'an is more beneficial and more weighty in hearts, we have consequently devoted to it an independent chapter, which is the second section of this book. And we ask Allah the Exalted for guidance, mercy, and direction to the truth by His favor, for He is the best success giver and helper.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage is the author's concluding reflection on the best method for presenting proofs of God. He affirms that the arguments of philosophers and theologians are valid and strong, but their subtlety has opened doors to doubts and questions. In contrast, the Quranic method avoids deep speculation and verbal disputes, instead directing the intellect to multiply observations of the created world. The author claims personal experience that this method is truer and more convincing. He announces that he has devoted the second part of his book to this Quranic approach. He ends with a prayer for guidance and success. The passage shows a balance: rational proofs are not rejected, but the Quranic approach is preferred for its clarity and ability to avoid confusion.

Key Terms
الحكماء
المتكلمون
التعمق
القيل والقال
العالم الأعلى والأسفل
هداية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author rejects rational theology entirely.
Correction: The author affirms rational proofs are complete and strong, but prefers the Quranic method because it is more effective in preventing doubts.

Checkpoint
Why does the author prefer the Quranic method over the arguments of philosophers and theologians?
A) Because the philosophers' arguments are false
B) Because the subtlety of those arguments opened doors to doubts
C) Because the Quranic method is easier to write
D) Because theologians are biased

Key Takeaway
The Quranic method is superior because it avoids the subtlety that breeds doubt, instead directing the intellect to observe the creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you engage in discussions about faith, do you notice yourself leaning toward subtle debates that leave you with more doubts than certainty? What would it look like to shift your approach to the Quranic method of reflecting on the creation?
?
The author says that leaving partisanship and testing his experience leads to knowing the truth. Are you willing to let go of any biases you hold—whether from a particular school or a strong opinion—and honestly try the Quranic method in your own life? What specific step will you take this week?
?
The Quranic method directs you to multiply proofs from the upper and lower worlds. When was the last time you spent serious time contemplating a single aspect of creation to strengthen your faith? Will you commit to doing that today, and if so, what will you ponder?

The Eight Types of Evidence in the Quran
Al-Razi enumerates eight types of evidence from a Quranic verse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the eight types of evidence that al-Razi extracts from a single Quranic verse and how he classifies them into celestial and elemental categories.

Core Idea
A single Quranic verse contains multiple layers of evidence for divine existence.

In this passage, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi demonstrates the richness of a single Quranic verse by enumerating eight distinct types of evidence it contains. He shows how the verse covers both celestial phenomena and earthly signs, providing a comprehensive proof for God's existence and power. This lesson will walk through each type of evidence as al-Razi identifies them.

Guided Path
1
Read the enumeration passage
Read the Arabic passage where al-Razi lists the eight types of evidence. Notice how he divides them into celestial and elemental categories.
The passage is dense; take your time to identify each type.
2
Identify the eight types
List each type as al-Razi mentions them: celestial (creation, night/day) and elemental (ships, plants, animals, winds, clouds). Note the verse citations for each.
There is a slight discrepancy in count; reflect on why al-Razi says eight though only seven are explicitly detailed.
3
Understand the classification
Recognize that the classification shows systematic theological reasoning: evidence from the cosmos and from earthly phenomena.
4
Connect to the verse's conclusion
The verse ends with 'signs for a people who reason.' See how al-Razi uses this to emphasize rationality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهذه الآية مشتملة على ثمانية أنواع من الدلائل . فالثلاثة الأول من الدلائل الفلكية ، وهي قوله تعالى : ( إن في خلق السموات والأرض واختلاف الليل والنهار) والخمسة الباقية من الدلائل، هي دلائل عالم العناصر ، وهي قوله تعالى : ( والفلك التي تجري في البحر بما ينفع الناس ) ثم ذكر بعده دلائل النبات فقال : ( وما أنزل الله من السماء من ماء فأحيا به الأرض بعد موتها) ثم ذكر بعده دلائل الحيوان فقال (وبث فيها من كل دابة) ثم ذكر دلائل الآثار العلوية وذكر فيها نوعين : الرياح والسحاب . فقال : (وتصريف الرياح والسحاب المسخّر بين السماء والأرض ) ولما ذكر هذه الدلائل الثمانية قال : ( لآيات لقوم يعقلون). ونختم هذه الفصول بخاتمة عظيمة النفع، وهي أن الدلائل التي ذكرها الحكماء والمتكلمون وإن كانت كاملة قوية ، إلا أن هذه الطريقة المذكورة في القرآن عندي : أنها أقرب إلى الحق والصواب ، وذلك لأن تلك الدلائل دقيقة ، وبسبب ما فيها من الدقة انفتحت أبواب الشبهات ، وكثرت السؤالات .
وأما الطريق الوارد في القرآن فحاصله راجع إلى طريق واحد وهو المنع من التعمق ، والاحتراز عن فتح باب القيل والقال ، وحمل الفهم والعقل على الاستكثار من دلائل العالم الأعلى والأسفل. ومن ترك التعصب ، وجرب مثل تجربتي ، علم أن الحق ما ذكرته ، ولما ثبت أن هذا الطريق الذي ذكره الله في القرآن أنفع ، وفي القلوب أرجح (۱) ، لا جرم أفردنا له بابا مستقلا ، وهو القسم الثاني من هذا الكتاب. ونسأل الله تعالى الهداية والرحمة والإرشاد إلى الحق ، بفضله ، فإنه خير موفق ومعين
القسم الثاني من الجزء الأول
مية علوم لهذا الكتاب
في تفصيل الدلائل الدالة على وجود الإله القديم الدلائل الموجودة في عالم الأفلاك وعالم العناصر
So this verse includes eight types of evidence. The first three are celestial evidence, namely His saying Exalted: 'Indeed in the creation of the heavens and the earth and the alternation of night and day' and the remaining five types are evidence of the world of elements, namely His saying Exalted: 'And the ships which sail in the sea with what benefits people' then He mentioned after it evidence of plants, saying: 'And what Allah sent down from the sky of water and revived thereby the earth after its death' then He mentioned after it evidence of animals, saying: 'And scattered therein from every creature' then He mentioned evidence of upper atmospheric signs and mentioned in them two types: the winds and the clouds. So He said: 'And the directing of the winds and the clouds subservient between the heaven and earth.' And when He mentioned these eight types of evidence, He said: 'Signs for a people who reason.' And we conclude these chapters with a greatly beneficial conclusion: that the evidence which the philosophers and theologians mentioned, even if complete and strong, this method mentioned in the Qur'an is in my view closer to truth and correctness, because those evidences are subtle, and due to what is in them of subtlety, the doors of doubts opened and questions multiplied. As for the way that occurs in the Qur'an, its sum goes back to one way: the prohibition of deep investigation, and guarding against opening the door of idle talk, and directing understanding and intellect to increase the evidence of the higher and lower worlds. And whoever abandons partisanship, and tries like my experience, knows that the truth is what I mentioned. And since it is established that this way which Allah mentioned in the Qur'an is more beneficial and more preponderant in hearts, inevitably we have singled out for it an independent chapter, and it is the second section of this book. And we ask Allah Almighty for guidance, mercy, and direction to the truth by His grace, for He is the best granter of success and helper. The second section of the first part [؟] of the sciences of this book, on the detailed exposition of the evidences indicating the existence of the pre-eternal God, the evidences found in the world of the celestial bodies and the world of the elements.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage is a concluding discussion that first enumerates the eight types of evidence found in a specific Qur'anic verse, then explains why the Qur'anic method of argumentation is superior to that of the philosophers and theologians. The author begins by stating that the verse contains eight types of evidence: three celestial (creation of heavens and earth, alternation of night and day, and perhaps the ships—though the first three are explicitly celestial, the ships are part of the world of elements according to the classification; actually the text says the first three are celestial: 'خلق السموات والأرض', 'اختلاف الليل والنهار', and then a third? Wait, the text only lists two for celestial, but then says the five remaining are from the world of elements: ships (evidence of divine mercy and power), then plant evidence (rain reviving earth), then animal evidence (scattering creatures), then upper atmospheric signs: winds and clouds. That makes total of 2 celestial + 5 elements? But the text says first three are celestial, then five are elements. Likely the third celestial is implicit or a different count; the OCR may have an issue. Nonetheless, the author lists them in order. He then notes that the verse concludes 'signs for a people who reason'. Then the author gives his personal assessment: the evidences of the philosophers and theologians (hukama' and mutakallimun) are complete and strong, but their subtlety opens doors to doubts and many questions. In contrast, the Qur'anic method is one of prohibiting excessive depth (ta'ammuq), avoiding idle talk (qil wa qal), and instead directing the intellect to multiply evidence from the higher and lower worlds. This method is more beneficial and more firmly rooted in hearts. Therefore, the author has dedicated the second section of the book to this Qur'anic approach. He ends with a prayer for guidance. Finally, the heading of the second section is given: it will detail the evidences for God's existence from the celestial and elemental worlds.

Al-Razi extracts eight distinct evidences from a single Quranic verse, showing its comprehensive and multi-layered argument for God's existence.

?

?
The verse concludes that these are signs 'for a people who reason.' Are you truly reasoning over the verses you read, or are you just reciting? What specific step will you take this evening to actively reason over one sign from creation? Do not wait—do it tonight.

The Superiority of the Quranic Method
Al-Razi argues for the superiority of the Quranic method over philosophical/theological proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Razi considers the Quranic method of argumentation superior to that of philosophers and theologians.

Core Idea
The Quranic method avoids subtlety and directs the mind to abundant evidence, preventing doubt.

After listing the eight types of evidence, al-Razi offers a personal methodological reflection. He acknowledges that the proofs of philosophers and theologians are valid and strong, but he argues that the Qur'anic approach is more beneficial because it avoids excessive subtlety that opens doors to doubts. This lesson explores his reasoning and why he dedicates the second section of his book to this method.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Razi's conclusion
Read the passage where al-Razi contrasts the Qur'anic method with philosophical/theological methods. Note his concession that the latter are 'complete and strong'.
2
Identify his critique
Al-Razi says philosophical proofs are 'subtle' and this subtlety leads to 'doors of doubts' and many questions. Reflect on this critique.
He is not rejecting them, but evaluating their effectiveness.
3
Understand the Qur'anic alternative
The Qur'anic method prohibits deep speculation (ta'ammuq) and idle talk (qil wa qal), instead encouraging multiplication of evidence from the higher and lower worlds.
4
See the personal appeal
Al-Razi invites the reader to abandon partisanship and test his view. He then announces that the second section of his book exemplifies this method.
