The Multiplication of Persuasive Evidences Leading to Certainty
The author explains how multiplying persuasive evidences yields certainty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the multiplication of persuasive evidences can yield certainty, and why this method is especially important in theology.

Core Idea
Multiple persuasive proofs can build up to certainty.

The author begins this chapter by explaining a key methodological principle: although individual persuasive evidences only yield probability, gathering many of them can lead to firm conviction. This principle justifies the use of dialectical arguments in theology, where certainty is essential.

Guided Path
1
Read and classify evidences
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice the two categories of evidences: certain and persuasive. Understand that persuasive evidences are the focus.
The author classifies them as 'qat'iyya' and 'iqna'iyya'.
2
Trace the cumulative argument
Follow how a single persuasive evidence gives only probability (ẓann), but adding more strengthens it, eventually reaching certainty (yaqīn). This is the core logic.
The word 'ẓann' is key; it means probable belief.
3
Examine the citation from logic
Find the author's reference to the logician (ṣāḥib al-manṭiq) who supports that dialectical rules can reach certainty when probabilities strengthen.
This shows the method is rooted in established logical tradition.
4
Identify the theological application
Note that the author singles out knowledge of God (al-ilāh al-mudīr al-ḥakīm) as the subject most deserving of this cautious, cumulative approach.
This explains why the section focuses on clear, persuasive proofs.
5
Connect to the book's structure
Understand that this section differs from the earlier rational proofs; here the author gathers easily accessible evidences to maximize benefit.
The prayer at the end signals reliance on God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفضلات الأولي في بيان أن الاستكثار من هذه الدلائل من أهم المسوحات اعلم . أن الدلائل قد تكون قطعية ، وقد تكون إقناعية . والاستكثار من الدلائل الإقناعية قد ينتهي إلى إفادة القطع، وذلك لأن الدليل الإقناعي الواحد قد يفيد الظن ، فإذا انضم إليه دليل ثان ، قوي الظن ، وكلما سمع دليلا آخر ازداد الظن قوة ، وقد ينتهي بالآخرة إلى حصول الجزم واليقين وهذا المعنى هو أحد الفوائد التي ذكرها صاحب المنطق في تعليم قوانين الجدل . قال : « لأن القوانين الجدلية ، وإن أفادت الظن ، إلا أن تلك الظنون ، إذا قويت فقد تنتهي إلى حد اليقين ) . فيثبت : أن الجدل قد يقوم مقام البرهان في إفادة اليقين . وأولى المواضع برعاية الاحتياط فيه ، والمبالغة في التقرير، وإزالة الشكوك ، والشبهات : معرفة الإله المدير الحكيم، ولما بالغنا في تقرير الدلائل العقلية في القسم الأول ، أردنا أن نجمع الدلائل الظاهرة الجلية المتبادرة إلى الأفهام في هذا القسم ، ليكون ذلك سعيا في الفوز بهذه السعادة بأقصى ما يقدر عليه [ وبالله التوفيق ]
The first chapter on the statement that the multiplication of these evidences is among the most important efforts. Know that evidences may be certain, and may be persuasive. And the multiplication of persuasive evidences may end in producing certainty, because a single persuasive evidence may yield probability (ẓann), then when a second evidence is added to it, the probability strengthens, and every time he hears another evidence, the probability increases, and it may eventually result in obtaining conviction and certainty. And this meaning is one of the benefits that the author of Logic mentioned in teaching the rules of dialectic. He said: 'Because dialectical rules, although they yield probability, yet those probabilities, when they become strong, may reach the level of certainty.' So it is established that dialectic may stand in the place of demonstration (burhān) in producing certainty. And the most deserving of places for exercising caution therein, and for elaborating in presentation, and for removing doubts and ambiguities, is knowledge of the wise directing God (al-ilāh al-mudīr al-ḥakīm). And since we have elaborated in presenting the rational evidences in the first section, we intend to gather the clear, manifest evidences that readily come to minds in this section, so that this may be an effort to attain this happiness to the utmost extent possible. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage is an introductory explanation of the author's method for presenting evidences. It begins by classifying evidences into two types: certain (qat‘iyya) and persuasive (iqnā‘iyya). The author then argues that the multiplication of persuasive evidences can lead to certainty (yaqīn), because a single such evidence only gives probability (ẓann), but when another is added, the probability increases, and eventually it may become firm conviction. He supports this by citing the logician (ṣāḥib al-manṭiq) who states that dialectical rules, though they yield probability, can when strengthened reach the level of certainty. Thus, dialectic (jadal) can take the place of demonstration (burhān) in producing certainty. The author then identifies theology—knowledge of the wise directing God—as the most important subject for this cautious and thorough approach, because it is here that doubts and ambiguities must be removed. Finally, he explains that while the first section of the book focused on rational proofs, this section will gather clear, evident arguments that are readily understood, in order to maximize the seeker's chance of attaining spiritual happiness. The passage concludes with a prayer for divine assistance.

Key Terms
دلائل قطعية
دلائل إقناعية
ظن
يقين
جدل
برهان
إله مدير حكيم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Only demonstrative proofs (burhān) can yield certainty.
Correction: The author argues that multiplied persuasive proofs can also lead to certainty, as probabilities strengthen into conviction.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the benefit of multiplying persuasive evidences?
A) They become certain proofs.
B) They become useless.
C) They remain only probable.
D) They confuse the mind.

Key Takeaway
Multiplied persuasive evidences can attain certainty, especially in the knowledge of God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your own certainty in the existence and attributes of God, have you ever considered that your belief might be built on a single argument? How would actively seeking multiple evidences strengthen your conviction and remove lingering doubts? What is one evidence you can add today?
?
The author says the most deserving subject for this careful accumulation of proofs is knowledge of God. Yet in your daily life, do you give the same deliberate attention to building your faith as you do to other pursuits? Where have you settled for weak probability when you could have pursued certainty?
?
If someone challenged your belief in God and you relied on only one argument, what would happen if that argument were refuted? Are you prepared to multiply evidences, not just for yourself but for those you influence? What step will you take this week to study another proof from this section?

Dhul-Nun: Knowing God by God's Grace
Dhul-Nun states he knew God through God, with a tree parable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sufi perspective that knowledge of God is a divine gift, not merely human attainment, as articulated by Dhul-Nun al-Misri.

Core Idea
Knowledge of God comes directly from God, not from human effort alone.

This lesson explores the famous answer of Dhul-Nun al-Misri when asked how he knew his Lord. His response, 'I knew my Lord by my Lord,' encapsulates the Sufi emphasis on divine grace in the acquisition of ma'rifa. Through a vivid parable of a tree planted in the human spirit, he illustrates the spiritual practices that prepare the soul, but ultimately insists that without God's prior illumination, no knowledge is possible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice Dhul-Nun's direct answer first, then the extended parable. Let the text speak for itself before analysing.
The passage is short but dense with meaning.
2
Identify the components of the tree parable
List the four elements of the tree (soil, water, branches, fruit) and what each represents according to Dhul-Nun. This is the heart of his explanation.
Each element corresponds to a spiritual practice.
3
Reflect on the necessity of divine grace
Consider Dhul-Nun's conclusion: without God filling the spirits and illumining hearts, a human being—a handful of dust—cannot know God. Why does he emphasize this?
This is the key theological point.
4
Connect to the Prophetic hadith
Read the hadith cited at the end. How does it reinforce Dhul-Nun's message about dependence on God?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس عشر : قيل لذي النون المصري: بم عرفت ربك ؟ قال : عرفت ربي بربي ، ولولا ربي أخبرني لما عرفت ربي . واعلم أن معنى الكلام : أنه سبحانه غرس شجرة المعرفة في أراضي الأرواح ، فهذه الشجرة أرضها الطاعة ، وماؤها العبودية ، وأغصانها الذكر ، وثمرتها الفكر ، فمن واظب على هذه الأعمال وجد هذه الآثار ، ولولا أنه سبحانه ملأ الأرواح من معرفته ، وأنار القلوب بمحبته ، وإلا فكيف يليق بكف من التراب ، معرفة رب الأرباب ؟ وهذا الكلام الذي ذكره ذو النون مأخوذ من قول النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم : والله لولا الله ما اهتدينا * ولا تصدقنا ، ولا صلينا.
The fifteenth: It was said to Dhul-Nun al-Misri: By what did you know your Lord? He said: I knew my Lord by my Lord, and were it not that my Lord informed me, I would not have known my Lord. And know that the meaning of the speech is: He, may He be exalted, planted the tree of knowledge in the lands of the spirits. This tree: its soil is obedience, its water is servitude, its branches are remembrance, its fruit is reflection. Whoever persists in these deeds finds these effects. And were it not that He, may He be exalted, filled the spirits with His knowledge and illumined the hearts with His love, then how could a handful of dust befit the knowledge of the Lord of lords? This speech which Dhul-Nun mentioned is taken from the saying of the Prophet, peace be upon him: 'By Allah, were it not for Allah, we would not have been guided, nor given charity, nor prayed.'
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage presents the famous statement of Dhul-Nun al-Misri about the source of knowledge of God. When asked how he knew his Lord, he responds: 'I knew my Lord by my Lord; were it not that my Lord informed me, I would not have known my Lord.' This indicates that knowledge of God is not something humans can attain by their own power; it is a divine gift through illumination. The text then provides an explanatory allegory: God planted the tree of knowledge in the spirits. The tree's soil is obedience, water is servitude, branches are remembrance, and fruit is reflection. Consistency in these acts brings the effects. But ultimately, without God filling the spirits with His knowledge and enlightening hearts with His love, a handful of dust (the human) cannot know the Lord of lords. The statement is linked to a hadith of the Prophet acknowledging dependence on God. This passage emphasizes the primacy of divine grace in theological knowledge.

Key Terms

شجرة المعرفة
أرض الأرواح
الطاعة
العبودية
الذكر
الفكر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Dhul-Nun denies all human effort in knowing God.
Correction: He does not deny effort; he affirms that the capacity for effort and its effectiveness are God-given. The tree parable shows that obedience, servitude, etc., are necessary, but their success depends on divine illumination.

Checkpoint
According to Dhul-Nun, how did he come to know God?
A) By his own intellectual effort.
B) By studying nature.
C) By God informing him and by God's grace.
D) By following the scholars.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge of God is ultimately a gift from God, attained through divinely given means even when human effort is involved.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly see your knowledge of God—every insight, every moment of closeness—as a gift from Him, or do you slip into thinking it’s because of your own piety, intelligence, or effort? How would your prayer change tonight if you fully acknowledged that without Him informing you, you would know nothing?
?
The tree of knowledge has soil (obedience), water (servitude), branches (remembrance), and fruit (reflection). Which of these is most lacking in your daily life? What will you commit to starting or increasing—starting tomorrow morning—to water your own tree?
?
If God withdrew His illumination for even a second, your heart would be dark. Are you living in that awareness, or do you take your faith for granted? What one habit can you adopt to remind yourself that your very ability to seek Him is a grace you did not earn?

Dhul-Nun al-Misri: Knowing God by God
Dhul-Nun states that knowledge of God is through God, followed by a metaphorical explanation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that true knowledge of God is a gift from God, not acquired through independent effort, as illustrated by Dhul-Nun's metaphor.

Core Idea
Knowledge of God comes from God alone, not from human effort.

In this passage, Dhul-Nun al-Misri answers the profound question of how one knows God. His answer is striking: he knows God by God, not by his own reasoning. The author then unpacks this statement with a vivid metaphor of a tree of knowledge planted by God in human souls. This lesson explores that metaphor and its implications for how we approach knowing our Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get the overall meaning. Notice Dhul-Nun's terse statement and the extended metaphor that follows.
Watch for the contrast between 'by my Lord' and 'without my Lord informing me'.
2
Understand Dhul-Nun's core claim
Reflect on the statement 'I knew my Lord by my Lord.' What does it mean to know God through God? The commentary explains the key idea: human knowledge of God is a divine gift.
This claim challenges the notion that humans can arrive at knowledge of God through reason alone.
3
Analyze the tree metaphor
Examine each part of the tree (earth, water, branches, fruit) and what it represents (obedience, servitude, remembrance, contemplation). Consider how this metaphor supports Dhul-Nun's claim.
The metaphor shows that human actions are responses to God's prior planting of knowledge.

Source Text

The fifteenth: It was said to Dhul-Nun al-Misri: By what did you know your Lord? He said: I knew my Lord by my Lord, and if not for my Lord informing me, I would not have known my Lord. Commentary
This passage is a definition of the source of knowledge of God: it is from God Himself. Dhul-Nun states that he knows God by God, not by independent reasoning. The author then explains this metaphorically: God plants a tree of knowledge in souls, with earth (obedience), water (servitude), branches (remembrance), and fruit (contemplation). Without God's prior filling of souls with knowledge and love, humans cannot know Him. This is supported by a prophetic hadith saying without God there is no guidance.

?

Do you live in constant awareness that your faith, your prayers, your every good deed are only possible because He first guided you? Or do you sometimes feel self-sufficient? Knowing Yourself, Knowing Your Lord by Contrast
Narrates hadith and explains knowledge of God by contrast of attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how recognizing your own limitations and contingent attributes leads to knowing God's perfect attributes through contrast.

Core Idea
Your deficiency points to His perfection — this is knowledge by contrast.

This passage presents a profound argument: self-knowledge is not an end in itself but a means to know God. The Prophet (peace be upon him) taught that whoever knows himself knows his Lord. But how? The scholars clarify it is not because we are like God, but precisely because we are not. By examining our own originated, needy, and limited nature, we infer the opposite attributes in Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and context
Begin by reading the prophetic tradition and the Torah quote cited. Notice the claim: self-knowledge leads to knowledge of the Lord.
The hadith is famous but often misunderstood.
2
Understand the principle of contrast
The scholars emphasize that the connection is 'by way of contrast' (mukhalafah), not equality. This is the key interpretive move.
Contrast means opposite attributes.
3
Examine the four contrasts
The passage lists four pairs: originated vs eternal, possible vs necessary, composed vs one/unique, ignorant/incapable/needy vs knowledgeable/powerful/self-sufficient. Read each carefully.
Each contrast is a logical inference from self to God.
4
Apply the method to other attributes
The author says 'measure upon this category,' meaning you can extend the same reasoning to other human imperfections and divine perfections.
This makes the argument versatile.
5
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this argument shows both our dependence and God's perfection. It reinforces tawhid and reliance on Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التاسع عشر : روي عن النبي أنه قال : « من عرف نفسه فقد عرف ربه ، وجاء في التوراة : « يا إنسان اعرف نفسك تعرف ربك ) قال أهل التحقيق : من عرف نفسه فقد عرف ربه ، لا بطريق المساواة بل بطريق المخالفة ، يعني من عرف نفسه بالحدوث، عرف ربه بالقدم ، ومن عرف نفسه بالامكان عرف ربه بالوجوب ومن عرف نفسه بالتركيب [ والتأليف] عرف ربه بالوحدانية والفردانية ومن عرف [ نفسه ] بالجهل والعجز والحاجة ، عرف ربه بالعلم والقدرة والاستغناء. وعلى هذا الباب فقس
The nineteenth: It is narrated from the Prophet that he said: 'He who knows himself knows his Lord.' And it came in the Torah: 'O man, know yourself and you will know your Lord.' The people of verification said: He who knows himself knows his Lord, not by way of equality but by way of contrast. That is, he who knows himself as originated knows his Lord as eternal; he who knows himself as possible knows his Lord as necessary; he who knows himself as composed and constructed knows his Lord as oneness and uniqueness; and he who knows himself as ignorance, incapacity, and need knows his Lord as knowledge, power, and self-sufficiency. And upon this category, measure.
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage presents the nineteenth argument for the existence of God. It begins by citing the famous prophetic tradition 'He who knows himself knows his Lord,' along with a similar statement from the Torah. The author then explains how the people of verification interpret this: not in the sense that knowing oneself directly equals knowing God, but rather by way of contrast (mukhalafah). The argument is structured as a series of contrasts: our own attributes of originatedness (huduth) point to God's eternity (qidam); our possibility (imkan) points to God's necessity (wujub); our composition (tarkib) points to God's oneness (wahdaniyyah) and uniqueness (fardaniyyah); our ignorance (jahl), incapacity ('ajz), and need (hajah) point to God's knowledge ('ilm), power (qudrah), and self-sufficiency (istighna'). The conclusion is that by recognizing our own limitations, we logically infer the corresponding perfect attributes of God. The author then says 'measure upon this category' meaning this method can be applied to other attributes as well.

Key Terms
معرفة النفس
معرفة الرب
الحدوث
القدم
الإمكان
الوجوب
التركيب
الوحدانية
الفردانية
الجهل
العجز
الحاجة
العلم
القدرة
الاستغناء
المخالفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Knowing oneself means one becomes equal to God or partakes in divine essence.
Correction: The passage explicitly says 'not by way of equality but by way of contrast' — we know God by recognizing our opposites.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, how does knowing oneself lead to knowing one's Lord?
A) By realizing that one's own attributes are identical to God's attributes
B) By way of contrast: recognizing one's own deficiency points to God's perfection
C) By studying one's physical body as a proof of a creator
D) By direct intuition without any reasoning

Key Takeaway
By knowing ourselves as contingent, we know Allah as the Necessary Being through logical contrast.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Which of the four contrasts (origination, possibility, composition, or need) do you most resist acknowledging in yourself? What would it cost you to admit that you are entirely dependent on Allah in that area?
?
When you feel deeply inadequate—ignorant, weak, or in need—do you turn that feeling into a recognition of Allah's perfect knowledge, power, and sufficiency, or does it lead you to despair? What one change could help you make this contrast a reflex of gratitude?
?
If you truly believed that knowing your own limitations is the most direct route to knowing your Lord, how would you begin each day differently tomorrow morning?

Classification of Proofs: The First Group of Scholars
Author classifies proofs by scholarly groups, starting with historians.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's classification of proofs for God's existence and the distinctive approach of the first group of scholars: historians.

Core Idea
Different sciences lead to God; history is the first path.

The author begins a new chapter by emphasizing that many sciences exist, each with its own method for proving knowledge of Allah. He then starts enumerating these groups, beginning with the historians. This passage sets the stage for a series of proofs derived from different academic disciplines.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the classification structure: a general statement followed by the first specific group.
The passage is from the third chapter, indicating a new topic.
2
Identify the first group
Find the description of the first group: 'the scholars who research the histories of the people of the world.' Recognize that this group relies on historical evidence.
The author uses the term 'الطائفة الأولى' (the first group).
3
Note the two aspects
The passage concludes by saying their proof has two aspects. Although not detailed here, this prepares you for elaboration in the next lesson.
The two aspects will be explained in the following sections.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثالث - تحديد الدلائل التي تذكرها أصناف طوائف العالم. اعلم أن أصناف العلوم وأقسامها كثيرة، ولكل واحد من أصناف العلماء طريق يختص به في إثبات معرفة الله تعالى. ونحن نعدها: الطائفة الأولى: العلماء الباحثون عن تواريخ أهل الدنيا، ومعرفة الأحوال الماضية من أحوال هذا العالم: ودليل هذا الصنف من العلماء على إثبات الإله لهذا العالم من وجهين.
The third chapter - Specification of the proofs mentioned by the groups of the world's denominations. And we enumerate them: The first group: the scholars who research the histories of the people of the world, and knowledge of past conditions of this world. And the proof of this kind of scholars for establishing the deity for this world is from two aspects.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage is a classification. It begins a new chapter entitled 'Specification of the proofs mentioned by the groups of the world's denominations.' The author states that the sciences and their divisions are many, and each group of scholars has its own unique method for proving knowledge of Allah. Then he begins to enumerate them. The first group mentioned are the scholars who investigate history and past conditions. Their proof for establishing the existence of a deity for this world is based on two aspects (which will be explained later). The passage sets up the structure for a series of proofs from different groups.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author is presenting his own argument for God's existence here.
Correction: The author is not giving his own proof; he is categorizing different approaches used by various groups of scholars. The first group is the historians.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Interpreting Anthropomorphic Verses in Scripture
Author compares anthropomorphic verses and explains rule for interpreting ambiguous texts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why ambiguous scriptural descriptions of God's limbs must be interpreted metaphorically to align with clear verses affirming God's transcendence.

Core Idea
Clear verses forbid likening God to creation; ambiguous verses must yield.

In this lesson, we examine how scriptures describe God using human-like features (limbs) and abstract attributes (power, will). We see examples from the Torah, Gospel, and Quran, and then learn a key principle: when verses conflict, the clear ones (muhkam) that deny similarity override the ambiguous ones (mutashabih). This leads to metaphorical interpretation of anthropomorphic language.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of attributes
Read the passage and notice the distinction between 'attributes of limbs' (e.g., hand, foot, throne) and 'attributes of meanings' (e.g., power, will, justice). These appear in all three scriptures.
Attributes of limbs, if taken literally, imply God has a body.
2
Recognize the apparent contradiction
The author presents verses that describe God with limbs and verses that say 'nothing is like Him.' Understand why this creates a theological problem: affirming literal limbs would violate God's transcendence.
The author argues that affirming corporeality leads to anthropomorphism, which is prohibited.
3
Learn the solution: prioritize muhkam over mutashabih
The author explains that verses denying similitude are muhkam (clear, single meaning), while limb verses are mutashabih (ambiguous, two meanings). The clear verses take precedence, so limbs must be interpreted metaphorically as attributes like power.
This principle is known as ta'wil in Islamic theology.

واليد أيضا تفسر بالقدرة - والقدرة صفة معنى - فأي التفسيرين يتفق مع الحكم ؟ هل اليد الجارحة أم صفة القدرة ؟ بالتأكيد هي صفة القدرة . لأن اليد الجارحة تشبه وتمثل بأي مخلوق كان والنصر النافي للتمثيل يمنع من ذلك . وعلى ذلك فمراد الله تعالى من صفات أعضائه هوا التأويل إلى صفات معالي ، فاليد تؤول بالقدرة ، والاستواء بالقهر والغلبة وهكذا .
And transgression and sin, but He will by no means clear [the guilty]? [Exodus 34:7?] 2 - And in the Gospel we find like what is in the Torah concerning attributes of limbs and attributes of meanings. Concerning attributes of limbs, Jesus - peace be upon him - says: 'And do not swear at all, neither by heaven, for it is God's throne; nor by the earth, for it is his footstool' [Matthew 5:34-35]. So the apparent meaning of the text indicates that God Almighty sits on a throne in heaven, and that His feet are on the earth. Sitting entails corporeality, and the feet are among the attributes of limbs. Concerning attributes of meanings, we find many verses describing God Almighty as the Torah described Him with power, will, etc. Among that is the saying of Jesus - peace be upon him - 'God Almighty gave him authority over all flesh to give eternal life to everyone whom God wills to give him: And this is eternal life, that they know you, the only true God, and Jesus Christ whom you have sent. I have glorified you on earth' [John 17:2-4]. So Jesus' acknowledgment that God Almighty gave him authority indicates that He is powerful, and his acknowledgment that he glorified God and magnified Him indicates that God is described with all perfection and is free from every deficiency. And in the Noble Quran concerning attributes of limbs: 'And the hand of God is above their hands' [Al-Fath 10]; and concerning attributes of meanings: 'And We destroyed them and their people all together. So those are their houses, empty because of their wrongdoing. Indeed in that is a sign for a people who know' [An-Naml 51]. In this text is an affirmation of complete power, that He is just, and that He knows that and witnesses what happened from the knowers of the realities of history. The reader of the Torah, the Gospel, and the Quran finds verses indicating that God Almighty is 'not like anything' - that He is not like anything, that He is not a body. Because affirming corporeality for God Almighty entails size and form according to the measure of conceptions and imaginations. And if we conceive and imagine according to our minds of conceiving God Almighty and imagine Him by what we observe in life of creatures He created. And because He Almighty prohibited similitude, therefore He is not a body. And if we negate corporeality, it negates the limbs such as the hand and the foot and others. And how can we negate when there have come in the books verses [that are] contrary to their apparent meaning? [unclear] affirming limbs? And the answer to this question: The Torah has stated that He Almighty is not like anything, that is His saying in the thirty-third chapter of the Book of Deuteronomy: 'There is none like God' [Deut 33:26]; and likewise the Noble Quran states in His saying: 'There is nothing like unto Him' [Ash-Shura 11]. And the expressions that negate similitude are muhkam verses, i.e., they have one clear interpretation. And the verses of limbs are mutashabih verses, i.e., they have two interpretations. So the hand is interpreted as the physical hand which entails corporeality, and the hand is also interpreted as power - and power is an attribute of meaning. So which of the two interpretations agrees with the ruling? Is it the physical hand or the attribute of power? Certainly it is the attribute of power. Because the physical hand resembles and is likened to any creature, and the text negating likeness prevents that. Therefore the intent of God Almighty from His attributes of limbs is their interpretation into attributes of meanings. So the hand is interpreted as power, and istiwa' as subjugation and dominance, and so on.
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage presents a comparative analysis of scriptural anthropomorphic language and establishes a hermeneutical principle for interpreting such verses. The author first gives examples from the Gospel and Torah showing both 'attributes of limbs' (e.g., God's throne, footstool) and 'attributes of meanings' (e.g., power, will). Jesus' words about glorifying God are cited to show God's perfection and transcendence. Quranic examples follow: 'the hand of God' (limb attribute) and the story of destroyed people (implying power and justice). The author then argues that across scriptures there are verses affirming that God is unlike anything, implying He is not a body. The core argument is that affirming corporeality would entail size and form, which leads to anthropomorphism, prohibited by verses like 'There is nothing like unto Him.' The dilemma is posed: how can we negate limbs when scriptures appear to affirm them? The answer is that the negation verses (like 'not like anything') are muhkam (clear, definitive) with one interpretation, while the limb verses are mutashabih (ambiguous) with two possible interpretations: literal (physical) or metaphorical (power, etc.). Since the clear verses forbid similitude, the metaphorical interpretation must be adopted. Thus 'hand' is interpreted as power, 'istiwa'' as dominance. The author follows the classic Ash'ari method of tafwid or ta'wil, prioritizing the clear verses over the ambiguous.

Key Terms
آيات محكمة
آيات متشابهة
صفات الأعضاء
صفات المعاني
التأويل
التشبيه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ambiguous verses about God's limbs can be understood literally as body parts.
Correction: The clear verses denying similitude (muhkam) take precedence, so limb verses must be interpreted metaphorically as attributes like power and dominance.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how should the ambiguous verses (mutashabih) about God's attributes be interpreted?
A) Literally as body parts
B) As metaphors indicating attributes like power and sovereignty
C) As completely ignored
D) As only for the Torah

Key Takeaway
The proper method to interpret anthropomorphic language in scripture is to prioritize clear verses (muhkam) that affirm God's transcendence, leading to metaphorical interpretation (ta'wil) of ambiguous verses (mutashabih).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read verses that describe God's hand or throne, do you feel a tension in your heart? How do you reconcile the plain meaning with God's transcendence? Will you commit to seeking knowledge of ta'wil rather than settling for literalism?
?
The lesson shows that clear verses must rule over ambiguous ones. In your own life, do you prioritize clear commands of God over ambiguous desires? If not, what is holding you back from submitting fully?
?
Jesus is quoted acknowledging God's authority and glory. Have you truly made God the only object of your worship and glorification, or do you seek glory from others? How can you change that starting today?

Why God Uses Anthropomorphic Language
Author answers the objection of anthropomorphic language in revelation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the pedagogical reason why God uses anthropomorphic language in scripture.

Core Idea
God speaks to us according to our capacity to understand.

This lesson explores a common question: why does the Qur'an describe God with human-like attributes? The author presents the classical answer: God uses such language to make His essence comprehensible to finite minds, while His true reality remains transcendent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the first part of the passage (the question and answer) to grasp the argument.
Notice the question is posed rhetorically.
2
Understand 'taqrib al-dhat'
Identify the phrase 'تقريب ذاته' and reflect on its meaning: bringing God's essence close to human understanding.
3
Identify the role of muhkam verses
Notice that God also provided clear verses for scholars to discern His true intent.
This shows that not all verses are taken literally.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولماذا عبر الله تعالى بصفات الأعضاء عن نفسه وهو لا يريدها ؟ وهذا سؤال قد أجاب عنه كثيرون من العلماء بقولهم: إن الله تعالى يريد تقريب ذاته إلى عقول البشر ، حتى تقدر العقول أن تدرك الألوهية وتقربها . أما هو عز وجل فإنه أكبر وأجل . ولذلك خاطب البشر على قدر عقولهم ، ووضع للعلماء الآيات المحكمات ليدركوا قصد الله تعالى من محكم كتابه . وإذا قلنا : إن الله تعالى قال في التوراة ، فقولنا بحسب المكتوب فيها على طريقة الزام الخصم بما يعتقد به ، ويسلم به .
And why did God Almighty express Himself with attributes of limbs while He does not intend them? This is a question that many scholars have answered by saying: God Almighty wants to bring His essence close to the minds of humans, so that the minds may be able to apprehend divinity and come near it. But He, Mighty and Majestic, is greater and more exalted. Therefore He addressed humans according to the capacity of their minds, and placed for the scholars the muhkam verses so that they may grasp the intent of God Almighty from the clear part of His Book. And if we say: 'God Almighty said in the Torah,' then we say so according to what is written in it, by way of binding the opponent to what he believes and accepts.
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage addresses a potential objection: if God does not really possess physical limbs, why does He describe Himself with such language in scripture? The author presents the classical answer: God uses anthropomorphic expressions to make His essence comprehensible to human minds, which are limited and need familiar concepts to approach the divine. However, God is actually far greater than any image. He has given scholars the clear (muhkam) verses as a key to understand His true intent. The passage also notes a methodological principle: when debating with Christians or Jews, Muslims may cite the Torah or Gospel as they are written, but only to argue against the opponent based on their own accepted scriptures; this is a dialectical tactic (ilzam al-khasm) rather than an endorsement of textual corruption.

Key Terms
تقريب الذات
المحكم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Anthropomorphic language means God has physical parts
Correction: The language is pedagogical, not literal. God is transcendent.

Checkpoint
Why does God use anthropomorphic language according to the author?
A) Because God actually has physical parts
B) To make His essence understandable to human minds
C) To deceive the ignorant
D) Only for the Torah

Key Takeaway
God uses anthropomorphic language not literally, but to make His essence accessible to human minds.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read verses describing God's hand or throne, do you feel a tension between your human understanding and God's transcendence? How does this lesson's explanation change your posture toward those verses?
?
The scholars in this passage are given muhkam verses to grasp God's intent. Have you personally wrestled with any ambiguous verses? What does this lesson challenge you to do next time you encounter them?
?
If God addresses you according to your capacity, how does that affect the way you seek knowledge of Him? Are you content with a surface understanding, or do you strive to reach the depth the muhkam verses offer?

The Method of Arguing with Opponents: Ilzam al-Khasm
Author explains the method of arguing with opponents by citing their own scriptures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dialectical method of citing scriptures of opponents to bind them to their own beliefs.

Core Idea
We cite other scriptures to bind opponents to their own accepted texts.

This lesson unpacks a methodological principle for debate: when Muslims refer to the Torah or Gospel, they do so according to what is written, using it as a tool to bind the opponent to his own beliefs. This is not an endorsement of textual corruption.

Guided Path
1
Read the final sentence
Read the sentence about the Torah. What is the context?
It comes after discussing anthropomorphic language.
2
Learn the term 'ilzam al-khasm'
Understand that this is a debating technique: using the opponent's own scripture to prove a point.
3
Apply the principle
Think of a real or hypothetical scenario where this method might be used.

Source Text

ولماذا عبر الله تعالى بصفات الأعضاء عن نفسه وهو لا يريدها ؟ وهذا سؤال قد أجاب عنه كثيرون من العلماء بقولهم: إن الله تعالى يريد تقريب ذاته إلى عقول البشر ، حتى تقدر العقول أن تدرك الألوهية وتقربها . أما هو عز وجل فإنه أكبر وأجل . ولذلك خاطب البشر على قدر عقولهم ، ووضع للعلماء الآيات المحكمات ليدركوا قصد الله تعالى من محكم كتابه . وإذا قلنا : إن الله تعالى قال في التوراة ، فقولنا بحسب المكتوب فيها على طريقة الزام الخصم بما يعتقد به ، ويسلم به .
And why did God Almighty express Himself with attributes of limbs while He does not intend them? This is a question that many scholars have answered by saying: God Almighty wants to bring His essence close to the minds of humans, so that the minds may be able to apprehend divinity and come near it. But He, Mighty and Majestic, is greater and more exalted. Therefore He addressed humans according to the capacity of their minds, and placed for the scholars the muhkam verses so that they may grasp the intent of God Almighty from the clear part of His Book. And if we say: 'God Almighty said in the Torah,' then we say so according to what is written in it, by way of binding the opponent to what he believes and accepts.
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage addresses a potential objection: if God does not really possess physical limbs, why does He describe Himself with such language in scripture? The author presents the classical answer: God uses anthropomorphic expressions to make His essence comprehensible to human minds, which are limited and need familiar concepts to approach the divine. However, God is actually far greater than any image. He has given scholars the clear (muhkam) verses as a key to understand His true intent. The passage also notes a methodological principle: when debating with Christians or Jews, Muslims may cite the Torah or Gospel as they are written, but only to argue against the opponent based on their own accepted scriptures; this is a dialectical tactic (ilzam al-khasm) rather than an endorsement of textual corruption.

Misconception & Correction

Correction: Muslims cite it only as a dialectical tool, not as a validation.

Classification of Beings from Spiritual Unveiling
Classification of existents into spiritual/corporeal and luminous/dark.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the people of unveilings classify existents into spiritual and corporeal, and within the corporeal world, identify luminous bodies and their hierarchy.

Core Idea
The sun is the noblest luminous body because it dispels darkness.

The second type of proofs relies on spiritual unveiling. Before drawing theological conclusions, the author establishes a clear classification of the physical world. This classification lays the groundwork for analogical reasoning about spiritual realities.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own classification come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise style and the enumeration.
2
Identify the two main categories of existents
The text states that existents are either spiritual or corporeal. These are the primary divisions.
Spiritual are not elaborated here, but corporeal are.
3
See the subdivision of corporeal bodies
Corporeal bodies are subdivided into luminous and dark. Then four luminous bodies are listed: sun, moon, stars, and fire.
The dark ones are not named, only mentioned.
4
Focus on the sun's unique status
The sun is singled out as the highest and noblest because it is the source of overpowering light and manifest radiance, and at its rising darkness disappears.
This establishes a hierarchy within luminous bodies.
5
Reflect on the argument's purpose
Consider why this classification matters for proofs. The physical hierarchy likely serves as an analogy for spiritual illumination and dependence.
The passage is cut off, but the structure is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثاني (١) من دلائل أصحاب المكاشفات، وأرباب المجاهدات :
قالوا : قد ثبت أن الموجودات إما روحانية، وإما جسمانية
أربعة :
·
فنقول : أما عالم الأجسام فهي إما نيرة وإما مظلمة. وأما النيرات فهي الشمس، والقمر، والكواكب، والنار. وأعلى هذه الأربعة وأشرفها هو الشمس، لأنها ينبوع النور القاهر، والضوء الظاهر وعند طلوعها تضمحل
The second type (1) of proofs from the people of unveilings and the masters of spiritual struggles: They said: It is established that existents are either spiritual or corporeal. Four: · We say: As for the world of bodies, they are either luminous or dark. As for the luminous ones, they are the sun, the moon, the stars, and fire. The highest of these four and the noblest is the sun, because it is the source of overpowering light and the manifest radiance, and at its rising, [darkness] disappears.
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage marks the beginning of a new section: the second type of proofs from the people of spiritual unveiling (مكاشفات) and spiritual struggles (مجاهدات). The author first states the foundational classification made by these practitioners: 'existents are either spiritual or corporeal.' Then an enumeration is given: 'Four' with a bullet point, which presumably lists the four luminous bodies, but the text directly proceeds to: 'As for the world of bodies, they are either luminous or dark.' So the 'four' likely refers to the number of luminous bodies that follow: the sun, the moon, the stars, and fire. Among these, the sun is singled out as the highest and noblest because it is the source of overpowering light (ينبوع النور القاهر) and manifest radiance (الضوء الظاهر). When it rises, darkness dissipates. This is a typical argument from natural theology: comparing physical lights to spiritual illumination. The passage is cut off before completing the argument, but it establishes a hierarchy within the physical world that will presumably be used analogically for spiritual realities.

المجاهدات (spiritual struggles)
الموجودات (existents)
روحانية (spiritual)
جسمانية (corporeal)
نيرة (luminous)
مظلمة (dark)
ينبوع النور القاهر (source of overpowering light)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: People of unveilings rely purely on mystical experience without rational structure.
Correction: This passage shows they present a logical classification (spiritual/corporeal, luminous/dark) as the basis for their proofs.

Checkpoint
According to the second type of proofs, what are the four luminous bodies?
A) Sun, moon, stars, and fire
B) Sun, moon, stars, and planets
C) Sun, moon, fire, and light
D) Sun, stars, fire, and lightning

Key Takeaway
The classification of beings into spiritual and corporeal, and the hierarchy within luminous bodies, forms the basis for proofs from spiritual unveiling.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage declares the sun the highest among luminous bodies because it is the source of light that dispels darkness. In your own spiritual journey, what is your 'sun'—the source you rely on to overcome ignorance and sin? Are you truly turning to it, or are you settling for lesser lights? What will you change today to orient your life around that ultimate source?
?
The people of unveilings built their proofs on a rational classification. Does your faith rest on a clear foundation of knowledge, or is it built on vague feelings? What specific step can you take this week to strengthen your understanding of the proofs for God's existence and attributes, so that your conviction matches the clarity of this classification?

Introduction to the Third Section: Key Metaphysical Topics
Section heading listing five key metaphysical topics.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scope of the third section of the book and the key metaphysical topics it covers.

Core Idea
The third section maps out five foundational metaphysical concepts for theological investigation.

This brief heading serves as a gateway to the core of Islamic metaphysical inquiry. The author lists the essential topics that will be examined, setting the stage for a detailed discussion. As we read, we should note how each term relates to the broader framework of kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic heading and its translation. Notice the five terms listed: necessity, existence, wills, determination, and essence.

These concepts will be elaborated in subsequent sections.

The phrase 'and what resembles them' indicates that similar topics are also included.

Two Meanings of 'Essence as Such'
Sanusi introduces two interpretations of 'essence as such'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the correct interpretation of the statement 'the essence as such is neither existent nor non-existent' and why the alternative interpretation is false.

Core Idea
The essence is conceptually distinct from existence and non-existence, not devoid of both.

This lesson addresses a crucial methodological distinction in kalam. The author clarifies a famous phrase often misunderstood: 'the essence as such is neither existent nor non-existent.' Before proceeding to the main inquiry about the First Principle, we must understand what this phrase truly means.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two interpretations
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice that the author presents two possible meanings of the phrase 'the essence as such is neither existent nor non-existent'.
2
Examine the triangle example and its proof
Focus on the triangle example. The author uses it to show that the concept of triangle is different from the concepts of existence and non-existence. Understand why this supports only the first interpretation.
The proof relies on the fact that we can conceive triangle without thinking about existence or non-existence.
3
Recognize the false interpretation
The second interpretation claims that essence is literally devoid of both existence and non-existence. The author states this is false. Reflect on why this is problematic for theological reasoning.
The passage cuts off, but the falsehood is clear from the triangle example: essence is not empty of attributes, just conceptually distinct.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الرابعة
في
تحقيق القول في بيان أن المبدأ الأول. هل هو تلك الحقيقة المخصوصة ؟
اعلم . أن هذا البحث يجب أن يكون مسبوقا بمقدمة . وهي : أنا نقول : أنا إذا قلنا : الماهية الفلانية من حيث هي هي ، لا موجودة ولا معدومة فهذا الكلام يحتمل وجهين :

الثاني : أن يكون المراد أن الماهية الفلانية خالية عن الوجود وعن العدم والمفهوم الأول حق وأما المفهوم الثاني باطل. لأنا إذا اعتبرنا المثلث من حيث إنه مثلث ، فهذا الاعتبار من حيث إنه هو ، مغاير لكونه موجودا ، أو لكونه معدوما . والدليل : أن المفهوم من لفظ الموجود ومن لفظ المعدوم مغاير للمفهوم لفظ المثلث . بدليل : أنه يصح تعقل الموجود وتعقل المعدوم حال الذهول عن تعقل معنى المثلث ، وكذلك يحصل أيضا تعقل المثلث من حيث إنه مثلث مع الذهول عن كونه موجوداً أو عن كونه معدوماً. فظهر بهذا أن كونه مثلثا غير وموجوداً أو معدوماً غير وأما الاحتمال الثاني وهو
The Fourth Problem: On the verification of the statement in explaining whether the First Principle is that specific reality. Know that this inquiry must be preceded by a preamble, which is: We say: When we say: 'Such-and-such an essence insofar as it is itself is neither existent nor non-existent,' this statement admits of two interpretations: One of them: that the intended meaning is that such-and-such an essence insofar as it is itself is an expression other than its being existent or non-existent. The second: that the intended meaning is that such-and-such an essence is devoid of existence and non-existence. The first concept is true, and the second concept is false. Because if we consider the triangle insofar as it is a triangle, this consideration insofar as it is itself is other than its being existent or its being non-existent. The proof: that the concept of the word 'existent' and the concept of the word 'non-existent' are other than the concept of the word 'triangle'. The evidence: that it is valid to conceive the existent and conceive the non-existent while being oblivious to conceiving the meaning of triangle, and likewise the conception of the triangle insofar as it is a triangle also occurs while being oblivious to its being existent or its being non-existent. Thus it appears from this that its being a triangle is other than its being existent or non-existent. As for the second interpretation, it is...
Source pages p. 312

Commentary
This passage introduces the fourth problem, which asks whether the First Principle is that specific reality. The author states that this inquiry must be preceded by a preamble distinguishing two interpretations of the common statement 'the essence as such is neither existent nor non-existent.' The first interpretation is that this statement merely indicates that the essence's self is other than its being existent or non-existent—denoting a conceptual distinction (al-maghayara). The second interpretation is that it means the essence is literally free from both existence and non-existence, which is false. The author proves the first interpretation using the example of a triangle: the concept of 'triangle' is distinct from the concepts of 'existent' and 'non-existent', as one can conceive a triangle without thinking about its existence or non-existence, and vice versa. This shows that being a triangle is other than being existent or non-existent. The second interpretation (that the essence is devoid of both) is mentioned but not fully discussed here as the text cuts off.

Correction: The author clarifies that this statement only indicates conceptual distinction; the essence is not devoid of both, but its concept is other than the concepts of existence and non-existence.

The Argument from Infinite Regress for Self-Difference
Sanusi presents the first proof that essences can differ by themselves, avoiding infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of the first proof that essences can differ by themselves, and why the infinite regress is rejected.

Core Idea
If difference requires an infinite chain, then self-difference is the only option.

Sanusi begins his first proof by setting up a conditional scenario. He supposes that essences are equal in essentiality and only differ through attributes. Then he examines whether those attributes can themselves differ, leading to either an infinite regress or self-difference. This argument establishes a key premise for the broader discussion.

Guided Path
1
Understand the supposition
Start by grasping the hypothetical scenario: essences are assumed equal in essentiality, and their difference is attributed to their associated attributes.
This is a conditional argument, not an assertion of fact.
2
Examine the attributes' difference
Consider whether those attributes themselves are different from each other or not. This is the key fork in the argument.
3
Reject the two problematic outcomes
If the attributes are not different, they cannot cause difference in essences. If they are different, the difference must be either from other attributes (leading to infinite regress) or from themselves. Infinite regress is impossible.
The impossibility of infinite regress is a key logical principle.
4
Accept the conclusion
Since the only remaining possibility is that the attributes differ by themselves, it follows that self-difference is possible. This supports the main claim.
The conclusion is that it is not impossible for things to be different by themselves.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والذي يدل على صحة ما ذكرناه وجوه: الأول: إنا لو فرضنا كون الذوات متساوية في الذاتية، وفرضنا أنه إنما يخالف بعضها بعضا لأجل الصفات القائمة بها، فتلك الصفات إما أن يكون بعضها مخالف للبعض، أو لا يكون. والثاني باطل لأن تلك الصفات إذا لم تكن متخالفة، استحال ضرورة أن تكون تلك الذوات متخالفة لأجلها، وإن كانت تلك الصفات متخالفة فاختلافها إما أن يكون لصفات أخرى، وحينئذ يلزم منه التسلسل، وهو محال. وإن كان اختلافها لأنفسها ولأعيانها، فحينئذ تكون تلك الصفات متخالفة لأنفسها من غير اعتبار صفة أخرى، وذلك يدل على أنه لا يمتنع كون تلك الأشياء مختلفة لأنفسها ولأعيانها.
What indicates the correctness of what we have mentioned are arguments: The first: if we suppose that essences are equal in essentiality, and we suppose that they only differ from one another due to attributes subsisting in them, then those attributes are either such that some are different from others, or they are not. The second is false, because if those attributes are not mutually different, it is impossible necessarily that those essences become different on account of them. And if those attributes are mutually different, then their difference is either due to other attributes, in which case infinite regress (tasalsul) follows, which is impossible; or their difference is due to themselves and their identities, then those attributes are mutually different by themselves without consideration of another attribute. This indicates that it is not impossible for those things to be different by themselves and their identities.
Source pages p. 314

Commentary
This passage is the first proof (الحجة الأولى) supporting the correctness of the claim that essences can differ by themselves. It proceeds as a conditional argument. It supposes that essences are equal in their essentiality and that they differ only by attributes. Then it considers the attributes: either they are themselves different from each other or they are not. If they are not different, they cannot cause differences in essences. If they are different, then that difference must be either due to other attributes (leading to infinite regress) or due to themselves. Since infinite regress is impossible, it must be that difference is due to themselves. Therefore, difference by self is possible, establishing the needed premise.

Key Terms
التسلسل
المخالفة
الصفات
الذوات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Infinite regress is sometimes considered acceptable or neutral.
Correction: In this context, infinite regress is deemed impossible (محال) because it would lead to an endless chain of causes for difference, which is logically untenable.

Checkpoint
In the first proof, what is the unacceptable consequence if the attributes are different from each other due to other attributes?
A) They would be the same
B) Infinite regress
C) They would be self-different
D) They would be attributes of God

Key Takeaway
The first proof shows that self-difference is necessary to avoid infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The argument uses logical impossibility to lead to a conclusion. Have you ever rejected a truth of Islam because it seemed 'counterintuitive'? How does this logical rigor of Sanusi push you to submit your intellect to the truth, even when it contradicts your initial assumptions?

Third Proof: Similarity and Difference Are Intrinsic
Sanusi critiques metaphorical speech about similarity/difference and proposes a part-based analysis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's argument that similarity and difference between things are caused by their own realities, not by additional attributes.

Core Idea
Similarity and difference come from the very nature of things, not from extra attributes.

In this third proof, Sanusi examines a common way of speaking—that two things are alike in some ways and different in others. He shows that such language is actually metaphorical, because if the same aspect explained both similarity and difference, it would be contradictory. He then provides a precise analysis: each thing is composed of parts, and the parts themselves account for the resemblance or difference. This argument reinforces the central claim that attributes are intrinsic, not added.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Sanusi uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify the common view and its critique
The passage starts with the statement 'this thing resembles that thing in some respects and differs in other respects.' Sanusi calls this metaphorical. Ask yourself: why is it metaphorical? What would go wrong if taken literally?
The key is the 'contradiction' (اجتماع النقيضين) that would follow.
3
Understand the part-based analysis
Sanusi replaces the metaphorical speech with a precise model: each thing is composed of two parts. One part from each thing shares the same reality (causing similarity), and the other parts have totally different realities (causing difference). Trace this model with a concrete example.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The conclusion is clear: similarity and difference are by the complete realities and quiddities themselves, not by any additional attribute. Reflect on how this conclusion supports the overall doctrine of the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة في صحة هذا المطلوب: إنا إذا قلنا: إن هذا الشيء يماثل ذلك الشيء من بعض الوجوه ويخالفه من سائر الوجوه. فهذا كلام مجازي. وذلك لأن الوجه الذي لأجله حصلت المشاركة مغاير للوجه الذي لأجله حصلت المخالفة، وإلا لزم اجتماع النقيضين في الشيء الواحد وهو محال. بل الحق أن كل واحد من هذين الشيئين مركب من جزءين. وأحد الجزءين من أحد الجانبين يماثل الجزء الآخر من الجانب الثاني مماثلة في حقيقتهما ومساواة في ماهيتهما، والجزء الثاني من الجانب الأول يخالف الجزء الثاني من الجانب الثاني مخالفة تامة، لنفس الحقيقتين. وإذا عرفت هذا فقد ظهر أن الشيئين اللذين [تماثلا فإنما] يتماثلان التمام حقيقتهما ولنفس ماهيتهما، لا لصفة زائدة، وأن الشيئين اللذين يختلفان [فإنما يختلفان] لتمام ...
The third proof for the correctness of this claim: if we say that this thing resembles that thing in some respects and differs from it in other respects, this is metaphorical speech. That is because the aspect by which similarity occurs is different from the aspect by which difference occurs; otherwise the combination of two contradictories in one thing would follow, which is impossible. Rather, the truth is that each one of these two things is composed of two parts. One of the two parts from one side resembles the other part from the second side with a resemblance in their reality and equality in their quiddity, and the second part from the first side differs from the second part from the second side with a total difference, by the two realities themselves. And when you understand this, it becomes apparent that the two things which [resemble each other] resemble each other by the entirety of their reality and by their very quiddity, not by an additional attribute, and that the two things which differ [differ] by the entirety...
Source pages p. 315

Commentary
This passage is the third proof (الحجة الثالثة). It analyzes the common statement that two things are alike in some aspects and different in others. The author points out that this is metaphorical, because if the same aspect were the cause of both similarity and difference, that would be contradictory. In reality, each thing is composed of parts: one part accounts for similarity with the other thing's corresponding part (by identical reality and quiddity), and another part accounts for difference (by complete opposition). Thus, similarity and difference occur by the things' own realities, not by additional attributes. The proof is incomplete at the end of the window, but the main idea is clear: similarity and difference are intrinsic.

Key Terms
التماثل
المخالفة
التركيب
الجزء
النقيضين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That similarity and difference are explained by additional attributes that attach to things.
Correction: The proof shows they are by the very nature and reality of the parts themselves, not by added attributes.

Checkpoint

A) It would be miraculous
B) It would entail contradiction
C) It would be possible
D) It would require an attribute

Key Takeaway
Similarity and difference are grounded in the very realities of things, not in additional attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you catch yourself thinking, 'I am like this person in some ways but different in others,' are you really looking at your intrinsic reality, or are you hiding behind superficial attributes? What would it take for you to see yourself as a composite of real parts given by Allah, not just a jumble of relative comparisons?  Commit today to identify one essential quality Allah has placed in you that is not relative to others.
?
The proof shows that the same aspect cannot cause both similarity and difference without contradiction. In your life, are there areas where you hold opposing judgments about yourself—like 'I am good in this way but bad in that way' without realizing the contradiction?  How can recognizing distinct aspects help you harmonize your self-understanding?  Write down one such contradiction and resolve it by acknowledging the different parts.

Conclusion: The Argument Fails to Prove Necessity
States that the argument does not prove the creator is eternal and necessary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's final verdict that the argument under critique fails to demonstrate the creator's eternity and necessary existence.

Core Idea
The proof does not establish the creator as eternal and necessary.

After a series of objections, the author delivers the conclusive verdict: the argument under scrutiny does not achieve its intended goal. This short but decisive passage sums up the entire critique. It reminds us that a failed argument does not mean the conclusion is false, only that the reasoning is insufficient.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the English translation. Notice the decisive tone of the conclusion.
2
Identify the core assertion
The passage asserts that the proof does not at all establish that the creator is eternal and necessary in Himself. This is the key claim.
Pay attention to the phrase 'upon all assumptions' — the failure is universal.
3
Connect to the broader context
Understand that this passage is the summary conclusion drawn from preceding objections. It serves as a turning point in the discussion.
The author is not rejecting the truth of the conclusion, only the validity of this specific argument.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider what this means for the overall project: if this argument fails, what other methods can be used to establish the same truths?
This sets the stage for alternative epistemological approaches.

And in summary: upon all assumptions, it is established that the proof they mentioned does not at all establish that the creator of this corporeal world is eternal, pre-eternal, necessary in Himself.

The author asserts that regardless of which assumption or possibility is considered, the proof offered by the opponents fails to definitively demonstrate that the creator of the corporeal world is eternal and necessary in essence. This sets the stage for the alternative epistemological methods that follow.

Two Methods for Attaining Divine Knowledge
Introduces two approaches to acquiring divine knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two distinct methodological approaches to acquiring divine knowledge introduced by the author.

Core Idea
Divine knowledge can be approached either through the worthier-and-more-fitting method or through certain proofs.

This lesson introduces a foundational distinction in Islamic theology: how do we come to know Allah? The author presents two contrasting schools of thought. One relies on the principle of what is most befitting of God, while the other insists on demonstrative certainty. Understanding this split is key to following the argument that follows.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the author's use of 'stations' (maqāmān) to describe these methods.
2
Identify the two stations
The passage lists two methods. Find each one and note how each is described. The first is tied to 'al-awlā wa al-akhlaq' and the second to 'dalā'il yaqīniyya'.
3
Ponder the difference in approach
Ask yourself: what does each method prioritize? The commentary will expand on the significance of these two paths.
This connects to a major debate in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أنه حصل ههنا مقامان : أحدهما : طريقة القائلين بأنه لا سبيل إلى تحصيل المعارف الإلهية إلا بالتمسك بطريقة الأولى والأخلق . والثاني : طريقة القائلين بأنه لا بد من الدلائل اليقينية
And know that here there are two stations: one: the method of those who say that there is no way to acquire divine knowledge except by adhering to the method of the worthier and more fitting. And the second: the method of those who say that there is no alternative to certain proofs.
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage introduces a new section contrasting two epistemological approaches to divine knowledge. The first method relies on the principle of 'the worthier and more fitting' (al-awlā wa al-akhlaq), which presumably argues from what is most befitting of the divine. The second insists on certain and definitive proofs (dalā'il yaqīniyya). The author will next explain the first method in detail.

Key Terms

طريقة الأولى والأخلق
دلائل يقينية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the two methods are conflicting and incompatible.
Correction: The text presents them as two different approaches that may be complementary rather than contradictory.

Checkpoint
How many methods for acquiring divine knowledge are introduced?
A) One
B) Two
C) Three
D) Four

Key Takeaway
The author introduces two fundamental approaches to divine knowledge: one based on divine worthiness and one based on certain proofs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you seek to know Allah, do you lean more towards rational proofs or towards reflecting on what is most befitting of Him? Which method do you neglect? How might that imbalance affect your certainty in faith?
?
The text presents two stations for attaining divine knowledge. Which station do you find yourself in most often? Are you content with that, or does this passage challenge you to explore the other method?
?
If you were to only use the method of the worthier without certain proofs, what risks might you face in your understanding of Allah? Conversely, if you rely only on proofs, what might you miss about His attributes? What will you do today to incorporate both approaches in your pursuit of divine knowledge?

Method of Learning
intermediate
6 min
Two Methods for Divine Knowledge and the First Method
Introduces two methods for divine knowledge and elaborates the first method (الأولى والأخلق).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two approaches to divine knowledge in Islamic theology and the reasoning behind the method of 'the first and more worthy' (al-awla wa al-akhlaq) which employs a parsimony argument regarding intermediaries.

Core Idea
The first method argues that since only a Necessary Being is proven, any intermediaries are superfluous.

This passage from al-Matalib al-Aliyah sets out two approaches to proving God's existence. The first, known as the method of 'the first and more worthy', bases itself on the principle of parsimony: once the existence of a Necessary Being is established, any intermediaries in the causal chain are not required. The second approach demands certain proofs. We will explore the first method in detail.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
Read the opening line where two positions are introduced. Notice that the first is called 'way of al-awla wa al-akhlaq' and the second insists on certain proofs.
The term 'al-awla wa al-akhlaq' literally means 'the first and more worthy'.
2
Understand the causal argument
The passage presents a causal argument: the world is originated, so it needs a maker. If that maker were also originated, it would need another, leading to an infinite regress or a cycle, both invalid.
So the chain must end with a being that is eternal and necessary.
3
Apply the principle of parsimony
The argument then says: since the proofs only establish the Necessary Being, and do not prove any intermediaries, those intermediaries should be dropped. This is the core of the method.
This is similar to Ockham's razor.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this method is called 'the first and more worthy' — it is seen as more straightforward and simpler.
It avoids unnecessary metaphysical entities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أنه حصل ههنا مقامان : أحدهما : طريقة القائلين بأنه لا سبيل إلى تحصيل المعارف الإلهية إلا بالتمسك بطريقة الأولى والأخلق والثاني : طريقة القائلين بأنه لا بد من الدلائل اليقينية أما الطريق الأول : فتقريره : أن يقال : إنه لما ثبت أن العالم محدث ، وجب افتقاره إلى فاعل، فذلك الفاعل إن كان محدثا ، افتقر إلى فاعل آخر فإما أن يتسلسل ، أو يدور ، أو ينتهي بالآخر إلى فاعل قديم واجب الوجود لذاته . أما الدور والتسلسل فهما باطلان (۲) وأما إثبات الوسائط مع الانتهاء إلى موجود واجب الوجود لذاته [فنقول : لما دلت الدلائل على أنه لا بد من الاعتراف بوجود واجب الوجود لذاته] (٣) ولم تدل الدلائل على وجود هذه الوسائط ، وجب إسقاط هذه الوسائط من البين ، والاكتفاء بالموجـود الذي هو واجب الوجود لذاته . فهذه طريقة القائلين بالأولى والأخلق .
And know that there are two positions here: One of them: the way of those who say that there is no way to acquire divine knowledge except by adhering to the method of the first and more worthy. And the second: the way of those who say that certain proofs are necessary. As for the first way: its exposition is that it is said: Since it is established that the world is temporally originated, it must need a maker. If that maker is temporally originated, it needs another maker, so either there is an infinite chain, or a cycle, or it ends with the last in an eternal maker necessary of existence in itself. As for the cycle and infinite chain, they are invalid. And as for establishing intermediaries while ending in a being necessary of existence in itself, we say: Since the proofs indicate that it is necessary to acknowledge the existence of the necessary of existence in itself, and the proofs do not indicate the existence of these intermediaries, it is necessary to drop these intermediaries from the middle and to be content with the being that is necessary of existence in itself. This is the method of those who say the first and more worthy.
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage first identifies two positions (maqaman) regarding the attainment of divine knowledge (al-ma'arif al-ilahiyya). The first is the method of 'the first and more worthy' (tariqat al-awla wa al-akhlaq), which holds that one must adhere to this principle. The second is the method that insists on certain proofs (al-dala'il al-yaqiniyya). The author then explains the first method in detail: since the world is temporally originated (muhdath), it requires a maker (fa'il). If that maker were also originated, it would require another maker, leading to an infinite regress (tasalsul) or a cycle (dawr), both of which are invalid. Therefore, the chain must end with an eternal maker that is necessary of existence in itself (wajib al-wujud li-dhatihi). The proponents then argue: since the proofs necessitate acknowledging a necessary being, but they do not necessitate the existence of any intermediaries (al-wasa'it) between the world and that necessary being, those intermediaries should be dropped, and we should be content with the necessary being alone. This is the essence of the method of 'the first and more worthy'.

Key Terms
المعارف الإلهية
طريقة الأولى والأخلق
دلائل يقينية
محدث
تسلسل
دور
وسائط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That al-awla wa al-akhlaq is a distinct argument for the existence of God, similar to the cosmological argument with multiple steps.
Correction: It is actually a principle of parsimony (like Ockham's razor) applied after establishing a Necessary Being through other proofs, to eliminate unnecessary intermediaries.

Checkpoint
According to the first method (طريقة الأولى والأخلق), why should intermediaries be dropped from the causal chain?
A) Because the proofs prove that intermediaries are impossible.
B) Because the proofs indicate the necessary being but not the intermediaries, so they are superfluous.
C) Because the world is not originated.
D) Because the cycle and infinite regress are valid.

Key Takeaway
The method of al-awla wa al-akhlaq teaches that once the existence of a Necessary Being is proven, any intermediaries in the causal chain should be discarded as unsupported by evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You know that only God is necessary and all else is dependent. Yet, in your daily life, do you anchor your security in created things—like health, wealth, or people—as if they were necessary? What would it mean to 'drop those intermediaries' from your heart and depend on God alone?
?
The method of الأولى والأخلق demands that you only accept what is proven. Are there beliefs or practices you hold that you have never tested against clear evidence from revelation or reason? What would you do if you found them unsupported?
?
If the causal chain ends with God alone, why do you still fear created causes more than you fear the Creator? What intermediary—whether a person, position, or plan—must you release today to fully trust in God?
